The Three Types of Signification: Aqliyya, Wad'iyya, Tabi'iyya
Author classifies signification (dalala) into three types based on the relation between signifier and signified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of signification (ʿaqliyya, waḍʿiyya, ṭabīʿiyya) based on the nature of the relation between signifier and signified, and identify examples of each from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Signification is not limited to words; it includes mental, conventional, and natural relations.

In logic, understanding how meaning is conveyed is fundamental. The author introduces three types of signification (dalāla) based on the kind of relation between the signifier and the signified. This classification is essential for analyzing how terms and statements convey meaning in Islamic sciences such as uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and contrast these three types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
The passage lists 'aqliyya (mental), wad'iyya (conventional), and tabi'iyya (natural). Note the Arabic terms and their transliterations.
These are the core categories of signification discussed in logic.
2
Extract the basis of each type
For each type, identify the specific relation that defines it: mental = essential relation (ʿalāqa dhātiyya), conventional = positing by a positor (jaʿl al-jāʿil), natural = production by nature (iḥdāth al-ṭabīʿa).
These differentiae distinguish the types from one another.
3
Understand the verbal/non-verbal dimension
The passage states that each type can be verbal (lafẓiyya) or non-verbal (ghayr lafẓiyya). This means the sign can be a word or another kind of indicator, such as smoke or a groan.
This shows that signification is broader than just language.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Think of examples from Islamic sciences: smoke indicating fire is mental (cause-effect), the word 'salāh' indicating prayer is conventional, and a groan indicating pain is natural.
These examples reinforce the distinctions and connect to real scholarly contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْإِفَادَةُ إِنَّمَا تَتِمُّ بِالدَّلَالَةِ، مِنْهَا عَقْلِيَّةٌ بِعَلَاقَةٍ ذَاتِيَّةٍ، وَمِنْهَا وَضْعِيَّةٌ بِجَعْلِ الْجَاعِلِ، وَمِنْهَا طَبَعِيَّةٌ بِإِحْدَاثِ الطَّبِيعَةِ، وَكُلٌّ مِنْهَا لَفْظِيَّةٌ وَغَيْرُ لَفْظِيَّةٍ.
Benefit is only completed by signification. Among it is mental (ʿaqliyya) with an essential relation, and among it is conventional (waḍʿiyya) by the positing of a positor, and among it is natural (ṭabīʿiyya) by the production of nature. And each of them is verbal and non-verbal.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of signification (dalāla). The author states that communication or benefit (ifāda) is achieved through signification, which is of three types: 1) mental (ʿaqliyya) – a relation that is essential, like the relation between a cause and its effect; 2) conventional (waḍʿiyya) – established by a positor (jāʿil), such as the meaning of a word by human agreement; 3) natural (ṭabīʿiyya) – produced by nature, such as smoke signifying fire. Each of these can be verbal (using words) or non-verbal (using other signs). This classification is important for understanding how different types of signs convey meaning, and it forms the basis for discussing the signification of words in logic.

Key Terms
الدَّلَالَةُ
عَقْلِيَّةٌ
وَضْعِيَّةٌ
طَبَعِيَّةٌ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of signification is based on an essential relation (ʿalāqa dhātiyya)?
A) Waḍʿiyya (conventional)
B) Ṭabīʿiyya (natural)
C) ʿAqliyya (mental)
D) Lafẓiyya (verbal)

Key Takeaway
Signification is classified into three types—mental, conventional, and natural—each distinguished by the nature of the relation between signifier and signified, and all can be verbal or non-verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify one example of each type of signification from the field of kalām or fiqh. For instance, how does the concept of dalīl (evidence) function in theological proofs? Explain which type of signification is at play.
?
Given a statement from the Qur'an, analyze whether its signification is conventional (based on linguistic convention) or also involves mental signification (e.g., an obvious causal relation).

The Three Types of Verbal Signification
Author defines the three types of verbal signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate between mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam in Islamic textual contexts.

Core Idea
Words signify meaning in three ways: fully, partially, or by external relation.

In the Islamic logical tradition, understanding how words convey meaning is foundational for interpreting revelation and legal texts. This lesson unpacks the three modes of verbal signification (dalalat al-lafz) as defined by classical logicians: correspondence, inclusion, and implication. Each mode plays a distinct role in how scholars derive rulings and arguments from scripture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word's immediate meaning
Start with the word's complete meaning as it was coined (mutabaqa). For example, 'salah' signifies the entire prayer ritual.
This is the default signification.
2
Check for a part of the meaning
Determine if the word implies a component of its definition (tadammun). For 'salah', this includes ruku' or sujud.
Tadammun is only possible if the word has parts.
3
Check for external implication
See if the word implies something outside its meaning but related intellectually or customarily (iltizam). For 'salah', this could imply purity (wudu) by customary association.
Iltizam requires a valid relation (alaqa).
4
Verify the relation for iltizam
Identify whether the relation is intellectual (e.g., cause-effect) or customary (e.g., roof implies wall).
Without a relation, iltizam is invalid.
5
Apply the dependency rule
Remember that tadammun and iltizam both presuppose mutabaqa, but mutabaqa does not imply them. Also, tadammun and iltizam are independent of each other.
This rule is stressed in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَدَلَالَهُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى تَمَامِ مَا وُضِعَ لَهُ مِنْ تِلْكَ الْحَيْثِيَّةِ مُطَابَقَةٌ، وَعَلَى جُزْئِهِ تَضَمُّنٌ، وَهُوَ لَازِمٌ لَهَا فِي الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ، وَعَلَى الْخَارِجِ الْتِزَامٌ، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ عَلَاقَةٍ عَقْلِيَّةٍ أَوْ عُرْفِيَّةٍ. وَقَدْ قِيلَ: الْالْتِزَامُ مَهْجُورٌ فِي الْعُلُومِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ عَقْلِيٌّ، وَنُقِضَ بِالتَّضَمُّنِ، وَيَلْزَمُهُمَا الْمُطَابَقَةُ، وَلَا عَكْسَ، وَكَوْنُهُ لَيْسَ غَيْرَهُ لَيْسَ مِمَّا يَسْبِقُ إِلَيْهِ الذَّهْنُ دَائِمًا. وَأَمَّا التَّضَمُّنِيَّةُ وَالْالْتِزَامِيَّةُ فَلَا لُزُومَ بَيْنَهُمَا.
So the signification of a word upon the entirety of what it was posited for from that aspect is correspondence (muṭābaqa), and upon its part is inclusion (taḍammun), and it is necessary for it in compounds, and upon what is external is implication (iltizām), and there must be an intellectual or customary relation. And it has been said: implication is abandoned in the sciences because it is intellectual, and it was refuted by inclusion, and both require correspondence, and not its converse, and its being not other than it is not something that the mind always precedes to. As for the inclusional and implicational [significations], there is no necessary relation between them.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage details the three types of verbal signification (dalālāt al-lafẓ). First, muṭābaqa (correspondence) is when the word signifies the entirety of what it was coined for, e.g., the word 'human' signifies the complete meaning of human. Second, taḍammun (inclusion) is when the word signifies a part of that meaning, e.g., 'human' implies 'animal' (a part of its definition). Third, iltizām (implication) is when the word signifies something external to the meaning but related by an intellectual or customary relation, e.g., 'roof' implies 'wall' (by customary association). The author notes that iltizām requires a relation (ʿalāqa) that is either intellectual or customary. A controversy is mentioned: some say that iltizām is abandoned in the sciences because it is purely intellectual and not reliable. But the objection (naqḍ) is that taḍammun also involves an intellectual relation, so the same criticism would apply. Both muṭābaqa and taḍammun/iltizām require muṭābaqa as a basis, but the converse is not true (a word can have muṭābaqa without the others). The passage ends by stating that there is no necessary connection between taḍammun and iltizām—they are independent modes of signification.

Key Terms
مُطَابَقَة
تَضَمُّن
الْتِزَام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of verbal signification does the word 'human' have when it implies 'animal'?
A) Muṭābaqa
B) Taḍammun
C) Iltizām
D) None

Key Takeaway
The three types of verbal signification — mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam — together form the foundation for how words convey meaning, with mutabaqa as the basis for the others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For the word 'hajj', identify which signification is used when it implies (a) the entire pilgrimage, (b) standing at Arafat, and (c) financial ability (istita'a). Provide a brief justification for each.
?
Explain why iltizam requires a relation (alaqa) and give an Islamic example where the relation is customary (e.g., 'roof' implies 'wall' in building, or 'salah' implies 'wudu' in Islamic practice).

Simple and Compound Words and the Logical Definition of Verb
Author defines simple and compound words and clarifies the logical definition of a verb (kalima).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mufrad and murakkab words according to logicians and identify the logical definition of a verb (kalima), comparing it to the grammatical definition.

Core Idea
The logical definition of a verb requires a specific tense and truth-aptness, unlike the grammatical verb.

This passage introduces two fundamental distinctions in logic: simple vs compound words, and the technical definition of a 'kalima' (verb). Logicians classify words based on whether their parts signify parts of the meaning. They then define a kalima more narrowly than grammarians do, requiring that it indicate time and admit truth and falsehood. Understanding these distinctions is essential for later topics in propositions and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criteria for compound vs simple words
Look at the Arabic: 'if its part indicates a part of its meaning, then it is compound; otherwise it is simple.' Note that this is about the word's own parts, not external composition.
2
Recognize the logical definition of a kalima
The passage states: 'if it indicates time by its form, then it is a kalima.' Also, it must be capable of truth and falsehood (as seen in the example of 'yamshī' vs 'amshī').
3
Compare logical and grammatical definitions
Grammarians consider any verb (fi'l) as a kalima, but logicians exclude verbs without a specific tense (e.g., 'amshī' – I walk) because they have no fixed time reference.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The author addresses the objection that grammarians' statements like 'min is a particle' are judgments on sounds, not meanings, so they don't conflict with logical categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْإِفْرَادُ وَالتَّرْكِيبُ حَقِيقَةٌ صِفَةُ اللَّفْظِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِنْ دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزْءٍ مَعْنَاهُ فَمُرَكَّبٌ، وَيُسَمَّى قَوْلًا وَمُؤَلَّفًا، وَإِلَّا فَمُفْرَدٌ. وَهُوَ إِنْ كَانَ مِرْآةً لِتَعَرُّفِ حَالِ الْغَيْرِ فَقَطْ فَأَدَاةٌ، وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْكَلِمَاتِ الْوُجُودِيَّةَ مِنْهَا، فَإِنَّ «كَانَ» مَثَلًا مَعْنَاهُ كَوْنُ الشَّيْءِ شَيْئًا لَمْ يُذْكَرْ بَعْدُ، وَتَسْمِيَتُهَا كَلِمَاتٍ لِتَصَرُّفِهَا وَدَلَالَتِهَا عَلَى الزَّمَانِ، وَإِلَّا فَإِنْ دَلَّ بِهَيْئَتِهِ عَلَى زَمَانٍ فَكَلِمَةٌ. وَلَيْسَ كُلُّ فِعْلٍ عِنْدَ الْعَرَبِ كَلِمَةً عِنْدَ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ، فَإِنَّ نَحْوَ «أَمْشِي» وَ «تَمْشِي» مَثَلًا فِعْلٌ عِنْدَهُمْ، وَلَيْسَ بِكَلِمَةٍ عِنْدَ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ لِاحْتِمَالِهِ الصِّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ، بِخِلَافِ «يَمْشِي»، وَإِلَّا فَهُوَ اسْمٌ. وَمِنْ خَوَاصِّهِ الْحُكْمُ عَلَيْهِ. وَقَوْلُهُمْ «مِنْ حَرْفُ جَرٍّ وَضَرَبَ فِعْلٌ مَاضٍ» لَا يَرِدُ؛ فَإِنَّهُ حُكْمٌ عَلَى نَفْسِ الصَّوْتِ، لَا عَلَى مَعْنَاهُ، وَالْمُخْتَصُّ بِهِ هُوَ هَذَا، وَالْأَوَّلُ يَجْرِي فِي الْمُهْمَلَاتِ أَيْضًا.
Simplicity and composition are truly attributes of the word; because if its part indicates a part of its meaning, then it is compound, and it is called a statement (qawl) and an composed (mu'allaf), otherwise it is simple. And if it is merely a mirror for knowing the state of something else, then it is a tool. And the truth is that existential words (kalimāt wujūdiyya) are among them, for 'kāna' for example, its meaning is the being of a thing as something not yet mentioned. And they are called kalimāt due to their conjugation and their indication of time. Otherwise, if it indicates time by its form, then it is a kalima. And not every verb (fi'l) according to the Arabs is a kalima according to the logicians; for example 'amshī' and 'tamshī' are verbs according to them, but not kalima according to the logicians because they admit truth and falsehood, unlike 'yamshī'. Otherwise, it is a noun. And among its properties is that judgment is made upon it. And their statement 'min is a particle of jar, and daraba is a past verb' is not problematic; for it is a judgment on the sound itself, not on its meaning, and what is specific to it is this, and the first also applies to meaningless words.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines the division of words into mufrad (simple) and murakkab (compound) according to logicians. A word is compound if its parts signify parts of its meaning; otherwise it is simple. A compound word is called qawl (statement) or mu'allaf (composed). Then the author discusses tools (adāt) – words that indicate the state of something else, like particles. He then clarifies the logical definition of a verb (kalima). Logicians define kalima as a word that signifies a meaning and indicates time through its form, and they restrict it to words that can be true or false (like 'yamshī' – he walks). In contrast, Arabic grammarians consider all verbs (fi'l) as kalimat, but logicians exclude imperfect verbs without tense (like 'amshī' – I walk) because they can be true or false in different contexts. The author addresses an objection: grammarians say 'min is a particle and daraba is a past verb' – this is a judgment on the word itself (as a sound) not on its meaning, so it does not conflict with the logical definition. This shows that the categories of grammar and logic differ: logic focuses on meaning and truth-aptness.

Key Terms
مُفْرَد
مُرَكَّب
كَلِمَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the logician exclude 'amshī' (I walk) from being a kalima?
A) It does not indicate time through its form.
B) It is a noun.
C) It is a compound word.
D) It is a particle.

Key Takeaway
In logic, a verb (kalima) is a simple word that indicates time by its form and can be true or false, which is more restrictive than the grammatical definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the criteria from this lesson, classify the following Arabic words as mufrad or murakkab, and if mufrad, determine whether it is a kalima (logical verb) or not: 'ضَارِب' (hitting), 'يَضْرِب' (he hits), 'عَبْدُ اللَّهِ' (servant of Allah). Explain your reasoning.
?
In a Usul al-Fiqh debate, a scholar argues that the word 'أَمَرَ' (he commanded) is a kalima because it is a verb in grammar. How would you respond using the logical definition from this lesson?

The Nature of Synonyms (Murādafah)
Author defines synonymy and its limitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether two words are full synonyms and explain why they may not be interchangeable in all contexts.

Core Idea
Synonyms share meaning but not usage; linguistic accidents block full substitution.

In Arabic, synonymy (murādafah) enriches expression, but not all synonyms are freely interchangeable. The author gives the example of 'ṣallā ʻalayhi' and 'daʻā ʻalayhi': both involve calling upon God, but one is used for blessing and the other for curse. Understanding this nuance prevents misuse in speech and argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about synonymy
The author affirms that synonymy occurs due to multiplicity of means and rhetorical needs such as rhyming prose.
2
Note the condition of interchangeability
The author states that it is not necessary for each synonym to take the place of the other even if from one language, because correct analogy (ṣiḥḥat al-ḍamm) is an accidental property.
3
Examine the example
The pair 'ṣallā ʻalayhi' and 'daʻā ʻalayhi' are not interchangeable: one is used for blessing, the other for curse.
4
Consider the broader question
The author asks whether synonymy can exist between a single word and a compound, noting that it is disputed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَتَكْثُرُ اللَّفْظُ مَعَ اتِّحَادِ الْمَعْنَى مُرَادَفَةٌ، وَذَلِكَ وَاقِعٌ؛ لِتَكَثُّرِ الْوَسَائِلِ، وَالتَّوَسُّعِ فِي مَحَالِّ الْبَدَائِعِ كَالسَّجْعِ، وَلَا يَجِبُ فِيهِ قِيَامُ كُلٍّ مَقَامَ الْآخَرِ، وَإِنْ كَانَا مِنْ لُغَةٍ؛ فَإِنَّ صِحَّةَ الضَّمِّ مِنَ الْعَوَارِضِ، يُقَالُ «صَلَّى عَلَيْهِ» وَلَا يُقَالُ «دَعَا عَلَيْهِ». هَلْ بَيْنَ الْمُفْرَدِ وَالْمُرَكَّبِ تَرَادُفٌ؟ اخْتُلِفَ فِيهِ. وَالْمُرَكَّبُ إِنْ صَدَقَ السُّكُوتُ عَلَيْهِ فَتَامٌ، خَبَرٌ وَقَضِيَّةٌ إِنْ قُصِدَ بِهِ الْحِكَايَةُ عَنِ الْوَاقِعِ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ يُوصَفُ بِالصِّدْقِ وَالْكَذِبِ ضَرُورَةً؛ فَقَوْلُ الْقَائِلِ: «كَلَامِي هَذَا كَاذِبٌ» لَيْسَ بِخَبَرٍ؛ لِأَنَّ الْحِكَايَةَ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ غَيْرُ مَعْقُولَةٍ. وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّهُ بِجَمِيعِ أَجْزَائِهِ مَأْخُوذٌ فِي جَانِبِ الْمَوْضُوعِ، فَالنِّسْبَةُ مَلْحُوظَةٌ إِجْمَالًا، فَهِيَ الْمَحْكِيُّ عَنْهَا، وَمِنْ حَيْثُ تَعَلُّقُ الْإِيقَاعِ بِهَا مَلْحُوظَةٌ تَفْصِيلًا، فَهِيَ الْحِكَايَةُ، فَانْحَلَّ الْإِشْكَالُ بِجَمِيعِ تَقَارِيرِهِ. وَنَظِيرُ ذَلِكَ قَوْلُنَا: «كُلُّ حَمْدٍ لِلَّهِ»؛ فَإِنَّهُ حَمْدٌ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ كُلِّ حَمْدٍ، فَالْحِكَايَةُ نَفْسُهَا مَحْكِيٌّ عَنْهَا، فَتَأَمَّلْ؛ فَإِنَّهُ جَذْرٌ أَصَمُّ.
And the multiplicity of words with unity of meaning is synonymy (murādafah), and that occurs, due to multiplicity of means and expansiveness in the contexts of rhetorical beautifications like rhyming prose; and it is not necessary in it that each takes the place of the other, even if they are from one language; for correct analogy is among the accidents: one says 'he prayed for him' (ṣallā ʻalayhi) and does not say 'he called for him' (daʻā ʻalayhi). Is there synonymy between the simple (mufrad) and the compound (murakkab)? It is disputed. As for the compound, if silence upon it is valid, it is complete (tām), a statement (khabar) and a proposition (qaḍiyya) if intended to narrate about reality; and therefore it is necessarily described as true or false. So the statement of the speaker: 'This speech of mine is false' is not a statement (khabar), because narrating about itself is not intelligible. And the truth is that it, with all its parts, is taken in the subject side; so the relation is observed summarily, and it is the thing narrated about (al-maḥkī ʻanhā); and from the aspect of the connection of predication to it, it is observed in detail, and it is the narration (al-ḥikāya). So the problem is resolved in all its forms. An analog of that is our saying: 'Every praise is for God'; for it is a praise among all praises, so the narration itself is narrated about. So reflect; for it is a solid root.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage from Bahr Ulum covers two topics: synonymy (الترادف) and the liar paradox. The author first states that synonymy — multiple words for the same meaning — does occur, due to the abundance of linguistic means and rhetorical needs like saj‘ (rhymed prose). However, synonyms are not always perfectly interchangeable; e.g., 'ṣallā ʻalayhi' (he prayed for him) is acceptable but 'daʻā ʻalayhi' (he called for him) is not, because the latter implies supplication against. Then the question: can a single word and a compound expression be synonymous? The author notes disagreement. The focus then shifts to compound expressions. A compound is complete (تام) if one can validly pause (i.e., it is a complete utterance). If intended to narrate reality (يقصد به الحكاية عن الواقع), it is a statement (خبر) and proposition (قضية), capable of truth and falsity. This leads to the liar paradox: the statement 'This speech of mine is false' cannot be a genuine statement because narrating about itself is unintelligible. The solution: the entire utterance is part of the subject; the relation (النسبة) is considered globally as the thing narrated about, and in detail as the narration itself. Thus the problem dissolves. The author gives a parallel: 'Every praise is for God' — this statement is itself a praise, so the narration (حكاية) becomes what is narrated about (محكي عنها). This is a deep point (جذر أصم).

Key Terms
مُرَادَفَة
عَوَارِض

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because they have different grammatical forms.
D) Because one is a compound and the other is simple.

Key Takeaway
Synonymy exists due to linguistic abundance, but synonyms are not always substitutable because of accidental usage norms.

The Liar Paradox and the Nature of Propositions
Author resolves the liar paradox by disqualifying self-referential statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions for a genuine statement (khabar) and explain why the liar paradox is resolved by denying self-narration.

Core Idea
A self-referential sentence fails to be a khabar because a statement cannot narrate about itself.

The liar paradox—'This speech of mine is false'—challenges the very notion of truth and falsity. The author argues that such a sentence is not a genuine statement (khabar) because a narration (ḥikāya) must be about something other than itself. He then resolves the paradox by showing that in such a sentence, the subject includes the entire utterance, and the relation is both the narrated content and the narration in different respects.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of a complete compound (tāmm)
A compound upon which silence is valid becomes a statement (khabar) if intended to narrate about reality, and thus can be true or false.
2
Identify the liar sentence
'This speech of mine is false' (kalāmī hādhā kādhib).
3
Check if it meets the condition of a khabar
It claims to narrate about itself; but the author says narrating about itself is not intelligible (ghayr maʻqūla). Therefore it is not a khabar.
4
Understand the author's resolution
The entire utterance is taken in the subject side; the relation (nisba) is considered summarily as the thing narrated about (al-maḥkī ʻanhā), and in detail as the narration (al-ḥikāya). Thus the paradox dissolves.
5
Apply to the parallel example
'Every praise is for God' is itself a praise, so the narration is about itself, but this is acceptable because it is not a self-referential denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَتَكْثُرُ اللَّفْظُ مَعَ اتِّحَادِ الْمَعْنَى مُرَادَفَةٌ، وَذَلِكَ وَاقِعٌ؛ لِتَكَثُّرِ الْوَسَائِلِ، وَالتَّوَسُّعِ فِي مَحَالِّ الْبَدَائِعِ كَالسَّجْعِ، وَلَا يَجِبُ فِيهِ قِيَامُ كُلٍّ مَقَامَ الْآخَرِ، وَإِنْ كَانَا مِنْ لُغَةٍ؛ فَإِنَّ صِحَّةَ الضَّمِّ مِنَ الْعَوَارِضِ، يُقَالُ «صَلَّى عَلَيْهِ» وَلَا يُقَالُ «دَعَا عَلَيْهِ». هَلْ بَيْنَ الْمُفْرَدِ وَالْمُرَكَّبِ تَرَادُفٌ؟ اخْتُلِفَ فِيهِ. وَالْمُرَكَّبُ إِنْ صَدَقَ السُّكُوتُ عَلَيْهِ فَتَامٌ، خَبَرٌ وَقَضِيَّةٌ إِنْ قُصِدَ بِهِ الْحِكَايَةُ عَنِ الْوَاقِعِ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ يُوصَفُ بِالصِّدْقِ وَالْكَذِبِ ضَرُورَةً؛ فَقَوْلُ الْقَائِلِ: «كَلَامِي هَذَا كَاذِبٌ» لَيْسَ بِخَبَرٍ؛ لِأَنَّ الْحِكَايَةَ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ غَيْرُ مَعْقُولَةٍ. وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّهُ بِجَمِيعِ أَجْزَائِهِ مَأْخُوذٌ فِي جَانِبِ الْمَوْضُوعِ، فَالنِّسْبَةُ مَلْحُوظَةٌ إِجْمَالًا، فَهِيَ الْمَحْكِيُّ عَنْهَا، وَمِنْ حَيْثُ تَعَلُّقُ الْإِيقَاعِ بِهَا مَلْحُوظَةٌ تَفْصِيلًا، فَهِيَ الْحِكَايَةُ، فَانْحَلَّ الْإِشْكَالُ بِجَمِيعِ تَقَارِيرِهِ. وَنَظِيرُ ذَلِكَ قَوْلُنَا: «كُلُّ حَمْدٍ لِلَّهِ»؛ فَإِنَّهُ حَمْدٌ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ كُلِّ حَمْدٍ، فَالْحِكَايَةُ نَفْسُهَا مَحْكِيٌّ عَنْهَا، فَتَأَمَّلْ؛ فَإِنَّهُ جَذْرٌ أَصَمُّ.
And the multiplicity of words with unity of meaning is synonymy (murādafah), and that occurs, due to multiplicity of means and expansiveness in the contexts of rhetorical beautifications like rhyming prose; and it is not necessary in it that each takes the place of the other, even if they are from one language; for correct analogy is among the accidents: one says 'he prayed for him' (ṣallā ʻalayhi) and does not say 'he called for him' (daʻā ʻalayhi). Is there synonymy between the simple (mufrad) and the compound (murakkab)? It is disputed. As for the compound, if silence upon it is valid, it is complete (tām), a statement (khabar) and a proposition (qaḍiyya) if intended to narrate about reality; and therefore it is necessarily described as true or false. So the statement of the speaker: 'This speech of mine is false' is not a statement (khabar), because narrating about itself is not intelligible. And the truth is that it, with all its parts, is taken in the subject side; so the relation is observed summarily, and it is the thing narrated about (al-maḥkī ʻanhā); and from the aspect of the connection of predication to it, it is observed in detail, and it is the narration (al-ḥikāya). So the problem is resolved in all its forms. An analog of that is our saying: 'Every praise is for God'; for it is a praise among all praises, so the narration itself is narrated about. So reflect; for it is a solid root.

Compound Expressions: Complete vs Incomplete, Khabar vs Insha'
Author defines compound classifications and the khabar/insha' distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Arabic expression as complete or incomplete, and if complete, as khabar or insha'.

Core Idea
A complete compound is truth-evaluable only if it narrates reality.

This passage establishes the foundational classification of expressions into compounds. Understanding whether a sentence is complete (one can stop on it) and whether it is intended to narrate (khabar) or to perform an action (insha') is crucial for evaluating truth and for further logical operations. This classification is the first step in identifying propositions (qadaya) that can serve as premises in arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify if the expression is compound
Determine if the expression consists of more than one word. If so, it is a murakkab.
2
Test completeness by stopping
Check if you can pause after the expression without needing more words. If yes, it is tamm; if no, it is naqis.
For example, 'Zayd's boy' needs more context, so it is incomplete.
3
Determine the intention of a complete expression
If the complete expression aims to narrate (hikaya) about reality, it is khabar and can be true/false; otherwise, it is insha' (command, prohibition, wish, etc.).
Insha' is not truth-evaluable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمُرَكَّبُ إِنْ صَحَ السُّكُوتُ عَلَيْهِ فَتَامٌ ، خَبَرٌ وَقَضِيَّةٌ إِنْ قُصِدَ بِهِ الْحِكَايَةُ عَنِ الْوَاقِعِ ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ يُوصَفُ بِالصِّدْقِ وَالْكِذْبِ؛ ضَرُورَةٌ ، فَقَوْلُ الْقَائِلِ: كَلَامِي هَذَا كَاذِبٌ لَيْسَ بِخَبَرٍ ؛ لِأَنَّ الْحِكَايَةَ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ غَيْرُ مَعْقُولَةٍ. وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّهُ بِجَمِيعِ أَجْزَائِهِ مَأْخُوذُ فِي جَانِبِ الْمَوْضُوعِ ، فَالنِّسْبَةُ مَلْحُوظَةٌ مُجْمَلًا، فَهِيَ الْمَحْكِيُّ عَنْهَا ، وَمِنْ حَيْثُ تَعَلَّقُ الإيقاع بِهَا مَلْحُوظَةٌ تَفْصِيلًا، فَهِيَ الْحِكَايَةُ، فَانْحَلَّ الْإِشْكَالُ بِجَمِيعِ تَقَارِيرِهِ. وَنَظِيرُ ذَلِكَ قَوْلُنَا: «كُلُّ حَمْدٍ لِلَّهِ» ؛ فَإِنَّهُ حَمْدٌ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ كُلِّ حَمْدٍ، فَالْحِكَايَةُ نَفْسُهَا مَحْكِيٌّ عَنْهَا ، فَتَأَمَّلْ ؛ فَإِنَّهُ جَذْرٌ أَصَمُّ. وَإِلَّا فَإِنْشَاءُ، مِنْهُ: أَمْرٌ وَنَهْي وَتَمَنْ وَتَرَجٌ وَاسْتِفْهَامٌ ، وَغَيْرُ ذَلِكَ . وَإِنْ لَمْ يَصِحَ فَنَاقِصٌ ، مِنْهُ تَفْسِيدِي ، كَغُلَامِ زَيْدٍ ، وَامْتِرَاجِيٌّ ، وَغَيْرُهُ.
And the compound: if silence upon it is permissible, then it is complete; it is a report (khabar) and proposition (qadiyya) if by it the narration of reality is intended, and therefore it is described as true or false by necessity. So the speaker's statement: 'This speech of mine is false' is not a report, because narrating about itself is inconceivable. The truth is that it, with all its parts, is taken in the side of the subject; so the relation is observed as a whole, and that is what is narrated about; and in terms of the connection attaching to it, it is observed in detail, and that is the narration. So the difficulty is resolved in all its formulations. And analogous to that is our saying: 'Every praise belongs to Allah'; for it is a praise from the totality of all praise, so the narration itself is narrated about. So reflect; for it is a firm root. Otherwise it is a construction (insha'), including: command, prohibition, wish, hope, interrogation, and others. And if [silence] is not permissible, then it is incomplete, including: relational (idafi) like 'Zayd's boy', and mixed (imtizaji), and others.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of compounds into complete (tamm) and incomplete (naqis). The author begins by defining the complete compound: it is one upon which one can silently stop. If it is intended to narrate (hikaya) about reality, then it is called a report (khabar) or proposition (qadiyya), and it can be described as true or false. Then the author addresses the famous liar paradox: 'My speech is false.' He argues that this is not a report because reporting about oneself is impossible. The correct analysis is that the entire sentence is taken as the subject, and the relation (nisba) is considered in two ways: as an aggregate (mujmal) as that which is narrated about, and in detail (tafsil) as the act of narration itself. This resolves the paradox. He provides an analogy: 'Every praise belongs to Allah' — this praise itself is a praise within the totality of all praise, so the narration is itself narrated about. Then he states that if the compound is not intended for narration, it is a construction (insha'), including command, prohibition, wish, hope, interrogation. Finally, if the compound is not complete (i.e., one cannot stop upon it), it is incomplete (naqis), of which there are two types: relational (idafi) like 'Zayd's boy', and mixed (imtizaji). The commentary carefully distinguishes between declarative and non-declarative sentences and shows how the liar paradox is dissolved by recognizing the self-referential structure.

Key Terms
الْمُرَكَّبُ
الْخَبَر
الْإِنْشَاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of an insha' (construction) according to the passage?
A) 'The sky is blue'
B) 'Do not steal'
C) 'Zayd is standing'
D) 'This is a pen'

Key Takeaway
The classification of compounds into complete (truth-evaluable khabar or non-truth-evaluable insha') and incomplete is the foundation for identifying propositions in logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Solving the Liar Paradox: The Two Aspects of Nisba
Author resolves the liar paradox using the two aspects of nisba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the liar sentence is not a khabar and how analyzing nisba as mujmal and tafsili resolves the paradox.

Core Idea
The liar sentence fails because it confuses the relation-as-narrated with the relation-as-narrating.

The liar paradox—'This statement is false'—has puzzled thinkers for centuries. The author provides a uniquely insightful solution by distinguishing two ways of considering the relational content (nisba) in a proposition. Understanding this distinction not only resolves the paradox but also deepens our grasp of how propositions function in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the liar sentence
The example is: 'My speech is false' (قول القائل: كلامي هذا كاذب). Recognize that it is a self-referential statement.
2
Apply the definition of khabar
A khabar must narrate about reality. The liar sentence attempts to narrate about itself, which is inconceivable (غير معقولة). Therefore, it is not a khabar.
3
Understand the two considerations of nisba
The entire sentence is taken as the subject (mawdu'), and the nisba (relation) is considered in two ways: as an aggregate (mujmal) that is narrated about, and in detail (tafsil) as the act of narrating. This shows how the sentence can be analyzed without paradox.
The relation is both the object of narration and the narration itself.
4
Analyze the analogy
The author provides a parallel: 'Every praise belongs to Allah' – this statement is a praise that is itself part of all praise, so the narration is self-referential but coherent. Reflect on how this example illustrates the same structure.
The analogy helps to see that self-reference is not inherently contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then the author addresses the famous liar paradox: 'My speech is false.' He argues that this is not a report because reporting about oneself is impossible. The correct analysis is that the entire sentence is taken as the subject, and the relation (nisba) is considered in two ways: as an aggregate (mujmal) as that which is narrated about, and in detail (tafsil) as the act of narration itself. This resolves the paradox. He provides an analogy: 'Every praise belongs to Allah' — this praise itself is a praise within the totality of all praise, so the narration is itself narrated about. Then he states that if the compound is not intended for narration, it is a construction (insha'), including command, prohibition, wish, hope, interrogation. Finally, if the compound is not complete (i.e., one cannot stop upon it), it is incomplete (naqis), of which there are two types: relational (idafi) like 'Zayd's boy', and mixed (imtizaji). The commentary carefully distinguishes between declarative and non-declarative sentences and shows how the liar paradox is dissolved by recognizing the self-referential structure.

Key Terms
النِّسْبَة
الْحِكَايَة
الْمَوْضُوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the liar sentence not a khabar?
A) It is incomplete (naqis).
B) It is an insha' (construction).
C) Narrating about itself is inconceivable.
D) It contains a contradiction.

Key Takeaway
The liar paradox is resolved by recognizing that the nisba (relation) can be considered both as the object of narration and as the act of narration, thereby avoiding self-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the sentence 'All knowledge belongs to Allah' (كلّ علم لله) using the two aspects of nisba: explain how the nisba is considered mujmal and tafsili, and why it is a valid khabar.
?
Consider the sentence 'This sentence is false'. Using the author's reasoning, explain why it is not a valid khabar.

Total vs. Detailed Judgment and the Nature of the Relative
Author defines judgment as total or detailed and explains the role of the relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish total judgment (ijmali) from detailed judgment (tafsili) and explain why the relative (nisba) is not an independent part of judgment.

Core Idea
Judgment can be a single disclosure or a detailed process; the relative is a mirror, not a separate object.

The author opens 'The Assents' by distinguishing two types of judgment: total and detailed. This distinction reveals how the mind grasps relations. He then clarifies that the relative (nisba) between subject and predicate is a particle meaning that only serves as a mirror for the two extremes—it is not independently observed. The true object of judgment is the composite form, such as union.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of judgment
Find where the author mentions 'total' (ijmali) and 'detailed' (tafsili). Note the definition given for each.
Total judgment is a single disclosure of union; detailed judgment requires multiple distinct forms.
2
Locate the description of the relative
Read the lines about the relative (nisba). The author says it is a particle meaning, not independently observed, but a mirror for the state of the two extremes.
This explains why the relative is not an independent part of the judgment.
3
Recognize the true object of judgment
The author states that judgment truly pertains to the meaning of the composite form (hay'a tarkibiyya), e.g., union. Understand that this composite form is the actual content of the judgment.
The composite form unites subject and predicate in the mind.
4
Apply to an example
Take the proposition 'The world is created.' Identify the subject (world), predicate (created), and the relative (is). Is the judgment total or detailed? The author would likely say it is detailed because it involves multiple mental forms.
In Islamic theology, this proposition is a detailed judgment requiring analysis of evidence.

Source Text

التصديقات
الْحُكْمُ مِنْهُ إِجْمَالِيُّ ، وَهُوَ الْكِشَافُ الإِنْحَادِ بَيْنَ الْأَمْرَيْنِ دَفْعَةٌ وَاحِدَةً، وَمِنْهُ تَفْصِيلِي، وَهُوَ الْمَنْطِقِيُّ الَّذِي يَسْتَدْعِي صُوَرًا مُتَعَدِّدَةً مُفَصَّلَةٌ ، وَالنِّسْبَةُ إِنَّمَا تَدْخُلُ فِي مُتَعَلَّقِ الْحُكْمِ بِالتَّبَعِيَّةِ ; لأَنَّهَا مِنَ الْمَعَانِي الْحَرْفِيَّةِ الَّتِي لَا تُلَاحَظُ بِالِاسْتِقْلَالِ ، إِنَّمَا هِيَ مِرْأَةٌ لِمُلَاحَظَةِ حَالِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، بَلْ إِنَّمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ الْحُكْمُ حَقِيقَةٌ بِمُفَادِ الْهَيْئَةِ التَّرْكِيبيَّةِ ، وَهُوَ الاِتِّحَادُ مَثَلًا، فَتَدَبَّر .
ثُمَّ الْقَضِيَّةُ إِنَّمَا تَتِمُّ بِأُمُورٍ ثَلَاثَةٍ ، ثَالِتُهَا نِسْبَةٌ تَامَّةٌ إِخْبَارِيَّةٌ حَاكِيَةٌ. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا يَسْتَبِينُ أَنَّ الظَّنَّ إِذْعَانُ بَسِيطٌ ، وَإِلَّا لَصَارَ أَجْزَاءُ الْقَضِيَّةِ هُنَاكَ أَرْبَعَةٌ.
The Assents. The judgment: from it is total, and it is the disclosure of the union between the two matters in one stroke; and from it is detailed, and it is the logical one that requires multiple detailed forms. And the relative only enters into the object of judgment as a dependent, because it is among the particle meanings that are not observed independently; rather it is a mirror for observing the state of the two extremes. Rather, judgment truly pertains to the meaning of the composite form, which is union, for example; so reflect. Then the proposition is only completed by three things, the third of which is a complete informative relative that reports. And from this it becomes clear that opinion is a simple assent; otherwise the parts of the proposition there would become four.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage, titled 'The Assents' (al-tasdiqat), begins by distinguishing two types of judgment (hukm): total (ijmali) and detailed (tafsili). Total judgment is a single disclosure of union between two things. Detailed judgment is the logical one that requires multiple detailed forms. The relative (nisba) between subject and predicate is not independently observed; it is a particle meaning (ma'na harfi) that serves as a mirror reflecting the state of the two extremes. In reality, judgment attaches to the composite form (hay'a tarkibiyya) — the unity between terms, like 'union' (ittihad). Then the author states that a proposition (qadiyya) is completed by three things: subject, predicate, and a complete informative relative that reports. From this, it becomes clear that opinion (zann) is a simple assent (idh'an basit); otherwise, the parts of the proposition would be four, which is not the case.

إجمالي
تفصيلي
النسبة
الهيئة التركيبية

Key Takeaway
Total judgment is a single act ; detailed judgment requires multiple forms, and the relative is only a dependent mirror, not an independent part.

The Three Components of a Proposition and the Simplicity of Opinion
Author states proposition components and deduces that opinion is simple assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three components of a proposition (qadiyya) and explain why opinion (zann) is a simple assent.

Core Idea
A proposition has three parts: subject, predicate, and a complete relative; opinion is simple, otherwise there would be four parts.

After discussing judgment, the author states that a proposition (qadiyya) is completed by exactly three things: subject, predicate, and a complete informative relative that reports. He then uses this structure to argue that opinion (zann) is a simple assent—if opinion had a separate additional component, then the proposition would have four parts, which it does not. This insight clarifies the nature of belief and propositional attitudes.

Guided Path
1
Find the statement about the three things
Locate the sentence: 'Then the proposition is only completed by three things.' Note what the third thing is: 'a complete informative relative that reports.'
The three parts are standard: subject (mawdu'), predicate (mahmul), and the copula/relative (nisba).
2
Understand the argument about opinion
Read the line: 'And from this it becomes clear that opinion is a simple assent; otherwise the parts of the proposition there would become four.' Why does the author conclude that opinion is simple?
If opinion were complex, it would add an extra part to the proposition, contradicting the three-part structure.
3
Connect to the earlier discussion
Recall that the relative is not independently observed. The three parts are subject, predicate, and the relative as a mirror. Opinion (zann) is a type of assent (idh'an) that accepts a proposition as true; it does not add a fourth part.
Simple assent means the proposition is accepted without additional mental acts.
4
Apply to an Islamic legal statement
Take 'Fasting in Ramadan is obligatory.' Identify the three parts. Then consider: if someone has a weak opinion that it is obligatory, does that add a fourth part? No, because opinion is just the mode of assent to the same three-part proposition.
This shows the universality of the three-part structure across different cognitive attitudes.

Doubt and the Restrictive Relative: A Critique
Author criticizes later scholars' view that doubt relates to restrictive relative, defends earlier view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the later scholars' claim that doubt attaches to the restrictive relative, and reconstruct the author's argument that hesitation must relate to occurrence.

Core Idea
Doubt is about the correspondence of the report to reality, not about the proposition's internal structure.

In this passage, the author critiques a later development in the theory of doubt (shakk). Later scholars claimed that doubt attaches to what they call the 'restrictive relative' (nisba taqyidiyya), but the author argues that hesitation cannot avoid relating to occurrence (wuqu'). He defends the earlier position and clarifies that the proposition remains intact in doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the later scholars' claim
Find where the author states that later scholars believe doubt is attached to the restrictive relative (النسبة التقييدية), which they call the relative between-between (النسبة بين بين).
This is in the first line: وَالْمُتَأَخِّرُونَ زَعَمُوا أَنَّ الشَّكَ مُتَعَلَّقٌ بِالنِّسْبَةِ التَّقَيُّدِيَّةِ
2
Understand the author's objection
The author questions: how can hesitation (التردد) be constituted without relating to occurrence? He asserts that the perceived reality (المدرك) in both doubt and assent is one; the difference is only in the mode of perception (إدراك إذعاني أو ترددي).
Key phrase: أَمَا فَهِمُوا أَنَّ التَّرَدُّدَ لَا يَتَقَوَّمُ حَقِيقَةٌ مَا لَمْ يَتَعَلَّقَ بِالْوُقُوعِ
3
Trace the author's resolution of a new doubt
The author raises a doubt: the three parts of a proposition are said to be realized in doubt, contradicting the famous view. He discusses a proposed solution (whole by accident) and rejects it, arguing that signification (الإفادة) precedes realization (الإيقاع).
The propositions 'Zayd is standing' remains a proposition in all cases; hesitation is about correspondence (المطابقة), not the report itself.
4
Analyze the role of the copula
The author concludes that a proposition has three parts (subject, predicate, relative) and the indicator of the relative is the copula (الرباطة). In Arabic, the copula may be omitted, relying on context.
Final lines: فَالدَّالُ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ يُسَمَّى رَابِطَةٌ... رُبَّمَا حُذِفَتِ الرَّابِطَةُ

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمُتَأَخِّرُونَ زَعَمُوا أَنَّ الشَّكَ مُتَعَلَّقٌ بِالنِّسْبَةِ التَّقَيُّدِيَّةِ ، وَهِيَ مَوْرِدُ الْحُكْمِ ، وَيُسَمُّونَهَا النَّسْبَةَ بَيْنَ بَيْنَ، وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ بِمَعْنَى الْوُقُوعِ وَاللَّاوُقُوعِ فَلَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ إِلَّا التَّصْدِيقُ أَعْجَبَنِي قَوْلُهُمْ، أَمَا فَهِمُوا أَنَّ التَّرَدُّدَ لَا يَتَقَوَّمُ حَقِيقَةٌ مَا لَمْ يَتَعَلَّقَ بِالْوُقُوعِ ؟ فَالْمُدْرَكُ فِي الصُّورَتَيْنِ وَاحِدٌ ، وَالتَّفَاوُتُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي الْإِدْرَاكِ بِأَنَّهُ إِذْعَانِيُّ أَوْ تَرَدُّدِيُّ ، فَقَوْلُ الْقُدَمَاءِ هُوَ الْحَقُّ .
وَهَهُنَا شَكٌ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ الْمَعْلُومَاتِ الثَّلاثَةَ الَّتِي هِيَ جَمِيعُ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ مُتَحَقِّقَةٌ فى صُورَةِ الشَّكُ، مَعَ أَنَّهَا غَيْرُ مُتَحَقِّقَةٍ عَلَى مَا هُوَ الْمَشْهُورُ .
قِيلَ فِي حَلِّهِ: إِنَّ الْقَضِيَّةَ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى تِلْكَ الْمَعْلُومَاتِ كُلٌّ بِالْعَرَضِ ، فَلاَ يَلْزَمُ تَحَقُّقُهُ ، كَالْكَاتِبِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْحَيَوَانِ النَّاطِقِ. أَقُولُ: إِذَا كَانَ كُلاً بِالْعَرَضِ فَيَجِبُ أَنْ يُعْتَبَرَ أَمْرُ آخَرُ بَعْدَ الْوُقُوعِ ، وَلَيْسَ إِلَّا إِدْرَاكُهُ، وَذَلِكَ خَارِجٌ إِجْمَاعًا. وَأَخُذُ الْوُقُوعِ بِشَرْطِ الْإِيقَاعِ تَصْحِيحٌ لِمَجْعُولِيَّةِ الذَّاتِي، وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ .
وَالْإِفَادَةُ مُقَدَّمٌ عَلَى الْإِيقَاعِ ، وَالْقَضِيَّةُ لَيْسَتْ مُنْتَظِرَةَ التَّحْصِيلِ بَعْدَهَا، فَاعْتِبَارُ تَعَلَّقِ الْإِيفَاعِ بِالْوُقُوعِ مِمَّا لَا دَخَلَ لَهُ في تَحْصِيلِ هَذِهِ الْحَقِيقَةِ، فَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ قَوْلَنَا زَيْدٌ هُوَ قَائِمٌ قَضِيَّةٌ عَلَى كُلِّ تَقْدِيرٍ؛ فَإِنَّهُ يُفِيدُ مَعْنَى مُحْتَمِلًا لِلصِّدْقِ وَالْكَذِبِ، فَفِي الشَّكْ إِنَّمَا التَّرَدُّدُ في مُطَابَقَةِ الْحِكَايَةِ، لَا فِي أَصْلِ الْحِكَايَةِ وَاحْتِمَالِهَا لَهُمَا .
نَعَمْ ، الْقَضَايَا الْمُعْتَبَرَةُ فِي الْعُلُومِ هِيَ الَّتِي تَعَلَّقَ بِهَا الْإِذْعَانُ ; إِذْ لَا كَمَالَ في تَحْصِيلِ الشَّكَ . هَذَا ، وَإِنْ كَانَ مِمَّا لَمْ يَقْرَعُ سَمْعَكَ ، لَكِنَّهُ هُوَ التَّحْقِيقُ . ثُمَّ إِذَا كَانَتِ الْأَجْزَاءُ ثَلَاثَةٌ فَحَقُّهَا أَنْ يُدَلَّ عَلَيْهَا بِثَلَاثِ عِبَارَاتٍ ، فَالدَّالُ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ يُسَمَّى رَابِطَةٌ، وَفي لُغَةِ الْعَرَبِ رُبَّمَا حُذِقَتِ الرَّابِطَةُ : اكْتِفَاء
And the later scholars claimed that doubt is attached to the restrictive relative, which is the subject of judgment, and they call it the relative between-between. As for judgment in the sense of occurrence and non-occurrence, only assent attaches to it. I admire their statement: Did they not understand that hesitation is not constituted as a reality unless it attaches to occurrence? So the perceived in both forms is one, and the difference is only in the perception; whether it is assenting or hesitant. So the statement of the earlier scholars is the truth.
And here is a doubt, which is that the three pieces of knowledge that are all the parts of the proposition are realized in the form of doubt, while they are not realized according to what is well-known.
It was said in its solution: The proposition in relation to those pieces of knowledge is a whole by accident, so its realization is not necessary, like the writer in relation to the rational animal. I say: If it is a whole by accident, then another matter must be considered after occurrence, and it is nothing but its perception, and that is outside by consensus. And taking occurrence with the condition of realization is a correction of the fabricated nature of the essential, and it is impossible.
And the signification (ifada) is prior to realization (iqa'), and the proposition does not await achievement after it. So considering the attachment of realization to occurrence has no role in achieving this reality. The truth is that our saying 'Zayd is standing' is a proposition in all cases, for it conveys a meaning that is possible for truth and falsehood. So in doubt, the hesitation is only about the correspondence of the report, not about the essence of the report and its possibility for both.
Yes, the propositions considered in the sciences are those to which assent attaches; for there is no perfection in achieving doubt. This, even if it has not reached your ears, is the verification. Then, if the parts are three, it is their right that they be indicated by three expressions. The indicator of the relative is called the copula, and in the language of the Arabs, the copula may be omitted, relying [on context].
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the later scholars' view on doubt (shakk). The later scholars claimed that doubt is attached to the 'restrictive relative' (nisba taqyidiyya), which they call the 'relative between-between' (nisba bayn bayn), and that the judgment of occurrence/non-occurrence is only for assent. The author expresses admiration but challenges them: doubt (taraddud) cannot be constituted without relating to occurrence. Both doubt and assent perceive the same thing; the difference is in the mode of perception. Therefore the earlier scholars are correct.
Then a new doubt arises: the three pieces of knowledge (subject, predicate, relative) that constitute a proposition are supposedly realized in doubt, yet according to the famous view they are not. A proposed solution: the proposition is a whole by accident (kull bi al-'arad) relative to those pieces, like 'writer' relative to 'rational animal' (i.e., being a writer is accidental to being human). The author rejects this: if it is accidental, another factor (perception) is needed, but that is outside. The author then argues that signification (ifada) precedes realization (iqa'), so the proposition is complete even without external realization. The truth: 'Zayd is standing' is a proposition in all states because it conveys a meaning that can be true or false. In doubt, hesitation is about the correspondence of the report (hikaya) to reality, not about the report itself.
Finally, the author states that only propositions accompanied by assent are considered in the sciences; there is no perfection in doubt. Then he adds: since a proposition has three parts, they should be indicated by three expressions. The indicator of the relative is called the copula (rabita). In Arabic, the copula may be omitted (e.g., 'Zayd qa'im') relying on context.

Key Terms
الشك
النسبة التقييدية
النسبة بين بين
التردد
الإذعان
الرباطة
الإفادة
الإيقاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's own view, what is the subject of hesitation (taraddud) in doubt?
A) The restrictive relative (nisba taqyidiyya).
B) The occurrence (wuqu') of the judgment.
C) The correspondence of the report (mutaabaqat al-hikaya).
D) The existence of the subject.

Key Takeaway
Doubt does not affect the structure of the proposition; it only concerns the correspondence of the proposition to reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Copula and the Two-Part/Three-Part Distinction
Author explains the copula and two-part/three-part propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the copula in an Arabic categorical proposition, classify it as temporal or non-temporal, and determine whether the proposition is two-part (thunaiyya) or three-part (thulathiyya).

Core Idea
The copula is the relational glue of a proposition; in Arabic it can be implicit or explicit as a noun or verb.

In this passage, the author explains the essential role of the copula in connecting subject and predicate. You will learn how Arabic grammar allows the copula to be omitted, creating a binary proposition, and how its explicit mention makes the proposition ternary. This distinction is crucial for correctly parsing logical statements in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
In any proposition, first locate the subject (mubtada') and the predicate (khabar). The copula indicates the relation between them.
In Arabic, the subject is usually in nominative case.
2
Look for an explicit copula
Check if there is a word like 'huwa' (it is) or 'kana' (was) linking the subject and predicate. If present, the proposition is three-part.
'Huwa' is a non-temporal copula; 'kana' is temporal.
3
If no explicit copula, check inflectional signs
In Arabic, the copula is often omitted because the case endings (i'rab) indicate the relation implicitly. The subject and predicate are both nominative in nominal sentences.
This makes the proposition two-part (thunaiyya).
4
Classify the copula type
If the copula is a noun like 'huwa', it is non-temporal (ghayr zamaniyya). If it is a verb like 'kana', it is temporal (zamaniyya), carrying a time reference.
This classification affects the logical analysis of the proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ إِذَا كَانَتِ الْأَجْزَاءُ ثَلَاثَةٌ فَحَقُهَا أَنْ يُدَلَّ عَلَيْهَا بِثَلَاثِ عِبَارَاتٍ ، فَالدَّالُ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ يُسَمَّى رَابِطَةٌ، وَفي لُغَةِ الْعَرَبِ رُبَّمَا حُذِقَتِ الرَّابِطَةُ : اكْتِفَاء بِعَلَامَاتٍ إِعْرَابِيَّةٍ دَالَّةٍ عَلَيْهَا دَلَالَةَ الْتِزَامِيَّةَ ، فَتُسَمَّى الْقَضِيَّةُ تُنَائِيَّةٌ ، وَرُبَّمَا ذُكِرَتْ ، فَتُسَمَّى ثُلاثِيَّةٌ. وَالْمَذْكُورُ وَإِنْ كَانَ أَدَاةٌ ، لَكِنَّهُ رُبَّمَا كَانَ في قَالَبِ الاِسْمِ ، كَـ هُوَ ، وَيُسَمَّى رَابِطَةٌ غَيْرَ زَمَانِيَّةٍ ، وَ «استن» في الْيُونَانِيَّةِ، وَ(است) في الفَارِسِيَّةِ مِنْهَا. وَرُبَّمَا كَانَ فِي قَالَبِ الْكَلِمَةِ، كَ«كَانَ»، وَيُسَمَّى رَابِطَةٌ زَمَانِيَّةٌ .
Then, if the parts are three, it is proper that they be indicated by three expressions. The indicator of the relation is called the copula. In the Arabic language, the copula may be omitted, relying on inflectional signs that indicate it by implication, so the proposition is called two-part. And sometimes it is mentioned, so it is called three-part. And the mentioned [copula], even if it is a particle, sometimes it is in the form of a noun, like 'huwa', and it is called a non-temporal copula; and 'estin' in Greek, and 'ast' in Persian are of this type. And sometimes it is in the form of a verb, like 'kana', and it is called a temporal copula.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion on the copula (rabita) in propositional logic. The author explains that when a proposition has three components (subject, predicate, relation), they should be expressed by three terms. The term that indicates the relation is called the copula. In Arabic, the copula is often omitted because the inflectional endings (i'rab) implicitly indicate the relation, making the proposition binary (thunaiyya). When the copula is explicitly mentioned, the proposition is ternary (thulathiyya). The copula, even if it is a particle, can take the form of a noun, such as 'huwa' (he/it), which is called a non-temporal copula; examples are given from Greek ('estin') and Persian ('ast'). It can also take the form of a verb, such as 'kana' (was), which is called a temporal copula. This classification is important for understanding the structure of propositions in logic and grammar.

Key Terms
الرَّابِطَة
الْقَضِيَّةُ الثُّنَائِيَّة
الْقَضِيَّةُ الثُّلَاثِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Thulathiyya (three-part)
B) Thunaiyya (two-part)
C) Hamliyya (categorical)
D) Shartiyya (conditional)

Key Takeaway
Arabic propositions can be two-part (copula implied by i'rab) or three-part (copula explicit, either non-temporal like 'huwa' or temporal like 'kana').

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Distinguishing Categorical and Conditional Propositions
Author defines categorical and conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a categorical proposition from a conditional proposition based on the presence or absence of a subject-predicate judgment.

Core Idea
A categorical proposition judges a subject; a conditional judges a relation between two propositions.

This lesson breaks down the author's clear division of propositions into two fundamental types. Understanding this distinction is essential because the logical rules that apply depend on whether a proposition is categorical or conditional. Let's learn how to identify each type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the judgment structure
Look for whether the proposition makes a direct judgment by affirming or denying a predicate of a subject. If it does, it is categorical. If not (i.e., it expresses a relation between two propositions), it is conditional.
2
Locate the components
Categorical propositions have a single subject and a single predicate. Conditional propositions have two parts: an antecedent (condition) and a consequent (result).
3
Test with Islamic examples
Apply the test to fiqh statements: 'Khamr is haram' is categorical (subject: khamr, predicate: haram). 'If khamr intoxicates, then it is haram' is conditional (antecedent: khamr intoxicates, consequent: it is haram).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْقَضِيَّةُ إِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِثْبُوتِ شَيْءٍ لِشَيْءٍ أَوْ نَفْيِهِ عَنْهُ فَحَمْلِيَّةٌ، وَإِلَّا فَشَرْطِيَّةٌ، وَيُسَمَّى المَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ مَوْضُوعًا وَمُقَدَّمًا، وَالْمَحْكُومُ بِهِ مَحْمُولًا وَتَاليا.
And the proposition: if in it judgment is made with the affirmation of something for something or its negation from it, then it is categorical; otherwise it is conditional. And the thing judged upon is called subject (mawdu') and antecedent (muqaddam); and the thing judged with is called predicate (mahmul) and consequent (tali).
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage provides a concise definition of the two main types of propositions in logic: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). A categorical proposition is one in which judgment is made by affirming or denying a predicate of a subject, e.g., 'Zayd is standing'. A conditional proposition is one that does not directly affirm or deny but expresses a relation between two propositions, e.g., 'If Zayd is standing, then he is moving'. The author then assigns terminology: the thing judged about (mahkum 'alayhi) is called subject (mawdu') in categorical and antecedent (muqaddam) in conditional; the thing judged with (mahkum bihi) is called predicate (mahmul) in categorical and consequent (tali) in conditional. This dual terminology is essential for understanding syllogistic theory.

Key Terms
الْقَضِيَّةُ الْحَمْلِيَّة
الْقَضِيَّةُ الشَّرْطِيَّة
الْمُقَدَّم
التَّالِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Mahmul
B) Tali
C) Muqaddam
D) Rabita

Key Takeaway
Propositions are either categorical (subject-predicate judgment) or conditional (relation between two propositions); this classification determines logical structure.

Parts of Categorical and Conditional Propositions
Author names parts of propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject and predicate in a categorical proposition and the antecedent and consequent in a conditional proposition.

Core Idea
The 'thing judged about' is subject in categorical, antecedent in conditional; the 'thing judged with' is predicate in categorical, consequent in conditional.

After learning to distinguish the two types, we now need to name their parts. The author gives a single mapping: the same logical roles (mahkum 'alayhi and mahkum bihi) receive different names depending on the proposition type. This lesson helps you apply those names correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mahkum 'alayhi (thing judged about)
In any proposition, find what is being judged about. That is the mahkum 'alayhi.
2
Identify the mahkum bihi (thing judged with)
Find what is being affirmed or denied of the mahkum 'alayhi. That is the mahkum bihi.
3
Determine the proposition type and assign names
If the proposition is categorical, the mahkum 'alayhi is the subject (mawdu') and the mahkum bihi is the predicate (mahmul). If conditional, the mahkum 'alayhi is the antecedent (muqaddam) and the mahkum bihi is the consequent (tali).
In conditional propositions, each part (antecedent and consequent) is itself a complete proposition, not a single term.

Source Text

And the thing judged upon is called subject (mawdu') and antecedent (muqaddam); and the thing judged with is called predicate (mahmul) and consequent (tali).
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage provides a concise definition of the two main types of propositions in logic: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). A categorical proposition is one in which judgment is made by affirming or denying a predicate of a subject, e.g., 'Zayd is standing'. A conditional proposition is one that does not directly affirm or deny but expresses a relation between two propositions, e.g., 'If Zayd is standing, then he is moving'. The author then assigns terminology: the thing judged about (mahkum 'alayhi) is called subject (mawdu') in categorical and antecedent (muqaddam) in conditional; the thing judged with (mahkum bihi) is called predicate (mahmul) in categorical and consequent (tali) in conditional. This dual terminology is essential for understanding syllogistic theory.

The Locus of Judgment in Conditionals: Logicians vs. Grammarians
Author presents the dispute between logicians and grammarians on whether judgment in conditionals links antecedent and consequent or the consequent alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical and grammatical positions on the locus of judgment in conditional propositions and evaluate the arguments for and against each view.

Core Idea
A conditional can be true even if its consequent is false in reality.

This passage dives into a core disagreement between logicians and grammarians: where exactly does the judgment reside in a conditional sentence? The logicians argue that the judgment connects the antecedent and consequent, while the grammarians place it solely in the consequent. Through the famous example of Zayd the donkey, you will see why the logicians have the stronger case, and then confront a subtle objection about hypothetical times.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing positions
Read the first sentence carefully. The logicians hold that the judgment (ḥukm) in a conditional is between the antecedent and consequent—it asserts a connection. The grammarians hold that judgment is only in the consequent (al-jazāʾ), with the condition serving as a restriction like a state or adverb.
2
Analyze al-Sayyid’s proof via the donkey example
Al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq offers the conditional 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray' as a counterexample to the grammarians’ view. This conditional is undeniably true, yet the consequent 'Zayd brays' is false in reality. If the judgment were only on the consequent, the conditional would be false—proving that the judgment must encompass the connection.
The key logical point: the truth of the conditional does not require the truth of its parts; it requires that the connection holds.
3
Examine al-Dawwani’s objection about hypothetical times
Al-Dawwani responds: the consequent may be false in actual times, but it is true in hypothetical times (the times supposed by the antecedent). In the imagined scenario where Zayd is a donkey, braying is indeed true of him. He draws an analogy with belief: 'Zayd is standing in my belief' is not falsified by reality.
This objection challenges whether the logicians’ counterexample truly refutes the grammarians.
4
Evaluate the strengths and determine the likely resolution
Consider whether al-Dawwani’s hypothetical times defense saves the grammarians’ view. The text begins to refute it but cuts off. However, the logicians’ position remains intuitive: the conditional asserts a relationship, not a separate fact. Reflect on which view better aligns with how conditionals function in Islamic sciences.
The debate shows the importance of clarifying the truth-conditions of conditional statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ مَذْهَبَ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ بَيْنَ الْمُقَدَّمِ وَالتَّالِي ، وَمَذْهَبُ أَهْلِ الْعَرَبِيَّةِ أَنَّهُ فِي الْجَزَاءِ، وَالشَّرْطُ قَيْدٌ لِلْمُسْنَدِ فِيهِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْحَالِ أَوِ الطَّرْفِ ، كَذَا فِي «الْمِفْتَاحِ». قَالَ السَّيِّدُ الْمُحَقِّقُ قُدَّسَ سِرُّهُ: الْأَوَّلُ هُوَ الْحَقُّ؛ لِلْقَطْعِ بِصِدْقِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ مَعَ كَذِبِ التَّالِي فِي الْوَاقِعِ ، كَقَوْلِنَا إِنْ كَانَ زَيْدٌ حِمَارًا كَانَ نَاهِقًا، وَلَوْ كَانَ الْخَبَرُ هُوَ التَّالِي لَمْ يُتَصَوَّرْ صِدْقُهَا مَعَ كَذِبِهِ؛ ضَرُورَةَ اسْتِلْزَامِ انْتِفَاءِ الْمُطْلَقِ انْتِفَاءَ الْمُقَيَّد.
قَالَ الْعَلَّامَةُ الدواني لا رَحمَ اللهُ: كَذِبُ التَّالي في جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ كَذِبُهُ فِي الْأَوْقَاتِ التَّقْدِيرِيَّةِ ، فَالنَّاهِقِيَّةُ فِي جَمِيعِ أَوْقَاتٍ قُدْرَ فِيهَا حِمَارِيَّةُ زَيْدٍ ثَابِتَةٌ لَهُ ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ بِحَسَبِ الْأَوْقَاتِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ مَسْلُوبَةٌ عَنْهُ ، أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ فِي ظَنِّي لَمْ يَكْذِبْ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْقِيَامِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ .
وَمَا ذُكِرَ مِنَ الِاسْتِلْزَامِ فَمُسَلَّمٌ، لَكِنْ لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ الْمُطْلَقَ هَهُنَا مُنْتَفٍ؛ فَإِنَّهُ
And know that the doctrine of the logicians is that the judgment in the conditional is between the antecedent and the consequent, and the doctrine of the grammarians is that it is in the consequent, and the condition is a restriction for the predicate, like the state or adverb, as in 'al-Miftah'. Al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq (may his secret be sanctified) said: The first is the truth, because of the certainty of the truth of the conditional with the falsehood of the consequent in reality, like our statement: 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray.' If the report were the consequent, its truth with its falsehood would be inconceivable, due to the necessary entailment that the negation of the absolute entails the negation of the restricted. Al-Allama al-Dawwani (may God not have mercy on him) said: The falsehood of the consequent in all actual times does not necessitate its falsehood in hypothetical times. For the braying in all times in which Zayd's donkey-ness is supposed is established for him, even if in actual times it is denied of him. Do you not see that 'Zayd is standing in my belief' is not falsified by the absence of standing in reality? And what was mentioned of entailment is conceded, but we do not concede that the absolute here is negated; for it...
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange about the locus of judgment in conditional propositions. The author first presents two doctrines: the logicians hold that judgment in a conditional links the antecedent and consequent (i.e., the conditional as a whole asserts a connection), while the grammarians hold that judgment is only in the consequent (al-jaza'), with the condition serving as a restriction on the predicate. Al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq (likely al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani) argues for the logicians' view, using the example 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray.' This conditional is true even though the consequent 'Zayd brays' is false in reality. If the grammarians were correct and judgment was in the consequent alone, the conditional would be false when the consequent is false, but it is true; thus the logicians are right. Al-Dawwani objects: the falsehood of the consequent in actual times does not entail its falsehood in hypothetical times. In the hypothetical scenario where Zayd is a donkey, the property of braying is true of him. He analogizes with belief: 'Zayd is standing in my belief' is not falsified by actual non-standing. The author's own position appears to be a defense of the logicians' view, as he begins to refute al-Dawwani's objection, but the text cuts off. The key point is understanding the conditional as a relation between propositions, not as a simple categorical about the consequent.

Key Terms
الشَّرْطِيَّة
الْجَزَاء
الْأَوْقَاتُ التَّقْدِيرِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq argue that the logicians' view (judgment between antecedent and consequent) is correct?
A) Because grammarians are always wrong
B) Because a conditional can be true even if its consequent is false in reality
C) Because the antecedent must be true for the conditional to be meaningful
D) Because al-Dawwani's objection is invalid

Key Takeaway
The debate hinges on whether a conditional's truth depends on the connection between antecedent and consequent or on the truth of the consequent alone; the donkey example shows the connection view is correct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the conditional statement: 'If an act is commanded by the Lawgiver, then it is good.' According to the grammarians' view, the judgment 'it is good' is the main report, and the condition 'if commanded' is a restriction. Explain what this would imply about the truth of the conditional when the act is not actually commanded. Then apply al-Sayyid's argument from the donkey example to show why the logicians' view is preferable for understanding such conditionals in usul al-fiqh.

Even if the person is not actually traveling, the conditional is considered true. Analyse how this example supports either the grammarians' or the logicians' view, and explain which interpretation is more consistent with juristic reasoning.

Categorical and Conditional Propositions: Definitions and Terminology
Definition of categorical and conditional propositions with terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya) propositions and identify their components (subject/predicate, antecedent/consequent).

Core Idea
A conditional proposition judges a connection between two statements, not the truth of either alone.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of propositions in Islamic logic: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). You will learn the key terms used to describe their parts and understand the crucial difference between directly affirming a predicate and judging a relational connection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Examine the wording: if the proposition directly affirms or negates a predicate of a subject, it is categorical (hamliyya); if it connects two statements with a condition (e.g., 'if...then'), it is conditional (shartiyya).
The passage defines shartiyya as the residual category.
2
Extract the components of a categorical proposition
For a hamliyya, locate the subject (mawdu') and predicate (mahmul). The judgment is about the affirmation or negation of the predicate for the subject.
3
Extract the components of a conditional proposition
For a shartiyya, locate the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). Note that the judgment is not about the truth of either part independently, but about the connection (ittisal) between them.
The author later argues that this connection is what is judged.
4
Distinguish the two types using an Islamic example
Consider: 'Salah is obligatory.' This is hamliyya: subject = salah, predicate = obligatory. Now consider: 'If salah is performed with doubt, it is invalid.' This is shartiyya: antecedent = 'salah is performed with doubt', consequent = 'it is invalid'. The judgment is that the invalidity follows from the doubt.
