وَالْقَضِيَّةُ إِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِثْبُوتِ شَيْءٍ لِشَيْءٍ أَوْ نَفْيِهِ عَنْهُ فَحَمْلِيَّةٌ، وَإِلَّا فَشَرْطِيَّةٌ، وَيُسَمَّى المَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ مَوْضُوعًا وَمُقَدَّمًا، وَالْمَحْكُومُ بِهِ مَحْمُولًا وَتَالِيًا. وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ مَذْهَبَ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ بَيْنَ الْمُقَدَّمِ وَالتَّالِي، وَمَذْهَبُ أَهْلِ الْعَرَبِيَّةِ أَنَّهُ فِي الْجَزَاءِ، وَالشَّرْطُ قَيْدٌ لِلْمُسْنَدِ فِيهِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْحَالِ أَوِ الطَّرْفِ، كَذَا فِي «الْمِفْتَاحِ». قَالَ السَّيِّدُ الْمُحَقِّقُ قُدَّسَ سِرُّهُ: الْأَوَّلُ هُوَ الْحَقُّ؛ لِلْقَطْعِ بِصِدْقِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ مَعَ كَذِبِ التَّالِي فِي الْوَاقِعِ، كَقَوْلِنَا إِنْ كَانَ زَيْدٌ حِمَارًا كَانَ نَاهِقًا، وَلَوْ كَانَ الْخَبَرُ هُوَ التَّالِي لَمْ يُتَصَوَّرْ صِدْقُهَا مَعَ كَذِبِهِ؛ ضَرُورَةَ اسْتِلْزَامِ انْتِفَاءِ الْمُطْلَقِ انْتِفَاءَ الْمُقَيَّدِ. قَالَ الْعَلَّامَةُ الدَّوَّانِيُّ لَا رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ: كَذِبُ التَّالِي فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ كَذِبُهُ فِي الْأَوْقَاتِ التَّقْدِيرِيَّةِ، فَالنَّاهِقِيَّةُ فِي جَمِيعِ أَوْقَاتٍ قُدِرَ فِيهَا حِمَارِيَّةُ زَيْدٍ ثَابِتَةٌ لَهُ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ بِحَسَبِ الْأَوْقَاتِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ مَسْلُوبَةً عَنْهُ، أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ زَيْدًا قَائِمٌ فِي ظَنِّي لَمْ يَكْذِبْ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْقِيَامِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ. وَمَا ذُكِرَ مِنَ الِاسْتِلْزَامِ فَمُسَلَّمٌ، لَكِنْ لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ الْمُطْلَقَ هَهُنَا مُنْتَفٍ؛ فَإِنَّهُ الْمَأْخُوذُ عَلَى وَجْهِ أَعَمَّ مِمَّا فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ أَيْضًا، غَايَةُ مَا يُقَالُ: إِنَّ الْعِبَارَةَ غَيْرُ مَوْضُوعَةٍ لِتَأْدِيَةِ ذَلِكَ الْمَعْنَى مُطَابَقَةً، وَلَا ضَيْرَ فِيهِ، وَبِمِثْلِ ذَلِكَ يَنْحَلُّ شُبْهَةُ مَعْدُومِ النَّظِيرِ. أَقُولُ: إِنَّهُمْ - وَمِنْهُمُ الْمُحَقِّقُ الدَّوَّانِيُّ - جَوَّزُوا اسْتِلْزَامَ شَيْءٍ لِلنَّقِيضِ وَالنَّقِيضَيْنِ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى جَوَازِ اسْتِلْزَامِ مُحَالٍ مُحَالًا، وَتَشَبَّلُوا بِذَلِكَ فِي مَوَاضِعَ عَدِيدَةٍ، مِنْهَا فِي جَوَابِ الْمُغَالَطَةِ الْعَامَّةِ الْوُرُودِ الْمَشْهُورَةِ، مِنْ أَنَّ الْمُدَّعَى ثَابِتٌ، وَإِلَّا فَنَقِيضُهُ ثَابِتٌ، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ نَقِيضُهُ ثَابِتًا كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْأَشْيَاءِ ثَابِتًا، فَكُلَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنِ الْمُدَّعَى ثَابِتًا كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْأَشْيَاءِ ثَابِتًا، وَيَنْعَكِسُ بِعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ إِلَى قَوْلِنَا كُلَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْأَشْيَاءِ ثَابِتًا كَانَ الْمُدَّعَى ثَابِتًا، هَذَا خُلْفٌ. وَبَعْدَ تَمْهِيدِ ذَلِكَ نَقُولُ: لَوْ كَانَ الشَّرْطُ قَيْدًا لِلْمُسْنَدِ فِي الْجَزَاءِ لَزِمَ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ فِيمَا إِذَا كَانَ الْمُقَدَّمُ مَلْزُومًا لَهُمَا، فَإِنَّ قَوْلَنَا زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ فِي وَقْتِ عَدَمِ ثُبُوتِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْأَشْيَاءِ يُنَاقِضُ قَوْلَنَا زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِقَائِمٍ فِي ذَلِكَ الْوَقْتِ، وَهُوَ بَدِيهِيٌّ. وَأَمَّا إِذَا كَانَ الْحُكْمُ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ بِالِاتِّصَالِ بَيْنَ النِّسْبَتَيْنِ فَلَا يَلْزَمُ ذَلِكَ، فَإِنَّ نَقِيضَ الِاتِّصَالِ رَفْعُهُ، لَا وُجُودُ اتِّصَالٍ آخَرَ أَيَّ اتِّصَالٍ كَانَ. فَمَذْهَبُ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ هُوَ الْحَقُّ.
The proposition: if judgment is made in it of the affirmation of something for something or its negation from it, then it is categorical; otherwise, it is conditional. The judged-upon is called subject and antecedent, and the judged-with is called predicate and consequent. Know that the doctrine of the logicians is that the judgment in the conditional is between the antecedent and the consequent, while the doctrine of the Arabic grammarians is that it is in the consequent, and the condition is a restriction for the predicate in it like a circumstantial qualifier or an adverbial phrase, as in 'al-Miftah'. Al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq (may his secret be sanctified) said: The first is the truth; due to the certainty of the truth of the conditional with the falsity of the consequent in reality, like our statement 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray'. And if the predicate were the consequent, its truth could not be conceived with its falsity; because of the necessity that the negation of the absolute entails the negation of the qualified. Al-Allama al-Dawwani (may God not have mercy on him) said: The falsity of the consequent in all actual times does not necessitate its falsity in all hypothetical times. For the braying property is established for Zayd in all times in which his donkeyhood is assumed, even if it is negated from him according to actual times. Do you not see that 'Zayd is standing' in my opinion is not falsified by the non-existence of standing in reality. What was mentioned of the entailment is conceded, but we do not concede that the absolute here is non-existent; for it is taken in a more general sense than what is in the mind as well. The most that can be said is that the expression is not set to convey that meaning by way of full correspondence, and there is no harm in it. By such a thing the doubt of the peerless is resolved. I say: They - and among them al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani - permitted the entailment of something for a contradiction and two contradictions, based on the permissibility of an impossibility entailing an impossibility, and they clung to that in numerous places, including in answering a widely circulating common fallacy: that the claim is established, otherwise its contradiction is established, and whenever its contradiction is established, something of things is established, so whenever the claim is not established, something of things is established, and it is reversed by the contrapositive to our statement 'Whenever nothing of things is established, the claim is established'; this is absurd. After laying that premise, we say: If the condition were a restriction for the predicate in the consequent, then the conjunction of two contradictions would be necessary in case the antecedent is a necessary condition for them both. For our statement 'Zayd is standing at a time of non-establishment of any thing from things' contradicts our statement 'Zayd is not standing at that time', and that is self-evident. But if the judgment in the conditional is about the connection between the two relationships, then that does not follow; for the contradictory of connection is its removal, not the existence of another connection, whatever that connection might be. Thus the doctrine of the logicians is the truth.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the nature of propositions, particularly conditional propositions, and the dispute between logicians and grammarians regarding where the judgment lies in a conditional. It begins by defining a categorical proposition (hamliyya) as one that affirms or denies something of something, and a conditional proposition (shartiyya) as the alternative. The terms for the judged-upon and judged-with are given: subject/antecedent (mawdu'/muqaddam) and predicate/consequent (mahmul/tali). The author then states the logicians' view: the judgment in a conditional is a relation between the antecedent and consequent. The grammarians, however, hold that the judgment is in the consequent alone, with the condition serving as a restriction (qayd) on the predicate, analogous to a circumstantial qualifier (hal) or adverbial (taraf). This is attributed to 'al-Miftah' (likely a reference to Miftah al-'Ulum). Al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq (al-Sharif al-Jurjani) argues that the logicians' view is correct because a conditional can be true even if the consequent is false in reality. For example, 'If Zayd is a donkey, he brays' is a true conditional even though Zayd is not actually a donkey and does not bray. If the judgment were solely about the consequent (braying), the conditional would be false because the consequent is false in reality. Al-Dawwani responds that the consequent's falsity in actual times does not affect its truth in the hypothetical times assumed by the condition. In the hypothetical scenario where Zayd is a donkey, braying is indeed true for him. The analogy is 'Zayd is standing in my opinion' — this statement is not falsified by Zayd not actually standing. Al-Dawwani concedes the logicians' entailment argument but denies that the 'absolute' here is non-existent; rather, it is taken in a broad sense. The author then adds his own resolution: logicians permit the entailment of a contradiction or two contradictions because they allow that an impossibility can entail another impossibility. This is used to resolve a popular fallacy: if the claim is not established, then its contradictory is established, leading to the conclusion that something is always established, which through contrapositive yields a contradiction. The author then presents a stronger argument: if the condition were a restriction on the predicate in the consequent, then when the antecedent itself is a necessary condition for two contradictory predicates (e.g., 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing' at the same time), the conditional would imply the conjunction of two contradictions, which is absurd. But if the judgment is about the connection (ittisal) between the two relationships (the antecedent's relationship and the consequent's relationship), then no such contradiction arises because the contradictory of connection is simply its absence, not the existence of a different connection. Thus the logicians' view is correct. This passage demonstrates a nuanced understanding of conditionals and defends the logical perspective against grammatical objections.

قَضِيَّة
حَمْلِيَّة
شَرْطِيَّة
مَوْضُوع
مَحْمُول
مُقَدَّم
تَالٍ

The Conditional Dispute: Logicians vs Grammarians and the Resolution
Dispute between logicians and grammarians on the locus of judgment in conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dispute between logicians and grammarians on the locus of judgment in conditionals, and explain why the logicians' view is supported by the argument from contradiction.

Core Idea
The conditional's judgment is a connection between antecedent and consequent, not an assertion of the consequent alone; otherwise contradictions arise.

This lesson delves into a classical debate: where exactly does the judgment reside in a conditional proposition? The logicians argue it is a relation between the two parts, while the grammarians claim the judgment is only in the consequent. We will follow the arguments that settle the matter.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Logicians: judgment in a conditional is between the antecedent and consequent (a connection). Grammarians: judgment is only in the consequent; the condition is a restriction (qayd) on the predicate, like a circumstantial qualifier.
Referenced from 'al-Miftah' (Miftah al-Ulum).
2
Analyze al-Sayyid's argument
Al-Sayyid gives the example 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray'. This conditional is true even though Zayd is not a donkey and does not bray in reality. If the judgment were the consequent alone, it would be false. Therefore, the judgment must be about the connection, not the consequent.
This shows that the conditional's truth does not depend on the actual truth of the consequent.
3
Examine al-Dawwani's objection
Al-Dawwani replies that the consequent is true in the hypothetical times assumed by the antecedent. The falsity in actual times does not matter. He draws an analogy: 'Zayd is standing' in my opinion is not falsified by Zayd not actually standing.
He concedes the entailment but denies that the absolute here is non-existent.
4
Understand the author's resolution
The author argues that if the condition were a qayd on the predicate, then in cases where the antecedent is a necessary condition for two contradictory predicates (e.g., standing and not standing), the conditional would imply the conjunction of contradictory propositions, which is absurd. But if the judgment is about the connection (ittisal), then the contradictory is simply the absence of that connection, not a different connection. Thus no contradiction arises.
This resolves the dispute in favor of the logicians.

وَالْقَضِيَّةُ إِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِثْبُوتِ شَيْءٍ لِشَيْءٍ أَوْ نَفْيِهِ عَنْهُ فَحَمْلِيَّةٌ، وَإِلَّا فَشَرْطِيَّةٌ، وَيُسَمَّى المَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ مَوْضُوعًا وَمُقَدَّمًا، وَالْمَحْكُومُ بِهِ مَحْمُولًا وَتَالِيًا. وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ مَذْهَبَ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ بَيْنَ الْمُقَدَّمِ وَالتَّالِي، وَمَذْهَبُ أَهْلِ الْعَرَبِيَّةِ أَنَّهُ فِي الْجَزَاءِ، وَالشَّرْطُ قَيْدٌ لِلْمُسْنَدِ فِيهِ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْحَالِ أَوِ الطَّرْفِ، كَذَا فِي «الْمِفْتَاحِ». قَالَ السَّيِّدُ الْمُحَقِّقُ قُدَّسَ سِرُّهُ: الْأَوَّلُ هُوَ الْحَقُّ؛ لِلْقَطْعِ بِصِدْقِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ مَعَ كَذِبِ التَّالِي فِي الْوَاقِعِ، كَقَوْلِنَا إِنْ كَانَ زَيْدٌ حِمَارًا كَانَ نَاهِقًا، وَلَوْ كَانَ الْخَبَرُ هُوَ التَّالِي لَمْ يُتَصَوَّرْ صِدْقُهَا مَعَ كَذِبِهِ؛ ضَرُورَةَ اسْتِلْزَامِ انْتِفَاءِ الْمُطْلَقِ انْتِفَاءَ الْمُقَيَّدِ. قَالَ الْعَلَّامَةُ الدَّوَّانِيُّ لَا رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ: كَذِبُ التَّالِي فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ كَذِبُهُ فِي الْأَوْقَاتِ التَّقْدِيرِيَّةِ، فَالنَّاهِقِيَّةُ فِي جَمِيعِ أَوْقَاتٍ قُدِرَ فِيهَا حِمَارِيَّةُ زَيْدٍ ثَابِتَةٌ لَهُ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ بِحَسَبِ الْأَوْقَاتِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ مَسْلُوبَةً عَنْهُ، أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ زَيْدًا قَائِمٌ فِي ظَنِّي لَمْ يَكْذِبْ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْقِيَامِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ. وَمَا ذُكِرَ مِنَ الِاسْتِلْزَامِ فَمُسَلَّمٌ، لَكِنْ لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ الْمُطْلَقَ هَهُنَا مُنْتَفٍ؛ فَإِنَّهُ الْمَأْخُوذُ عَلَى وَجْهِ أَعَمَّ مِمَّا فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ أَيْضًا، غَايَةُ مَا يُقَالُ: إِنَّ الْعِبَارَةَ غَيْرُ مَوْضُوعَةٍ لِتَأْدِيَةِ ذَلِكَ الْمَعْنَى مُطَابَقَةً، وَلَا ضَيْرَ فِيهِ، وَبِمِثْلِ ذَلِكَ يَنْحَلُّ شُبْهَةُ مَعْدُومِ النَّظِيرِ. أَقُولُ: إِنَّهُمْ - وَمِنْهُمُ الْمُحَقِّقُ الدَّوَّانِيُّ - جَوَّزُوا اسْتِلْزَامَ شَيْءٍ لِلنَّقِيضِ وَالنَّقِيضَيْنِ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى جَوَازِ اسْتِلْزَامِ مُحَالٍ مُحَالًا، وَتَشَبَّلُوا بِذَلِكَ فِي مَوَاضِعَ عَدِيدَةٍ، مِنْهَا فِي جَوَابِ الْمُغَالَطَةِ الْعَامَّةِ الْوُرُودِ الْمَشْهُورَةِ، مِنْ أَنَّ الْمُدَّعَى ثَابِتٌ، وَإِلَّا فَنَقِيضُهُ ثَابِتٌ، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ نَقِيضُهُ ثَابِتًا كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْأَشْيَاءِ ثَابِتًا، فَكُلَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنِ الْمُدَّعَى ثَابِتًا كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْأَشْيَاءِ ثَابِتًا، وَيَنْعَكِسُ بِعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ إِلَى قَوْلِنَا كُلَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْأَشْيَاءِ ثَابِتًا كَانَ الْمُدَّعَى ثَابِتًا، هَذَا خُلْفٌ. وَبَعْدَ تَمْهِيدِ ذَلِكَ نَقُولُ: لَوْ كَانَ الشَّرْطُ قَيْدًا لِلْمُسْنَدِ فِي الْجَزَاءِ لَزِمَ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ فِيمَا إِذَا كَانَ الْمُقَدَّمُ مَلْزُومًا لَهُمَا، فَإِنَّ قَوْلَنَا زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ فِي وَقْتِ عَدَمِ ثُبُوتِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْأَشْيَاءِ يُنَاقِضُ قَوْلَنَا زَيْدٌ لَيْسَ بِقَائِمٍ فِي ذَلِكَ الْوَقْتِ، وَهُوَ بَدِيهِيٌّ. وَأَمَّا إِذَا كَانَ الْحُكْمُ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ بِالِاتِّصَالِ بَيْنَ النِّسْبَتَيْنِ فَلَا يَلْزَمُ ذَلِكَ، فَإِنَّ نَقِيضَ الِاتِّصَالِ رَفْعُهُ، لَا وُجُودُ اتِّصَالٍ آخَرَ أَيَّ اتِّصَالٍ كَانَ. فَمَذْهَبُ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ هُوَ الْحَقُّ.
The proposition: if judgment is made in it of the affirmation of something for something or its negation from it, then it is categorical; otherwise, it is conditional. The judged-upon is called subject and antecedent, and the judged-with is called predicate and consequent. Know that the doctrine of the logicians is that the judgment in the conditional is between the antecedent and the consequent, while the doctrine of the Arabic grammarians is that it is in the consequent, and the condition is a restriction for the predicate in it like a circumstantial qualifier or an adverbial phrase, as in 'al-Miftah'. Al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq (may his secret be sanctified) said: The first is the truth; due to the certainty of the truth of the conditional with the falsity of the consequent in reality, like our statement 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray'. And if the predicate were the consequent, its truth could not be conceived with its falsity; because of the necessity that the negation of the absolute entails the negation of the qualified. Al-Allama al-Dawwani (may God not have mercy on him) said: The falsity of the consequent in all actual times does not necessitate its falsity in all hypothetical times. For the braying property is established for Zayd in all times in which his donkeyhood is assumed, even if it is negated from him according to actual times. Do you not see that 'Zayd is standing' in my opinion is not falsified by the non-existence of standing in reality. What was mentioned of the entailment is conceded, but we do not concede that the absolute here is non-existent; for it is taken in a more general sense than what is in the mind as well. The most that can be said is that the expression is not set to convey that meaning by way of full correspondence, and there is no harm in it. By such a thing the doubt of the peerless is resolved. I say: They - and among them al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani - permitted the entailment of something for a contradiction and two contradictions, based on the permissibility of an impossibility entailing an impossibility, and they clung to that in numerous places, including in answering a widely circulating common fallacy: that the claim is established, otherwise its contradiction is established, and whenever its contradiction is established, something of things is established, so whenever the claim is not established, something of things is established, and it is reversed by the contrapositive to our statement 'Whenever nothing of things is established, the claim is established'; this is absurd. After laying that premise, we say: If the condition were a restriction for the predicate in the consequent, then the conjunction of two contradictions would be necessary in case the antecedent is a necessary condition for them both. For our statement 'Zayd is standing at a time of non-establishment of any thing from things' contradicts our statement 'Zayd is not standing at that time', and that is self-evident. But if the judgment in the conditional is about the connection between the two relationships, then that does not follow; for the contradictory of connection is its removal, not the existence of another connection, whatever that connection might be. Thus the doctrine of the logicians is the truth.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the nature of propositions, particularly conditional propositions, and the dispute between logicians and grammarians regarding where the judgment lies in a conditional. It begins by defining a categorical proposition (hamliyya) as one that affirms or denies something of something, and a conditional proposition (shartiyya) as the alternative. The terms for the judged-upon and judged-with are given: subject/antecedent (mawdu'/muqaddam) and predicate/consequent (mahmul/tali). The author then states the logicians' view: the judgment in a conditional is a relation between the antecedent and consequent. The grammarians, however, hold that the judgment is in the consequent alone, with the condition serving as a restriction (qayd) on the predicate, analogous to a circumstantial qualifier (hal) or adverbial (taraf). This is attributed to 'al-Miftah' (likely a reference to Miftah al-'Ulum). Al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq (al-Sharif al-Jurjani) argues that the logicians' view is correct because a conditional can be true even if the consequent is false in reality. For example, 'If Zayd is a donkey, he brays' is a true conditional even though Zayd is not actually a donkey and does not bray. If the judgment were solely about the consequent (braying), the conditional would be false because the consequent is false in reality. Al-Dawwani responds that the consequent's falsity in actual times does not affect its truth in the hypothetical times assumed by the condition. In the hypothetical scenario where Zayd is a donkey, braying is indeed true for him. The analogy is 'Zayd is standing in my opinion' — this statement is not falsified by Zayd not actually standing. Al-Dawwani concedes the logicians' entailment argument but denies that the 'absolute' here is non-existent; rather, it is taken in a broad sense. The author then adds his own resolution: logicians permit the entailment of a contradiction or two contradictions because they allow that an impossibility can entail another impossibility. This is used to resolve a popular fallacy: if the claim is not established, then its contradictory is established, leading to the conclusion that something is always established, which through contrapositive yields a contradiction. The author then presents a stronger argument: if the condition were a restriction on the predicate in the consequent, then when the antecedent itself is a necessary condition for two contradictory predicates (e.g., 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing' at the same time), the conditional would imply the conjunction of two contradictions, which is absurd. But if the judgment is about the connection (ittisal) between the two relationships (the antecedent's relationship and the consequent's relationship), then no such contradiction arises because the contradictory of connection is simply its absence, not the existence of a different connection. Thus the logicians' view is correct. This passage demonstrates a nuanced understanding of conditionals and defends the logical perspective against grammatical objections.

اتِّصَال
نَقِيض
مُقَدَّم
تَالٍ

Checkpoint
Which argument did al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq use to support the logicians' view?
A) The conditional 'If Zayd is a donkey, he brays' is true even though Zayd does not bray in reality.
B) The condition functions like a circumstantial qualifier (hal) in grammar.
C) The antecedent and consequent must both be true for the conditional to be true.
D) The judgment in a conditional must be about the connection because otherwise contradictions would arise.

Key Takeaway
The logicians' view that a conditional judges a connection between antecedent and consequent is supported by the possibility of true conditionals with false consequents and the avoidance of contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the conditional: 'If the contract includes riba, it is invalid.' According to the grammarians, the judgment is that 'it is invalid' is asserted with the condition as a restriction. According to the logicians, the judgment is a connection between 'contract includes riba' and 'it is invalid'. Test which view is correct using al-Sayyid's method: is the conditional still true if the consequent is false in reality? (e.g., if no riba is actually present)
?
Construct a scenario where treating the condition as a qayd would lead to a contradiction, as the author argued.

Classifying Propositions by Subject Type: Shakhsiyya, Muhmala, Tabi'iyya
Author classifies propositions based on subject type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given proposition is shakhsiyya, muhmala, or tabi'iyya based on its subject type and the presence or absence of the condition of mental unity.

Core Idea
Three proposition types arise from how we treat the subject: as a particular individual, as a universal without condition, or as a universal in its mental unity.

In this lesson, we examine how logicians classify propositions based on the nature of their subject (mawdu'). The author distinguishes between a particular subject, which yields an individual proposition, and a universal subject, which splits into further types depending on whether we add a condition or not. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for correctly applying propositions in syllogisms, as the quantity and condition of the subject affect logical distribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject type
Look at the proposition's subject: is it a particular individual (like 'Zayd') or a universal term (like 'man')? If particular, the proposition is shakhsiyya.
Particular subjects refer to a single specific entity.
2
Check for the condition of mental unity
If the subject is universal, ask: is judgment made on the universal itself without any condition, or with the condition of mental unity (al-wahda al-dhihniyya)? Without condition → muhmala; with mental unity → tabi'iyya.
Mental unity means considering the universal as a single concept in the mind, not distributed over individuals.
3
Recognize the ancients' usage
The term muhmala (unrestricted) is used by the ancients (al-qudama') for propositions where the universal is taken without quantification. Note that modern logicians may treat muhmala differently.
This historical context is important for reading older texts.
4
Distinguish with examples
Practice: classify propositions like 'Zayd is standing' (shakhsiyya), 'Man is a species' (muhmala), 'Man is a rational animal' (tabi'iyya). Note the difference between muhmala (no condition) and tabi'iyya (mental unity).
The same universal term can appear in different types depending on how it is understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَوْضُوعُ إِنْ كَانَ جُزْئِيًّا فَالْقَضِيَّةُ شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَمَخْصُوصَةٌ، وَإِنْ كَانَ كُلِّيًّا فَإِنْ حُكِمَ عَلَيْهِ بِلَا زِيَادَةِ شَرْطٍ فَمُهْمَلَةٌ عِنْدَ الْقَدَمَاءِ، وَإِنْ حُكِمَ عَلَيْهِ بِشَرْطِ الْوَحْدَةِ الذِّهْنِيَّةِ فَطَبِيعِيَّةٌ. وَإِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى أَفْرَادِهِ، فَإِنْ بَيَّنَ فِيهَا كَمِّيَّةُ أَفْرَادِهِ
The subject: if it is particular, then the proposition is individual and specific; and if it is universal, then if judgment is made upon it without the addition of a condition, it is unrestricted according to the ancients; and if judgment is made upon it with the condition of mental unity, it is natural; and if judgment is made in it upon its individuals, then if the quantity of its individuals is specified in it...
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of propositions based on the nature of the subject (mawdu'). It begins by distinguishing the subject as either particular (juz'i) or universal (kulli). If the subject is particular, the proposition is called shakhsiyya (individual) or makhsusa (specific). For example, 'Zayd is standing' — here 'Zayd' is a particular subject. If the subject is universal, then three types are distinguished based on how the judgment is made. First, if the judgment is made on the universal itself without any added condition (bila ziyadat shart), the proposition is called muhmala (unrestricted) according to the ancients (al-qudama'). For example, 'Human is a species' — here 'human' is taken as a universal concept without considering its individuals. Second, if the judgment is made on the universal with the condition of mental unity (shart al-wahda al-dhihniyya), the proposition is called tabi'iyya (natural). For example, 'Man is a rational animal' — here 'man' is considered as a concept in the mind with its mental unity. Third, the text begins to discuss the case where judgment is made upon the individuals of the universal. It starts: 'if judgment is made in it upon its individuals, then if the quantity of its individuals is specified in it...' but the text cuts off here. This would lead to the quantified propositions: universal (kulliyya), particular (juz'iyya), etc. The passage thus provides a foundational taxonomy for understanding how propositions vary depending on the scope of the subject term. This is crucial for syllogistic logic because the quantity of the subject affects the logical properties of the proposition (e.g., distribution).

Key Terms
شَخْصِيَّة
مُهْمَلَة
طَبِيعِيَّة
جُزْئِي
كُلِّي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of proposition has a universal subject considered with the condition of mental unity (al-wahda al-dhihniyya)?
A) Shakhsiyya
B) Muhmala
C) Tabi'iyya
D) Muhmala or Tabi'iyya

Key Takeaway
A proposition's classification depends on whether its subject is particular or universal, and for universal subjects, whether a condition of mental unity is attached.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the proposition type for each of the following: 'Zayd is a scholar', 'Man is a rational animal', 'Human is a species', 'Every human is mortal'. Explain your reasoning using the conditions from the passage.
?
In a debate, a mutakallim says 'The world is temporally originated'. Assuming 'world' is a universal term, is this proposition muhmala or tabi'iyya? What difference would it make for the argument?

Particular Subject Propositions: Personal and Specified
Classification of particular subject propositions into personal and specified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a personal (شخصية) and a specified (مخصوصة) proposition based on the subject type.

Core Idea
A particular subject yields either a personal proposition (one individual) or a specified proposition (a specific group).

In this passage, the author categorizes propositions by the subject's quantity. If the subject is particular (جزئي), the proposition falls into two types: personal (شخصية), referring to a specific individual like 'Zayd', and specified (مخصوصة), referring to a defined class like 'the Quraysh'. Understanding this distinction helps in analyzing how judgments are applied in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject as particular
Look at the subject term of the proposition. If it refers to one specific entity (e.g., 'Zayd') or a specific group (e.g., 'the people of Madina'), it is particular.
Particular here means not universal; it's about a definite individual or class.
2
Determine if the subject is a single individual or a class
If the subject is a single named individual, it is شخصية (personal). If it is a group or species (like 'the Quraysh'), it is مخصوصة (specified).
Some later scholars treat مخصوصة as a subtype of شخصية, but here they are distinct.
3
Classify the proposition
Based on the above, assign the correct label: 'Zayd is standing' is شخصية; 'The Quraysh are traders' is مخصوصة.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَوْضُوعُ إِنْ كَانَ جُزْيْبًا فَالْقَضِيَّةُ شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَمَخْصُوصَةٌ، وَإِنْ كَانَ كُلِّيًّا فَإِنْ حُكِمَ عَلَيْهِ بِلَا زِيَادَةِ شَرْطٍ فَمُهْمَلَةٌ عِنْدَ الْقَدَمَاءِ، وَإِنْ حُكِمَ عَلَيْهِ بِشَرْطِ الْوَحْدَةِ الدُّهْنِيَّةِ فَطَبِيعِيَّةٌ. وَإِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى أَفْرَادِهِ، فَإِنْ بَيْنَ فِيهَا كَمِّيَّةُ أَفْرَادِهِ فَمَحْصُورَةٌ وَمُسَوَّرَةٌ، وَمَا بِهِ الْبَيَانُ يُسَمَّى سُورًا، وَقَدْ يُذْكَرُ السُّورُ فِي جَانِبِ الْمَحْمُولِ فَتُسَمَّى الْقَضِيَّةُ مُنْحَرِفَةٌ. وَإِنْ لَمْ تُبَيَّنْ فَمُهْمَلَةٌ عِنْدَ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالُوا إِنَّهَا تُلَازِمُ الْجُزْيَّةَ.
The subject: if it is particular, then the proposition is personal and specified. If it is universal, then if judgment is made on it without adding a condition, it is indefinite according to the ancients. If judgment is made on it with the condition of mental unity, it is natural. If judgment is made in it on its individuals, then if the quantity of its individuals is specified in it, it is quantified and furnished with a quantifier. That by which the specification is made is called a quantifier. Sometimes the quantifier is mentioned in the side of the predicate, then the proposition is called deviated. If it is not specified, then it is indefinite according to the later scholars, and hence they said it entails particularity.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a classification of propositions based on the subject term. The author begins with the basic distinction: if the subject is a particular (جزئي), the proposition is either personal (شخصية) if it refers to a specific individual, or specified (مخصوصة) if it refers to a particular class. If the subject is universal (كلي), there are several cases. According to the ancients (القدماء), if we judge the universal without any added condition, the proposition is called indefinite (مهملة). If we judge it with the condition of mental unity (شرط الوحدة الذهنية), it is called natural (طبيعية). If we judge it about its individuals and the quantity of individuals is specified, it is called quantified (محصورة) and provided with a quantifier (مسورة). The tool of specification is called a quantifier (سور). Sometimes the quantifier is placed next to the predicate instead of the subject; such a proposition is called deviated (منحرفة). If the quantity is not specified, the later scholars (المتأخرون) still call it indefinite (مهملة), but they note that it entails particularity (الجزئية).

Key Terms
موضوع
قضية شخصية
قضية مخصوصة

Key Takeaway
A particular subject proposition is either personal (one individual) or specified (a specific class).

Universal Subject Propositions: Indefinite, Natural, Quantified, and Deviated
Classification of universal subject propositions: indefinite, natural, quantified, deviated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a universal subject proposition as indefinite (مهملة), natural (طبيعية), quantified (محصورة), or deviated (منحرفة), and explain the shift in the interpretation of indefinite among logicians.

Core Idea
Universal subject propositions are further qualified by the presence or absence of a quantifier and the condition of judgment.

When the subject is universal (كلي), the author outlines several possibilities depending on how the judgment is made: without condition (indefinite, with a shift in interpretation), with the condition of mental unity (natural), or on the individuals with a specified quantifier (quantified). If the quantifier is placed next to the predicate, the proposition is deviated. This classification is crucial for precise reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Determine if the subject is universal
If the subject term is universal (e.g., 'man', 'world'), check the conditions below.
2
Identify the presence of a quantifier
If the proposition has a word like 'every' (كل) or 'some' (بعض), it is quantified (محصورة). If not, check further.
If no quantifier, it is indefinite (مهملة) according to later scholars.
3
Check the condition of judgment
If the judgment is on the universal nature with the condition of mental unity (e.g., 'Man is rational' meaning by definition), it is natural (طبيعية).
Natural propositions are about the essence, not individuals.
4
Note the position of the quantifier
If the quantifier is placed after the predicate instead of before the subject, it is deviated (منحرفة). For example, 'The man is every animal' (rare but possible).
This is uncommon in normal usage.
5
Understand the shift in indefinite
Early logicians treated indefinite propositions as universal in scope. Later scholars treat them as particular because without a quantifier, they default to some individuals.
This affects many logical proofs in Islamic theology.

Source Text

Judgment in Quantified Propositions: Essence vs. Individuals
Author defends that judgment in quantified propositions is primarily on the universal nature, not on individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a judgment in a quantified proposition is on the universal essence or on the individuals, and defend the verifiers' position using the given analogy.

Core Idea
Judgment in quantified propositions targets the universal nature, not the individuals — individuals are known accidentally.

When we say 'Every human is mortal,' are we primarily talking about each human individually or about the nature of humanity itself? The verifiers (ahl al-tahqiq) argue that the judgment is on the universal essence (haqiqa) because that essence is what is present in the mind. Individuals are only known accidentally. This distinction is crucial for understanding how universal statements work in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis
Find the claim of the verifiers: judgment in a quantified proposition is primarily on the essence itself (nafs al-haqiqa). Note that 'essence' here means the universal nature.
Look at the first sentence: 'اعْلَمْ أَنَّ مَذْهَبَ أَهْلِ التَّحْقِيقِ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الْمَحْصُورَةِ عَلَى نَفْسِ الْحَقِيقَةِ.'
2
Understand the basis
Why do the verifiers hold this view? Because the essence is what is 'obtained in the mind' (الحَاصِلَةُ في الذَّهْنِ), while the individuals are known 'accidentally' (بالعرض). So judgment applies to the universal first.
The phrase 'بِالْعَرَضِ' is key: individuals are secondary in the mental representation.
3
Recognize the objection
An objector argues: If judgment is on the essence, then an affirmative proposition would require the existence of that essence as an essence, even when the essence is non-existent or negative. This seems problematic.
Look for 'رُبَّمَا يَتَرَأَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ لَاقْتَضَى الْإِيجَابُ وُجُودَ الْحَقِيقَةِ حَقِيقَةٌ.'
4
Analyze the response
The author clarifies that individuals are indeed judged essentially, even if known accidentally. He uses the analogy of 'general concept' (الوَضْعُ الْعَام) and 'specific referent' (الْمَوْضُوعُ لَهُ الْخَاص) to show that something known accidentally can still be the true referent of the judgment.
The response is in the middle: 'فَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْأَفْرَادَ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مَعْلُومَةٌ بِالوَجْهِ، لَكِنَّهَا مَحْكُومٌ عَلَيْهَا حَقِيقَةٌ.'
5
Reflect on the nuance
The author concludes that every judgment established for individuals is established for the nature as a whole. The question of whether it is primarily for the nature or for the individual is an additional concept beyond the essence, requiring further reflection.
Final sentence: 'وَأَمَّا أَنَّهُ لِمَاذَا أَوَّلًا وَبِالذَّاتِ: لِلطَّبِيعَةِ أَوْ لِلْفَرْدِ ؟ فَمَفْهُومٌ زَائِدٌ عَلَى الْحَقِيقَةِ، فَتَأَمَّلْ.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ مَذْهَبَ أَهْلِ التَّحْقِيقِ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الْمَحْصُورَةِ عَلَى نَفْسِ الْحَقِيقَةِ؛ لِأَنَّها هِيَ الْحَاصِلَةُ في الذَّهْنِ حَقِيقَةٌ، وَالْجُزْئِيَّاتُ مَعْلُومَةٌ بِالْعَرَضِ، فَلَيْسَتْ مَحْكُومًا عَلَيْهَا إِلَّا كَذَلِكَ. وَرُبَّمَا يَتَرَأَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ لَاقْتَضَى الْإِيجَابُ وُجُودَ الْحَقِيقَةِ حَقِيقَةٌ ، فَإِنَّ الْمُثْبَتَ لَهُ هُوَ الْمَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ حَقِيقَةٌ، مَعَ أَنَّهَا قَدْ تَكُونُ عَدَمِيَّةٌ ، بَلْ سَلْبِيَّةٌ. فَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْأَفْرَادَ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مَعْلُومَةٌ بِالوَجْهِ، لَكِنَّهَا مَحْكُومٌ عَلَيْهَا حَقِيقَةٌ ، أَلَا تَرَى إِلَى الْوَضْعِ الْعَام وَالْمَوْضُوعِ لَهُ الْخَاص ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَعْلُومَ بِالْوَجْهِ هُوَ الْمَوْضُوعُ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ . وَالْجَوَابُ: إِنَّ مُفَادَ الْإِيجَابِ مُطْلَقًا هُوَ النُّبُوتُ مُطْلَقًا، وَكُلُّ حُكْم ثَابِتٍ لِلْأَفْرَادِ ثَابِتٌ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ. وَأَمَّا أَنَّهُ لِمَاذَا أَوَّلًا وَبِالذَّاتِ: لِلطَّبِيعَةِ أَوْ لِلْفَرْدِ ؟ فَمَفْهُومٌ زَائِدٌ عَلَى الْحَقِيقَةِ، فَتَأَمَّلْ.
Know that the doctrine of the verifiers is that the judgment in a quantified proposition is on the essence itself; because it is the essence that is obtained in the mind, and the particulars are known accidentally, so they are not judged except in that way. Perhaps it appears that if it were so, affirmation would require the existence of the essence as an essence, for that to which something is affirmed is the subject of judgment essentially, even though it may be non-existent, rather negative. So the truth is that the individuals, although known accidentally, are judged essentially. Do you not see the general concept (الوضع العام) and the specific referent (الموضوع له الخاص)? For what is known accidentally is the referent essentially. And the answer: The import of affirmation absolutely is predication absolutely, and every judgment that is established for individuals is established for the nature in total. As for why it is primarily and essentially for the nature or for the individual, that is a concept additional to the essence, so reflect.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the nature of judgment in quantified propositions. The author begins by stating the position of the verifiers (أهل التحقيق): the judgment in a quantified proposition is essentially on the universal essence (الحقيقة) itself, because it is the universal that is present in the mind, while the individuals are known only accidentally. Therefore, the judgment applies to the universal first. A potential objection is raised: if that is the case, then an affirmative proposition would require the existence of the universal essence as an essence, even when the essence might be non-existent or negative. The author then clarifies that the truth is that individuals are indeed the subjects of judgment essentially, even if they are known accidentally. He uses the analogy of the general conceptual meaning (الوضع العام) and the specific referent (الموضوع له الخاص) to illustrate that something known accidentally can still be the true referent. Finally, he gives the answer: the meaning of affirmation is simply predication; any judgment that holds for individuals also holds for the nature as a whole. The question of whether it is primarily for the nature or the individual is an additional concept beyond the essence, and one should reflect on that.

The Four Quantified Propositions and Their Quantifiers
Author lists the four types of quantified propositions with their quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four quantified propositions and match them with their correct quantifiers in Arabic.

Core Idea
Four quantified propositions form the backbone of categorical logic; each has a distinct quantifier.

In this passage, the author enumerates the four types of quantified propositions (المحصورات): universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative. Each type is paired with its specific quantifier words (السور) that indicate whether the proposition is universal or particular, affirmative or negative. Understanding these quantifiers is essential for analyzing categorical statements in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types
Read the Arabic passage and list the four types: الموجبة الكلية, الموجبة الجزئية, السالبة الكلية, السالبة الجزئية.
These correspond to A, I, E, O in Aristotelian logic.
2
Extract the quantifiers for each type
For each type, note the quantifier words given: for universal affirmative: كل and لام الاستغراق; for particular affirmative: بعض and واحد; for universal negative: لا شيء, لا واحد, and the indefinite under negation; for particular negative: ليس كل, ليس بعض, بعض ليس.
Lam al-istighraq is a grammatical marker for universality.
3
Distinguish universal from particular
Universal quantifiers (كل, لا شيء) refer to all or none; particular quantifiers (بعض, ليس كل) refer to some or not all.
4
Distinguish affirmative from negative
Affirmative propositions have no negation sign; negative propositions include لا, ليس, etc.
5
Recognize language-specific quantifiers
The author notes that each language has its own quantifier expressions, but the logical types are universal.
In Arabic, lam al-istighraq is specific; other languages may use articles or other words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَحْصُورَاتُ أَرْبَعُ: الْمُوجَبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ ، وَسُورُها كُلُّ وَلَامُ الاسْتِغْرَاقِ، وَالْمُوجَبَةُ الْجُزْيَّيَّةُ، وَسُورُهَا بَعْضُ وَ وَاحِدٌ» ، وَالصَّالِبَةُ الْكُليه ، وَسُورُهَا الا شَيْءٍ وَلَا وَاحِدَ وَوُقُوعُ النَّكِرَةِ تَحْتَ النَّفْيِ، وَالصَّالِبَةُ الْجُزْئِيَّةُ، وَسُورُهَا لَيْسَ كُلُّ» ، وَلَيْسَ بَعْضُ» ، وَبَعْضُ لَيْسَ ، وَفي كُلِّ لُغَةٍ سُورٌ يَخُصُّهَا .
The quantified propositions are four: the universal affirmative, its quantifier is 'every' and the lām of all-inclusiveness; the particular affirmative, its quantifier is 'some' and 'one'; the universal negative, its quantifier is 'no thing' and 'not one' and the occurrence of the indefinite under negation; the particular negative, its quantifier is 'not every' and 'not some' and 'some is not'. And in every language there is a quantifier specific to it.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the four types of quantified propositions in Aristotelian logic. The author lists them as: (1) Universal affirmative (الموجبة الكلية) with quantifiers such as 'كل' (every) and the lām that indicates all-inclusiveness (e.g., 'جميع' or the definite article). (2) Particular affirmative (الموجبة الجزئية) with quantifiers like 'بعض' (some) and 'واحد' (one). (3) Universal negative (السالبة الكلية) with quantifiers like 'لا شيء' (no thing), 'لا واحد' (not one), and the occurrence of an indefinite term under negation (e.g., 'ليس أحد' meaning no one). (4) Particular negative (السالبة الجزئية) with quantifiers such as 'ليس كل' (not every), 'ليس بعض' (not some), and 'بعض ليس' (some not). He adds that each language has its own specific quantifier expressions, meaning that the logical types are universal but their linguistic expressions vary.

اللام الاستغراق

Key Takeaway

The Subject of Judgment in Quantified Propositions: Nature or Individuals?
Author argues whether judgment falls on the nature or the individuals in quantified propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the positions in the dialectical debate on the subject of judgment in quantified propositions and analyze the objection and response.

Core Idea
The debate on quantified propositions hinges on whether judgment attaches primarily to the universal nature or to its individuals.

This passage presents a classic dialectical exchange in Islamic logic: when we say 'Every human is mortal', are we judging the universal nature 'humanity' or each individual human? The people of verification (ahl al-tahqiq) argue for the nature, but a strong objection is raised. The author then resolves the issue by distinguishing between the primary and secondary attachment of judgment. Let's walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial position
Find where the author states the doctrine of ahl al-tahqiq. What is their claim about the subject of judgment in quantified propositions?
The passage begins with 'اعلم أن مذهب أهل التحقيق أن الحكم في المحصورة على نفس الحقيقة'.
2
Understand the objection
The passage says 'وربما يترأى...' What is the objection? Why would the initial view lead to a problem with negative or non-existent natures?
The objection argues that if judgment is on the nature, an affirmative would require the nature's existence, which fails for non-existent or negative subjects.
3
Find the author's response and resolution
The author says 'فالحق أن الأفراد...' How does he reconcile the two views? What analogy from usul al-fiqh does he use?
The author uses the distinction between the general concept (al-wad' al-'amm) and the specific referent (al-mawdu' lahu al-khass) from usul al-fiqh.
4
Analyze the final answer
The author gives a nuanced conclusion. What does he say about the import of affirmation? How does he distinguish between the judgment being 'for the nature' vs 'for the individual'?
The answer distinguishes between the absolute affirmation (al-thubut mutlaqan) and the question of 'why primarily' (li-madha awwalan wa bi al-dhat), which is an additional concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ مَذْهَبَ أَهْلِ التَّحْقِيقِ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الْمَحْصُورَةِ عَلَى نَفْسِ الْحَقِيقَةِ؛ لِأَنَّها هِيَ الْحَاصِلَةُ في الذَّهْنِ حَقِيقَةٌ، وَالْجُزْئِيَّاتُ مَعْلُومَةٌ بِالْعَرَضِ، فَلَيْسَتْ مَحْكُومًا عَلَيْهَا إِلَّا كَذَلِكَ. وَرُبَّمَا يَتَرَأَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ لَاقْتَضَى الْإِيجَابُ وُجُودَ الْحَقِيقَةِ حَقِيقَةٌ ، فَإِنَّ الْمُثْبَتَ لَهُ هُوَ الْمَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ حَقِيقَةٌ، مَعَ أَنَّهَا قَدْ تَكُونُ عَدَمِيَّةٌ ، بَلْ سَلْبِيَّةٌ. فَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْأَفْرَادَ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مَعْلُومَةٌ بِالوَجْهِ، لَكِنَّهَا مَحْكُومٌ عَلَيْهَا حَقِيقَةٌ ، أَلَا تَرَى إِلَى الْوَضْعِ الْعَام وَالْمَوْضُوعِ لَهُ الْخَاص ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَعْلُومَ بِالْوَجْهِ هُوَ الْمَوْضُوعُ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ . وَالْجَوَابُ: إِنَّ مُفَادَ الْإِيجَابِ مُطْلَقًا هُوَ النُّبُوتُ مُطْلَقًا، وَكُلُّ حُكْم ثَابِتٍ لِلْأَفْرَادِ ثَابِتٌ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ. وَأَمَّا أَنَّهُ لِمَاذَا أَوَّلًا وَبِالذَّاتِ: لِلطَّبِيعَةِ أَوْ لِلْفَرْدِ ؟ فَمَفْهُومٌ زَائِدٌ عَلَى الْحَقِيقَةِ، فَتَأَمَّلْ .
Know that the doctrine of the people of verification is that the judgment in quantified propositions is upon the nature itself, because it is what is obtained in the mind as a nature, and particulars are known accidentally, so they are not judged except as such. But it might be seen that if that were the case, the affirmative would require the existence of the nature as a nature, for that to which something is affirmed is the thing judged as a nature, while it may be non-existent, indeed negative. So the truth is that individuals, even if they are known in a certain way, are judged as a reality. Do you not see the general concept and the specific referent? For that which is known in a certain way is the referent in reality. The answer: The import of the affirmative in all cases is the absolute affirmation, and every judgment that is established for individuals is established for the nature in general. As for why it is primarily and essentially for the nature or for the individual? That is an additional concept beyond the reality, so reflect.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical discussion about whether the judgment in a quantified proposition is about the nature (the universal concept) or about the individual instances. The author begins by stating the position of the people of verification (أهل التحقيق): the judgment is on the nature itself because it is the nature that is present in the mind, while individuals are only known accidentally. An objection is raised: if that were true, then an affirmative proposition would always require the actual existence of the nature (as a concrete universal), which is problematic for negative or non-existent subjects. The author then answers that the truth is that individuals are judged in reality, though they are known indirectly. The answer clarifies that the import of any affirmative is absolute affirmation (of the predicate to the subject), and any judgment true of individuals is also true of the nature in general. The question of whether it is primarily for the nature or the individual is a secondary conceptual consideration. This is similar to the distinction in Usul al-Fiqh between the general concept (الوضع العام) and the specific referent (الموضوع له الخاص): what is known in a general way is nonetheless the actual referent.

Key Terms
الْحَقِيقَة (al-ḥaqīqa) - the universal nature or essence; here specifically the nature as a mental concept
الْأَفْرَاد (al-afrād) - the individual instances falling under a universal
الْوَضْع الْعَام وَالْمَوْضُوع لَهُ الْخَاص (al-waḍʿ al-ʿāmm wa al-mawḍūʿ lahu al-khāṣṣ) - the general concept and the specific referent; a technical distinction from uṣūl al-fiqh

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'doctrine of the people of verification' (مذهب أهل التحقيق) regarding the subject of judgment in quantified propositions?
A) The judgment is on the individual instances (الأفراد)
B) The judgment is on the nature itself (نفس الحقيقة)
C) The judgment is on the quantifier (السور)
D) The judgment is on the predicate (المحمول)

Key Takeaway
The dialectical resolution shows that while the initial position holds judgment on the nature, the objection forces the recognition that individuals are the actual subject, with the nature serving as a conceptual intermediary.

The Four Types of Quantified Propositions
The author defines quantified propositions and their quantifiers, then lists the four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four types of quantified propositions and their corresponding quantifiers in Arabic.

Core Idea
A quantified proposition's quantifier determines its logical force.

Quantified propositions are the building blocks of syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic. The author here defines what makes a proposition quantified (musawwara), explains the quantifier (sur), and then lists the four canonical types. Understanding which quantifier goes with which type is essential for correctly interpreting and manipulating propositions in arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier
Locate the word in the proposition that indicates quantity: كل (every), بعض (some), لا شيء (no thing), ليس كل (not every), etc. This word is the sur (quantifier).
The quantifier may appear on the subject or the predicate side (inverted proposition).
2
Determine the quantity
If the quantifier applies to all individuals of the subject, it is universal; if only to some, it is particular.
The universal quantifier 'كل' or 'لا' indicates the whole subject class.
3
Determine the quality
Check whether the proposition affirms or denies the predicate of the subject. Affirmative uses 'is/are'; negative includes a negation word like 'ليس' or 'لا'.
Negative quantifiers often embed negation: 'لا شيء' means 'no thing is'.
4
Combine into one of the four types
Match quantity and quality to the four types: universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative. Each has a defined set of quantifier words.
The author lists the quantifier words for each type; memorize them.
5
Handle muhmala and munharifa
If no quantifier appears, the proposition is muhmala (unquantified) and is treated as particular by later scholars. If the quantifier is on the predicate side, it is munharifa (inverted).
Inverted propositions still fit into the four types; the quantifier simply appears later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَمَحْصُورَةٌ وَمُسَوَّرَةٌ، وَمَا بِهِ الْبَيَانُ يُسَمَّى سُورًا، وَقَدْ يُذْكَرُ السُّورُ فِي جَانِبِ الْمَحْمُولِ فَتُسَمَّى الْقَضِيَّةُ مُنْحَرِفَةٌ. وَإِنْ لَمْ تُبَيَّنْ فَمُهْمَلَةٌ عِنْدَ الْمُتَأَخِّرِينَ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالُوا إِنَّهَا تُلَازِمُ الْجُزْيَّةَ. الْمَحْصُورَاتُ أَرْبَعُ: الْمُوجَبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ ، وَسُورُها كُلُّ وَلَامُ الاسْتِغْرَاقِ، وَالْمُوجَبَةُ الْجُزْيَّيَّةُ، وَسُورُهَا بَعْضُ وَ وَاحِدٌ» ، وَالصَّالِبَةُ الْكُليه ، وَسُورُهَا الا شَيْءٍ وَلَا وَاحِدَ وَوُقُوعُ النَّكِرَةِ تَحْتَ النَّفْيِ، وَالصَّالِبَةُ الْجُزْئِيَّةُ، وَسُورُهَا لَيْسَ كُلُّ» ، وَلَيْسَ بَعْضُ» ، وَبَعْضُ لَيْسَ ، وَفي كُلِّ لُغَةٍ سُورٌ يَخُصُّهَا .
So it is quantified and quantified, and that by which the explanation is made is called the quantifier. And sometimes the quantifier is mentioned on the side of the predicate, so the proposition is called inverted. And if it is not made clear, then it is unquantified according to the later scholars, and therefore they said that it entails particularity. The quantified propositions are four: the universal affirmative, and its quantifier is 'every' and the lam of universality; the particular affirmative, and its quantifier is 'some' and 'one'; the universal negative, and its quantifier is 'no' and 'not one' and the occurrence of the indefinite under negation; and the particular negative, and its quantifier is 'not every' and 'not some' and 'some are not'. And in every language there is a quantifier specific to it.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage defines quantified propositions (المحصورة) and their quantifiers (السور). The author first states that a proposition is called 'musawwara' (quantified) when it includes a quantifier (sur). If the quantifier is attached to the predicate, the proposition is called 'munharifa' (inverted). If no quantifier is specified, it is 'muhmala' (unquantified) and, according to later scholars, implies a particular judgment. Then the author enumerates the four types of quantified propositions: universal affirmative (quantifier: كل or لام الاستغراق), particular affirmative (quantifier: بعض or واحد), universal negative (quantifier: لا شيء or لا واحد, or the occurrence of an indefinite under negation), and particular negative (quantifier: ليس كل, ليس بعض, or بعض ليس). Finally, he notes that each language has its own specific quantifiers. In Islamic logic, these quantifiers are crucial for syllogistic reasoning. For example, 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden' is a universal affirmative; 'Some animals are not horses' is a particular negative.

Key Terms
مَحْصُورَة
سُور
مُهْمَلَة

Key Takeaway
The four types of quantified propositions—universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative—are distinguished by their quantifiers, which must be identified correctly for logical reasoning.

Distinguishing the Three Meanings of 'كل' (Kull)
Author distinguishes three uses of 'كل': universal, collective, and distributive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the universal, collective, and distributive meanings of 'كل' and identify the distributive meaning as the correct one for logical propositions.

Core Idea
The distributive 'كل' is the master key for universal quantification in Islamic logic.

In Arabic, the word 'كل' (every/all) is used in everyday language, but in logic it has three distinct meanings that must be carefully separated. The first is the universal (الكلي), referring to the concept itself; the second is the collective (المجموعي), referring to the aggregate; and the third is the distributive (الأفرادي), referring to each individual separately. This lesson will help you identify which meaning is intended in a proposition, because only the distributive meaning is suitable for syllogisms and scientific reasoning. Misunderstanding 'كل' can lead to fallacious arguments, especially in theological and legal contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject term
Look at the proposition and find the word 'كل' (kull). Examine what follows it to determine which meaning might be intended.
2
Test the universal meaning
Check if the proposition is about the universal concept itself (e.g., 'الإنسان نوع' – 'Human is a species'). If it talks about the essence, it is the universal meaning.
This yields a 'natural proposition' (قضية طبيعية).
3
Test the collective meaning
Check if the proposition refers to the entire group as a single entity (e.g., 'كل الناس لا يسعهم هذا البيت' – 'All people do not fit in this house'). If it is about the group acting as a whole, it is collective.
This yields a personal (شخصية) or unquantified (مهملة) proposition.
4
Test the distributive meaning
Check if the proposition applies to each individual separately (e.g., 'كل إنسان حيوان' – 'Every human is an animal'). If it holds for every individual taken one by one, it is distributive.
This is the meaning used in quantified propositions (محصورة).
5
Confirm for syllogisms
Remember: in logical arguments, the distributive meaning is required, as stated in the passage: 'والمعتبر في القياسات والعلوم هو المعنى الثالث'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْأَوَّلُ: أَنَّ الْكُلَّ يُطْلَقُ بِمَعْنَى الْكُلّيّ، مِثْلَ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَوْعٌ، وَبِمَعْنَى الْكُلِّ الْمَجْمُوعِيٌّ ، نَحْوُ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ لَا يَسَعُهُ هَذِهِ الدَّارُ»، وَبِمَعْنَى الْكُلِّ الْأَقْرَادِيِّ. وَالْفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ الثَّلَاثَةِ ظَاهِرُ وَالْمُعْتَبَرُ فِي الْقِيَاسَاتِ وَالْعُلُوم هُوَ الْمَعْنَى الثَّالِثُ، وَالْمُشْتَمِلُ عَلَيْهِ هِيَ الْمَحْصُورَةُ، وَأَمَّا الْأُولى فَطَبيعيَّةٌ، وَالثَّانِيَةُ شَخْصِيَّةٌ، أَوْ مُهْمَلَةٌ، وَالَّتِي اشْتَمَلَتْ عَلَى الْبَعْضِ الْمَجْمُوعِي مُهْمَلَةٌ.
The first: That 'kull' is used in the sense of the universal, like 'Every human is a species'; and in the sense of the collective, like 'Every human does not fit in this house'; and in the sense of the distributive individual. The difference between the three concepts is apparent. And what is considered in syllogisms and sciences is the third meaning, and that which contains it is the quantified proposition. As for the first, it is a natural proposition; and the second is a personal or unquantified proposition; and the one that contains the collective part is unquantified.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is the first of two investigations (مبحث). It clarifies the three distinct meanings of the word 'كل' (every/all). The first meaning is the universal (الكلي), as in the proposition 'Every human is a species' – here 'كل' refers to the universal concept itself. The second meaning is the collective (المجموعي), as in 'Every human does not fit in this house' – this refers to the aggregate of humans. The third meaning is the distributive individual (الأفرادي), which refers to each individual taken separately. The author states that only the third (distributive) meaning is relevant for syllogisms and sciences; the first yields a 'natural' proposition (القضية الطبيعية) and the second yields a personal or unquantified (مهملة) proposition. For example, in Fiqh, the rule 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is understood distributively: each individual intoxicant is forbidden, not the concept of intoxicant as a whole.

Key Terms
كُلّ
قَضِيَّة طَبِيعِيَّة
مُهْمَلَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The collective (المجموعي)
C) The distributive individual (الأفرادي)
D) All three are equally used

Key Takeaway
Only the distributive meaning of 'كل' is valid for universal quantification in logical arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Symbol ج: What Does the Subject Term Really Refer To?
Clarifies the reference of ج and contrasts al-Farabi vs Ibn Sina on criterion for truth of subject term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify what the symbol ج represents in a proposition and distinguish al-Farabi's potential truth view from Ibn Sina's actual truth view.

Core Idea
The subject term references individuals, not essence; al-Farabi allows possibility; Ibn Sina demands actuality.

In Islamic logic, the letter ج is often used as a placeholder for the subject term. But what does it actually denote? This passage clarifies that ج refers to the individuals of which the term is true, not the essence or the description. The author then highlights a crucial disagreement between al-Farabi and Ibn Sina over whether those individuals must actually exist or merely be possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject symbol
Locate ج in the Arabic. Note that it is a symbol representing the subject term in logical propositional schemata.
2
Understand what ج does NOT represent
The author explicitly states that ج does not denote the essence (what its reality is) nor what is described by it (the attribute).
3
Recognize what ج DOES represent
It refers to the individuals (al-afrād) to which the term applies. These can be real (e.g., particular persons) or mental (e.g., the genus animal).
The conventional usage is real individuals.
4
Compare the two criteria
Al-Farabi allows the term to apply bi-l-imkān (by possibility) even to non-existent individuals. Ibn Sina insists on bi-l-fiʿl (by actuality) either in external existence or mental assumption.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider 'Every knower is living'. Al-Farabi would accept this even if no knower exists potentially; Ibn Sina requires actual knowers in reality or mental assumption.
This distinction matters in Kalam discussions about divine attributes and existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أَنَّ (ج) لَا نَعْنِي بِهَا مَا حَقِيقَتُهُ (جَ)، وَلَا مَا هُوَ مَوْصُوفٌ بِهِ، بَلْ أَعَمَّ مِنْهُمَا، وَهُوَ مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ (ج) مِنَ الْأَفْرَادِ. وَتِلْكَ الْأَفْرَادُ قَدْ تَكُونُ حَقِيقِيَّةٌ، كَالْأَفْرَادِ الشَّخْصِيَّةِ أَوِ النَّوْعِيَّةِ، وَقَدْ تَكُونُ اعْتِبَارِيَّةٌ، كَالْحَيَوَانِ الْجِنْسِ ، فَإِنَّهُ أَخَصُّ مِنْ مُطْلَقِ الْحَيَوَانِ إِلَّا أَنَّ الْمُتَعَارَفَ فِي الاعْتِبَارِ هُوَ القِسْمُ الْأَوَّلُ. ثُمَّ الْفَارَابِيُّ اعْتَبَرَ صِدْقَ عِنْوَانِ الْمَوْضُوعِ عَلَى ذَاتِهِ بِالْإِمْكَانِ ; حَتَّى يَدْخُلَ في كُلِّ أَسْوَدَ الرُّومِيُّ، وَالشَّيْخُ لَمَّا وَجَدَهُ مُخَالِفًا لِلْعُرْفِ وَاللُّغَةِ اعْتَبَرَ صِدْقَهُ عَلَيْهَا بِالْفِعْلِ فِي الْوُجُودِ الْخَارِجِيِّ، أَوْ فِي الْفَرْضِ الذَّهْنِيِّ بِمَعْنَى أَنَّ الْعَقْلَ يَعْتَبِرُ انْصافَهَا بِأَنَّ وُجُودَهَا بِالْفِعْلِ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ يَكُونُ كَذَا، سَوَاءٌ وُجِدَ
The second: That by (ج) we do not mean what its essence is (ج), nor what is described by it, but rather what is more general than both, namely what the term (ج) applies to from among the individuals. And those individuals may be real, like personal or specific individuals, or they may be mental, like the genus 'animal', for it is more specific than absolute animal, except that the conventional in consideration is the first division. Then al-Farabi considered the truth of the title of the subject upon its essence by possibility, so that even 'the Roman black' would enter under every black. And the Sheikh (Ibn Sina), when he found it contrary to custom and language, considered its truth upon them by actuality in external existence, or in mental assumption, meaning that the mind considers them to be actually existing in reality in such a way, whether it exists or not.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is the second investigation. It clarifies what the symbolic letter ج (serving as the subject term in logical schemata) actually represents. The author explains that ج does not mean the essence of ج itself, nor that which is described by it, but rather it refers to the individuals of which the term ج is true. These individuals may be real (like particular humans or species) or mental (like the genus 'animal', which is a mental concept). The conventional usage is the first (real individuals). Then the author notes a disagreement: Al-Farabi considered that the subject term can apply to its referent even if only potentially (bi-l-imkān), so that even a description like 'Roman black' (probably an example of a black Roman person) would fall under the term 'black'. In contrast, Ibn Sina (referred to as 'the Sheikh') required actual truth (bi-l-fiʿl) in external existence or in mental assumption, i.e., the mind must consider them actually existing in reality, even if they do not exist externally. This debate impacts the interpretation of universal quantifiers in propositions about non-existent subjects, relevant in Kalam discussions about the attributes of God.

Key Terms
ج
الإنكان
الشيخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina (the Sheikh), the subject term (ج) applies to individuals based on:
A) Potential truth (بالإمكان)
B) Actual truth in existence or mental assumption (بالفعل)
C) Whatever the mind conceives
D) The essence of the term

Key Takeaway
The subject term in a proposition refers to the individuals of which it is true, and the debate between al-Farabi and Ibn Sina hinges on whether those individuals need to actually exist or only be possible.

Placeholder Letters in Categorical Propositions
Author explains the use of ج and ب as placeholder letters in categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to interpret the placeholder letters ج and ب in categorical propositions and explain why logicians abstract from specific matter.

Core Idea
ج and ب are symbolic placeholders to ensure logical rules apply universally.

Logicians often express categorical propositions using the letters ج and ب, such as 'كل ج ب' for the universal affirmative. This practice is similar to the disjointed letters of the Qur'an. In this lesson, we will examine why abstraction is necessary and how to correctly understand these placeholders.

Guided Path
1
Identify the letters and their roles
In the expression 'كل ج ب', the letter ج stands for the subject term and ب stands for the predicate term. They are not actual terms but abstract placeholders.
This is analogous to using variables in mathematics.
2
Understand the reason for abstraction
Logicians strip the proposition of specific content (مادة) to avoid the illusion that the rule applies only to particular instances (دفعًا لتوهم الانحصار).
If they used 'كل إنسان حيوان', one might think the rule only holds for humans and animals.
3
Connect to the universal affirmative
The universal affirmative proposition is schematically 'كل ج ب', meaning 'Every subject is predicate'. This form captures the logical structure without specifying what subject and predicate are.
The same pattern applies to other categorical propositions like 'ليس شيء من ج ب'.
4
Note the four matters (أمور أربعة)
The passage mentions that there are four matters to verify: the subject, the predicate, the copula, and the quantifier. These are the components of a categorical proposition.
The copula is implicit in the Arabic expression.
5
Appreciate the analogy with Quranic disjointed letters
Just as the disjointed letters (حروف مقطعة) are pronounced as compound names (e.g., 'جيم'), the logicians pronounce the placeholder letters as 'al-jim' and 'al-ba''.
This reinforces that the letters themselves are not words but symbolic names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن سلم العلوم
بـ (ب) ، وَالْأَشْهَرُ التَّلَفْظُ بِهِمَا اسْمًا مُرَكَّبًا ، كَالْمُقَطَّعَاتِ الْقُرْآنِيَّةِ ، وَيَدُلُّ عَلَى ذَلِكَ أَنَّهُمْ يُعَبِّرُونَ بِالْجِيمِ وَالْجِيمِيَّةِ وَالْبَاءِ وَالْبَائِيَّةِ. وَبِالْجُمْلَةِ إِذَا أَرَادُوا التَّعْبِيرَ عَنِ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْكُلْيَةِ مَثَلًا لِإِجْرَاءِ الْأَحْكَامِ عَلَيْهَا جَرَّدُوهَا عَنِ الْمَوَادِّ؛ دَفْعًا لِتَوَهُم الانْحِصَارِ. وَقَالُوا: كُلُّ جَ بَ، فَهَهُنَا أَرْبَعَةُ أُمُورٍ، فَلْتُحَقِّقُ أَحْكَامَهَا فِي مَبَاحِثُ .
Text of Sullam al-Ulum: With (ب), and the more common is to pronounce them as a compound name, like the disjointed letters of the Quran. And evidence for that is that they express with al-jim and al-jimiyya and al-ba' and al-ba'iyya. In short, when they want to express the universal affirmative, for example, to apply rulings to it, they strip it of its matter to avoid the illusion of confinement. And they said: Every ج is ب. So here are four matters — let their rulings be verified in discussions.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage explains the conventional use of the placeholder letters ج (jim) and ب (ba') to represent the subject and predicate terms in categorical propositions, particularly the universal affirmative (كل ج ب). The author notes that the practice is similar to the disjointed letters (muqatta'at) in the Quran, which are pronounced as compound names. This abstraction from specific content (mawad) is done to prevent the misconception that the logical rule applies only to particular instances. The passage sets the stage for discussing the four matters (subject, predicate, copula, and quantifier) that follow.

Key Takeaway
Placeholder letters like ج and ب allow logicians to discuss logical form without being tied to specific content, ensuring universal applicability of rules.

Understanding Predication: Primary vs. Common
Author defines predication and its primary/common division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between primary predication (al-haml al-awwal) and common conventional predication (al-haml al-sha'i' al-muta'arif) and identify which type is used in a given proposition from an Islamic science text.

Core Idea
Primary predication identifies concepts; common predication connects existence.

Predication (al-haml) is the core logical relation between subject and predicate in a proposition. The author defines it as the union of two different concepts in one aspect of intellection according to another aspect of existence. Then he distinguishes two fundamental types: primary predication, where subject and predicate are identical in concept, and common conventional predication, where mere union in existence suffices. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing definitions and arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Locate how the author defines 'predication' (al-haml). Note the key phrase 'union of two different things in some aspect of intellection according to another aspect of existence'.
This definition emphasizes both conceptual and existential aspects.
2
Distinguish the two main types
Find the two types mentioned: primary predication (al-haml al-awwal) where the subject itself is the predicate (conceptual identity), and common conventional predication (al-haml al-sha'i' al-muta'arif) where mere existential union is enough.
Memorize the Arabic terms: الحمل الأولى and الحمل الشائع المتعارف.
3
Analyze example statements
For a statement like 'Human is a rational animal', determine if it is primary (yes, because 'human' and 'rational animal' are conceptually identical). For 'Zaid is standing', it is common (concepts differ but existentially united).
Common predication is the standard in the sciences.
4
Apply the self-predication rule
Understand the author's claim that every concept is predicated of itself by primary predication, so self-negation is impossible. Then note that some concepts (like 'concept' and 'universal possible') are also self-predicated commonly, while others (like 'particular') are not.
This has implications for contradiction conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّالِثُ الْحَمْلُ اتَّحَادُ الْمُتَغَابِرَيْنِ في نَحْوٍ مِنَ التَّعَقُلِ بِحَسَبِ نَحْوِ آخَرَ مِنَ الْوُجُودِ، اتِّحَادًا بِالذَّاتِ، أَوْ بِالْعَرَضِ. وَهُوَ إِمَّا أَنْ يُعْنَى بِهِ أَنَّ الْمَوْضُوعَ بِعَيْنِهِ الْمَحْمُولُ، فَيُسَمَّى الْحَمْلَ الْأَوْلَى ، وَقَدْ يَكُونَ نَظْرِيَّا أَيْضًا، أَوْ يُقْتَصَرُ فِيهِ عَلَى مُجَرَّدِ الإِنْحَادِ فِي الْوُجُودِ ، فَيُسَمَّى الْحَمْلَ الشَّائِعَ الْمُتَعَارَفَ، وَهُوَ الْمُعْتَبرُ فِي الْعُلُومِ. وَيَنْقَسِمُ بِحَسَبِ كَوْنِ الْمَحْمُولِ ذَاتِيَّا ، أَوْ عَرَضِيًّا لَهُ إِلَى الْحَمْلِ بِالذَّاتِ أَوْ بِالْعَرَضِ ، وَقَدْ يَنْقَسِمُ بِأَنَّ نِسْبَةَ الْمَحْمُولِ إِلَى الْمَوْضُوعِ إِمَّا بِوَاسِطَةِ «في» «ذُو» أَوْ «لَهُ» ، فَهُوَ الْحَمْلُ بِالِاشْتِقَاقِ ، أَوْ بِلَا وَاسِطَةٍ، وَهُوَ الْمَقُولُ بِعَلَى، فَهُوَ الْحَمْلُ بِالْمُوَاطَاةِ ، وَالْأَشْبَهُ أَنَّ إِطْلَاقِ الْحَمْلِ عَلَيْهِمَا بِالاشْتِرَاكِ اللَّفْظِيُّ. اعْلَمْ أَنَّ كُلَّ مَنْهُومٍ يُحْمَلُ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ بِالْحَمْلِ الْأَوْلَى، وَمِنْ هُنَاكَ تَسْمَعُ أَنَّ سَلْبَ الشَّيْءِ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ مُحَالٌ . ثُمَّ طَائِفَةٌ مِنَ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ يُحْمَلُ عَلَى أَنْفُسِهَا حَمْلاً شَائِعًا أَيْضًا ، كَالْمَفْهُومِ وَالْمُمْكِنِ الْعَام وَنَحْوِهِمَا، وَطَائِفَةٌ لاَ يُحْمَلُ عَلَى أَنْفُسِهَا ذَلِكَ الْحَمْلَ، بَلْ يُحْمَلُ عَلَيْهَا نَقَائِضُهَا ، كَالْجُزْنِي وَاللأَمَفهُومِ. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا اعْتُبِرَ فِي التَّنَاقُضِ اتَّحَادُ نَحْوِ الْحَمْلِ فَوْقَ الْوَحْدَاتِ الثَّمَانِ الذَّائِعَاتِ .
The third (topic): Predication is the union of two different things in some aspect of intellection according to another aspect of existence, either essential union or accidental. And it is either meant by it that the subject itself is the predicate, so it is called primary predication, and it may also be theoretical, or it is restricted to mere union in existence, so it is called common conventional predication, and it is the one considered in the sciences. And it is divided according to whether the predicate is essential or accidental to it into essential predication or accidental predication, and it may be divided by that the relation of the predicate to the subject is either by mediation of 'fi', 'dhu', or 'lahu', so it is predication by derivation, or without mediation, and it is what is said by 'ala, so it is predication by homonymy. And the more likely is that the application of predication to both is by verbal equivocation. Know that every concept is predicated of itself by primary predication, and hence you hear that negating a thing of itself is impossible. Then a group of concepts are predicated of themselves by common predication as well, like concept and the universal possible and the like, and a group are not predicated of themselves by that predication, but rather their opposites are predicated of them, like the particular and the non-concept. And from this, it is considered in contradiction the union of the mode of predication in addition to the eight well-known unities.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage defines al-haml (predication) and explains its types. It begins by defining haml as the union of two different things in one aspect of intellection according to another aspect of existence, either essentially or accidentally. Then it distinguishes two main types: al-haml al-awwal (primary predication) where the subject is the very same as the predicate, and al-haml al-sha'i' al-muta'arif (common conventional predication) where it is mere union in existence. The latter is what is used in the sciences. The passage then further divides predication according to whether the predicate is essential (dhati) or accidental ('aradi), and according to whether the relation is mediated by prepositions like 'fi' (in), 'dhu' (possessing), or 'lahu' (for it) resulting in derived predication (bil-ishtiqaq), or without mediation, which is said bi-'ala (by 'ala) and is called predication by homonymy (bi'l-muwata'a). The author notes that applying the term 'predication' to both these types is by verbal equivocation (ishtirak lafzi). Then he notes that every concept is predicated of itself by primary predication, so self-negation is impossible. Some concepts, like 'concept' and 'universal possible', are also predicated of themselves by common predication, while others, like 'particular' and 'non-concept', are not; rather their opposites are predicated of them. This shows that in contradiction, we must add unity of the mode of predication to the eight well-known unities.

Key Terms
الْحَمْلُ
الْحَمْلُ الْأَوْلَى
الْحَمْلُ الشَّائِعُ الْمُتَعَارَفُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of predication is considered in the sciences?
A) Primary predication (al-haml al-awwal)
B) Common conventional predication (al-haml al-sha'i' al-muta'arif)
C) Essential predication (al-haml bi al-dhat)
D) Derived predication (al-haml bi al-ishtiqaq)

Key Takeaway
Predication has two fundamental types: primary (conceptual identity) and common (existential union); the latter is the standard in scientific discourse.
