The Famous Doubt on Predication and Its Resolution
Author presents the famous objection to predication and resolves it by distinguishing aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the famous doubt about the impossibility of predication and explain its resolution using the distinction between aspects.

Core Idea
Predication is possible because unity in existence does not require identity of concepts.

The passage presents a well-known objection: predication seems impossible because if subject and predicate are identical in concept, there is no real predication; if they are different, they cannot be united. The resolution lies in recognizing that difference in one aspect (concept) does not preclude union in another (existence). This lesson unpacks the doubt and its solution, essential for understanding how propositions in Islamic sciences like kalam and usul al-fiqh function.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dilemma
The doubt states that the concept of the subject is either identical to the concept of the predicate or different. If identical, predication is trivial (no distinction). If different, they cannot be united in one entity.
2
Locate the resolution
The author resolves by saying 'difference in one aspect does not prevent union in another aspect.' This means two things can be different in concept (mafhum) but the same in existence (wujud).
3
Understand the condition for predicate
The predicate must be taken 'unconditionally' (la bi-shart shay’), so that it can have two aspects: its own concept and the fact that it is predicated of something.
4
Apply to conventional predication
The author defines 'conventional predication' (al-haml al-muta'arif) as the truth of the predicate's concept of the subject, whether essentially (dhati) or accidentally (wasf), abstracted with or without addition. The example of 'five is even' shows that even if five has the property of being 'pair' in some sense, the predicate 'zawj' does not truthfully apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٌ مَشْهُورٌ ، وَهُوَ : أَنَّ الْحَمْلَ مُحَالٌ ؛ لِأَنَّ مَنْهُومَ (جَ) عَيْنُ مَنْهُومٍ (بَ) أَوْ غَيْرُهُ، وَالْعَيْنِيَّةُ تُنَافِي الْمُغَايَرَةَ ، وَالْمُغَايَرَةُ تُنَافِي الاِتِّحَادَ. وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّ التَّغَائِرَ مِنْ وَجْهِ لاَ يُنَافِي الاِتِّحَادَ مِنْ وَجْهِ آخَرَ . نَعَمْ، يَجِبُ أَنْ يُؤخَذَ الْمَحْمُولُ لا بِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ، حَتَّى يُتَصَوَّرَ فِيهِ أَمْرَانِ. وَالْمُعْتَبرُ في الْحَمْلِ الْمُتَعَارَفِ صِدْقُ مَفْهُوم الْمَحْمُولِ عَلَى الْمَوْضُوع، بأَنْ يَكُونَ ذَاتِيَّا لَهُ، أَوْ وَصْفًا قَائِمًا بِهِ، أَوْ مُنتَزَعًا عَنْهُ بِلاَ إِضَافَةٍ أَوْ بِإِضَافَةٍ، فتبوتُ زَوْجِيَّةِ الْخَمْسَةِ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ صِدْقَ قَوْلِنَا: «الْخَمْسَةُ زَوْجٌ» .
And here is a famous doubt, and it is that predication is impossible; because the concept of (ج) is either the same concept as (ب) or other than it. Sameness contradicts difference, and difference contradicts union. Its resolution is that difference in one aspect does not prevent union in another aspect. Yes, the predicate must be taken unconditionally, so that two things can be conceived in it. And what is considered in conventional predication is the truth of the concept of the predicate of the subject, such that it is essential to it, or a description subsisting in it, or abstracted from it without addition or with addition. Thus the establishment of the pairness of five does not entail the truth of our statement 'five is even'.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents a famous doubt (shakk mashhur) about predication. The objection is that predication is impossible because any predication involves two terms, say (ج) and (ب). If they are identical in concept, then there is no difference, so predication is trivial. If they are different, then there is no union, so predication is false. The resolution is that difference in one aspect does not prevent union in another; for example, the subject and predicate can be the same in existence but different in concept. The predicate must be taken unconditionally (la bi-shart shay') so that two aspects can be conceived. Then the author states what is considered in conventional predication: the truth of the predicate concept of the subject, either essentially or as a description, etc. An example is given: the fact that five has the property of being 'pair' (zawjiyya) in some abstract sense does not mean we can truly say 'five is even', because the predicate 'zawj' (even) does not truthfully apply to five.

Key Terms
الشك المشهور
الحمل المتعارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the resolution of the famous doubt, why is predication possible?
A) The subject and predicate are always identical in concept.
B) Difference in one aspect does not prevent union in another aspect.
C) The subject and predicate must be taken unconditionally.
D) Predication is only possible if the predicate is essential.

Key Takeaway
The famous doubt that predication is impossible is resolved by recognizing that difference in aspect (concept) does not preclude union in existence, thereby saving all propositions in Islamic sciences.

Classification of Propositions and the Status of the Impossible
Classification of propositions and nature of the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition into one of five types based on the existence mode of its subject, and explain why the impossible cannot be conceived.

Core Idea
The existence mode of the subject determines the proposition type; the impossible has no mental form.

In this passage, the author expands on the classification of propositions in Islamic logic. He distinguishes propositions based on whether the subject is mental or external, verified or assumed. He also clarifies a key epistemological point: the impossible, as such, cannot be conceived in the mind. This lesson will guide you through these distinctions and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The author states that the affirmation of something for something in a situation is a branch of the actuality of what is affirmed for it. Understand that every proposition involves a subject and a predicate, and the existence of the subject in a certain mode is foundational.
2
Trace the fivefold classification
Extract the five types: mental (dhihniyya) where the subject is a verified mental entity, mental-real (haqiqiyya dhihniyya) where assumed mental, external (kharjiyya) where verified external, external-real (haqiqiyya kharjiyya) where assumed external, and real absolutely (haqiqiyya 'ala al-itlaq) which applies regardless of actual existence like geometric propositions.
Each type corresponds to how the subject exists in the judgment.
3
Examine negative propositions
Negative propositions do not require existence of the subject; they can be true even if the subject does not exist. However, the concept of the subject must still exist in the mind at the time of judgment.
This distinguishes negatives from affirmatives.
4
Analyze the impossible
The impossible (muhal) as such has no form in the intellect, so it is non-existent both mentally and externally. Therefore, a mentally existing concept cannot be judged as impossible in itself, only with respect to its instances.
This implies that the subject of a proposition must be conceivable.
5
Apply the distinction
Given a proposition, determine its type based on the subject's existence condition. Also test whether a concept like 'round square' can be the subject of a proposition according to the author.
This step solidifies the theory through practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ فِيهِ نِكَاتُ الْأُولَى : تُبُوتُ شَيْءٍ لِشَيْءٍ فِي ظَرْفٍ فَرْعُ فِعْلِيَّةِ مَا ثَبَتَ لَهُ، وَمُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِتُبُوتِه في ذَلِكَ الطَّرْفِ، فَمِنْهُ مَا ثَبَتَ لِأَمْرِ ذِهْنِي مُحَفَّقٍ، وَهِيَ الذَّهْنِيَّةُ، أَوْ مُقَدَّرٍ، وَهِيَ الْحَقِيقِيَّةُ الذَّهْنِيَّةُ ، أَوْ أَمْرٍ خَارِجِيٌّ مُحَفَّقٍ، وَهِيَ الْخَارِجِيَّةُ ، أَوْ مُقَدَّرٍ ، وَهِيَ الْحَقِيقِيَّةُ الْخَارِجِيَّةُ ، أَوْ مُطْلَقًا، وَهِيَ الْحَقِيقِيَّةُ عَلَى الْإِطْلَاقِ، كَالْقَضَايَا الْهَنْدَسِيَّةِ وَالْحِسَابِيَّةِ. وَأَمَّا السَّالِبَةُ فَلَا تَسْتَدْعِي وُجُودَ الْمَوْضُوعِ ، بَلْ قَدْ يَصْدُقُ بِانْتِفَائِهِ. نَعَمْ، تَحَقُّقُ مَنْهُومُ السَّالِبَةِ فِي الذِّهْنِ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا بِوُجُودِهِ فِيهِ حَالَ الْحُكْمِ فَقَطْ . الثَّانِيَةُ : الْمُحَالُ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ مُحَالٌ لَيْسَ لَهُ صُورَةٌ فِي الْعَقْلِ، فَهُوَ مَعْدُومٌ ذِهْنَا وَخَارِجًا، وَمِنْ هَهُنَا تَبَيَّنَ أَنَّ كُلَّ مَوْجُودٍ في الذِّهْنِ حَقِيقَةٌ مَوْجُودٌ في نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، فَلَا يُحْكُمُ عَلَيْهِ إِيجَابًا بِالامْتِنَاعِ ، أَوْ سَلْبًا بِالْوُجُودِ مَثَلًا، إِلَّا عَلَى أَمْرٍ كُلى، إذَا كَانَ مِنَ الْمُمْكِنَاتِ تَصَوُّرُهُ. وَكُلُّ مَحْكُومٍ عَلَيْهِ بِالتَّحْقِيقِ هِيَ الطَّبِيعَةُ الْمُتَصَوَّرَةُ، وَكُلُّ مُتَصَوَّرٍ ثَابِتٌ ، فَلَا يَصحُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ هُوَ بِالامْتِنَاعِ وَمَا يَحْذُو حَذْوَهُ. نَعَمْ، إِذَا لُوحِظَ بِاعْتِبَارِ جَمِيعِ مَوَارِدِ تَحَقُّقِهِ أَوْ بَعْضِهَا يَصِحُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ بِالِامْتِنَاعِ مَثَلًا،
The fourth (topic): In it are notes of the first: The affirmation of something for something in a situation is a branch of the actuality of what is affirmed for it, and it necessitates its affirmation in that situation. Among it is what is affirmed for a verified mental thing, and that is the mental (proposition), or an assumed one, and that is the mental real (proposition), or a verified external thing, and that is the external (proposition), or an assumed one, and that is the external real (proposition), or absolutely, and that is the real (proposition) absolutely, like the geometrical and arithmetical propositions. As for the negative, it does not require existence of the subject; rather it may be true by its negation. Yes, the realization of the concept of the negative in the mind only occurs by its existence in it at the time of judgment. The second (note): The impossible, insofar as it is impossible, has no form in the intellect, so it is non-existent in mind and external. From this it is clear that everything existing in the mind is a reality existing in the mind's estimation (nafs al-amr), so it is not judged upon it positively as impossible or negatively as existence, for example, except upon a universal matter, when its conception is from the possibilities. And everything judged upon as realized is the conceived nature, and every conceived thing is fixed, so it is not correct to judge it in itself as impossible and what follows suit. Yes, if it is considered with respect to all its instances of realization or some of them, it is correct to judge it as impossible, for example.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is the fourth topic, containing two notes (nikat). The first note discusses the division of propositions according to the scope of the subject's existence: mental (dhihniyya) where the subject is a verified mental entity, mental-real (haqiqiyya dhihniyya) where the subject is assumed mental, external (kharjiyya) where the subject is verified external, external-real (haqiqiyya kharjiyya) where the subject is assumed external, and real absolutely (haqiqiyya 'ala al-itlaq) which applies to geometrical and arithmetical propositions. Negative propositions do not require existence of the subject; they can be true even when the subject does not exist, though the concept must exist in the mind during judgment. The second note discusses the impossible (muhal): as such, it has no image in the mind, so it is non-existent both mentally and externally. Therefore, anything that exists in the mind is a reality in the mind's estimation (nafs al-amr). Consequently, we cannot judge a mentally existent concept as impossible or as non-existent in itself. However, if we consider it with respect to all or some of its instances, we can judge it as impossible.

Key Terms
القضية الذهنية
القضية الحقيقية
المحال
القضية الحقيقية على الإطلاق
السَّالِبَة

C) Real proposition absolutely
D) Negative proposition

Key Takeaway
Propositions are classified by the existence mode of their subject; the impossible cannot be conceived and thus cannot be the subject of a proposition.

Conditions and Types of Predication
Author explains the conditions for correct predication and distinguishes essential from accidental predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given predication is valid in the common sense by checking its type: essential, subsisting, or abstracted.

Core Idea
Predication requires a balance of identity and difference between subject and predicate.

In logic, predication (al-haml) is not merely any true attribution. The author explains that for a predication to be valid in common usage, the predicate must relate to the subject in one of three specific ways: being essential, subsisting, or abstracted. Understanding these conditions is crucial for evaluating definitions and arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the identity-difference condition
The author notes that identity and difference are contradictory in a simple sense, but predication requires unity in one aspect and difference in another. The predicate must be taken without condition so that two aspects can be conceived.
2
Identify the three valid types of predication
List: dhati (essential) where predicate is part of subject's essence; wasf qa'im (subsisting attribute) where predicate is an accident inhering in the subject; muntaza' (abstracted) where predicate is derived from the subject with or without relation.
3
Apply the types to the example of 'five is even'
Evenness is not essential to five, not a subsisting attribute, and not abstracted from five in the required sense. Therefore, despite being true, it is not a valid common predication.
The establishment of evenness for five does not entail 'Five is even' as a valid common predication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَ) أَوْ غَيْرُهُ، وَالْعَيْنِيَّةُ تُنَافِي الْمُغَايَرَةَ ، وَالْمُغَايَرَةُ تُنَافِي الاِتِّحَادَ. وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّ التَّغَائِرَ مِنْ وَجْهِ لاَ يُنَافِي الاِتِّحَادَ مِنْ وَجْهِ آخَرَ . نَعَمْ، يَجِبُ أَنْ يُؤخَذَ الْمَحْمُولُ لا بِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ، حَتَّى يُتَصَوَّرَ فِيهِ أَمْرَانِ. وَالْمُعْتَبرُ في الْحَمْلِ الْمُتَعَارَفِ صِدْقُ مَفْهُوم الْمَحْمُولِ عَلَى الْمَوْضُوع، بأَنْ يَكُونَ ذَاتِيَّا لَهُ، أَوْ وَصْفًا قَائِمًا بِهِ، أَوْ مُنتَزَعًا عَنْهُ بِلاَ إِضَافَةٍ أَوْ بِإِضَافَةٍ، فتبوتُ زَوْجِيَّةِ الْخَمْسَةِ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ صِدْقَ قَوْلِنَا: «الْخَمْسَةُ زَوْجٌ» .
Or other, and identity contradicts difference, and difference contradicts unity. Its solution: that difference in one aspect does not contradict unity in another aspect. Yes, the predicate must be taken unconditioned, so that two things can be conceived in it. And what is considered in common predication is that the concept of the predicate is true of the subject, either being essential to it, or an attribute subsisting in it, or abstracted from it without relation or with relation. Thus the establishment of evenness for five does not entail the truth of our saying 'Five is even'.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for valid predication (al-haml). It first addresses the issue of identity and difference: predication requires that the subject and predicate are not identical in every respect, but they can be unified in one aspect while differing in another. The predicate must be taken without any condition so that two aspects (subject and predicate) can be conceived. Then it lists the three types of common predication: essential (dhati), where the predicate is part of the subject's essence; subsisting attribute (wasf qa'im), where the predicate is an accident inhering in the subject; and abstracted (muntaza'), where the predicate is derived from the subject with or without a relation. The example of 'five' and 'evenness' illustrates that evenness is a property of five but not a valid predicate in common predication because it is neither essential, subsisting, nor abstracted in the required sense.

المُغَايَرَة
ذَاتِي
وَصْف قَائِم

Checkpoint

A) The predicate is essential (dhati) to the subject.
B) The predicate is an attribute subsisting in the subject.
C) The predicate is abstracted from the subject with or without relation.
D) The predicate is a mere concept that happens to apply to the subject but is not related in these ways.

Key Takeaway
Valid common predication requires the predicate to be essential, subsisting, or abstracted from the subject, not merely true in some other sense.

Five Types of Propositions by Container
Author classifies propositions by container of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition into one of five types based on the container and reality of its subject.

Core Idea
Propositions are classified by whether their subject exists in mind or world, real or estimated.

This lesson unpacks the author's classification of propositions according to the container (zarf) of the subject's existence. Understanding these five types helps us see how different kinds of judgments relate to reality. We will explore each type step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two axes: container and reality
The container is either the mind (dhihn) or the external world (kharij). Within each, the subject can be real (muhaqqaq, actually existing) or estimated (muqaddar, hypothetical).
These two axes yield four combinations, plus a fifth type for absolute truths.
2
Identify the five types from the passage
Mental (dhihniyya): real mental entity, e.g., an idea currently in your mind. True mental: estimated mental, e.g., a concept that could be imagined. External: real external object, e.g., this book. True external: estimated external, e.g., a unicorn considered as if real. Absolute true: propositions true regardless of existence, like mathematical truths.
The Arabic terms are: الذهنية, الحقيقية الذهنية, الخارجية, الحقيقية الخارجية, الحقيقية على الإطلاق.
3
Note the special status of absolute true propositions
The absolute true type (haqiqiyya 'ala al-itlaq) covers universal truths like 'the triangle's angles sum to two right angles'. These hold for all conceivable instances, whether or not any exist.
Geometrical and arithmetical propositions are given as examples.
4
Practice classifying example propositions
Given a proposition, determine its type: ask, does the subject exist in the mind or outside? Is it real or estimated? If it holds universally, it may be absolute true.
For instance, 'Angels are created' has an external real subject (angels are real) → external proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ فِيهِ نِكَاتُ الْأُولَى : تُبُوتُ شَيْءٍ لِشَيْءٍ فِي ظَرْفٍ فَرْعُ فِعْلِيَّةِ مَا ثَبَتَ لَهُ، وَمُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِتُبُوتِه في ذَلِكَ الطَّرْفِ، فَمِنْهُ مَا ثَبَتَ لِأَمْرِ ذِهْنِي مُحَفَّقٍ، وَهِيَ الذَّهْنِيَّةُ، أَوْ مُقَدَّرٍ، وَهِيَ الْحَقِيقِيَّةُ الذَّهْنِيَّةُ ، أَوْ أَمْرٍ خَارِجِيٍّ مُحَفَّقٍ، وَهِيَ الْخَارِجِيَّةُ ، أَوْ مُقَدَّرٍ ، وَهِيَ الْحَقِيقِيَّةُ الْخَارِجِيَّةُ ، أَوْ مُطْلَقًا، وَهِيَ الْحَقِيقِيَّةُ عَلَى الْإِطْلَاقِ، كَالْقَضَايَا الْهَنْدَسِيَّةِ وَالْحِسَابِيَّةِ. وَأَمَّا السَّالِبَةُ فَلَا تَسْتَدْعِي وُجُودَ الْمَوْضُوعِ ، بَلْ قَدْ يَصْدُقُ بِانْتِفَائِهِ. نَعَمْ، تَحَقُّقُ مَنْهُومُ السَّالِبَةِ فِي الذِّهْنِ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا بِوُجُودِهِ فِيهِ حَالَ الْحُكْمِ فَقَطْ .
The fourth [point] contains the subtleties of the first: The establishment of something for something in a container is a branch of the actuality of that for which it is established, and entails its establishment in that container. Among them, what is established for a real mental matter is mental, or for an estimated matter is true mental, or for a real external matter is external, or for an estimated matter is true external, or absolutely is true absolutely, like geometrical and arithmetical propositions. As for the negative, it does not require the existence of the subject; rather, it may be true by its non-existence. Yes, the concept of the negative is only realized in the mind by its existence in it at the time of judgment only.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions based on the container (zarf) of the subject's existence. The container is either the mind or the external world, and can be real or estimated. Thus we have five types: mental (dhihniyya) for real mental objects, true mental (haqiqiyya dhihniyya) for estimated mental objects, external (kharijiyya) for real external objects, true external (haqiqiyya kharijiyya) for estimated external objects, and absolute true (haqiqiyya 'ala al-itlaq) for universals like geometrical truths. The passage then clarifies that negative propositions do not require the subject to exist; they can be true simply because the subject does not exist. However, the concept of the negative itself must exist in the mind at the time of judgment.

Negative Propositions Do Not Require Subject Existence
Author clarifies truth conditions for negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why negative propositions can be true even when the subject does not exist, and identify the minimal existence requirement for the concept.

Core Idea
Negative propositions can be true even if the subject never existed.

The author adds an important clarification about negative propositions (saliba). Unlike affirmative propositions, negatives do not require the subject to actually exist. This has implications for how we evaluate the truth of statements like 'There is no mountain of gold'.

Guided Path
1
Contrast negative with affirmative propositions
Affirmative propositions require the subject to exist in some container (mind or external) for the proposition to be true. Negatives, however, can be true simply because the subject does not exist.
The passage says: 'السَّالِبَةُ فَلَا تَسْتَدْعِي وُجُودَ الْمَوْضُوعِ'
2
Understand truth by non-existence
A negative proposition can be true if the subject is absent. For example, 'No phoenix is seen' is true because phoenixes do not exist. The truth condition does not depend on any instance of the subject.
This is a key difference from affirmatives, which need at least one instance of the subject.
3
Recognize the minimal mental existence of the concept
Although the subject need not exist, the concept of the subject must exist in the mind at the time of judgment. Otherwise, you couldn't even formulate the thought.
The passage ends: 'تَحَقُّقُ مَنْهُومُ السَّالِبَةِ فِي الذِّهْنِ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا بِوُجُودِهِ فِيهِ حَالَ الْحُكْمِ فَقَطْ'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The fourth [point] contains the subtleties of the first: The establishment of something for something in a container is a branch of the actuality of that for which it is established, and entails its establishment in that container. Among them, what is established for a real mental matter is mental, or for an estimated matter is true mental, or for a real external matter is external, or for an estimated matter is true external, or absolutely is true absolutely, like geometrical and arithmetical propositions. As for the negative, it does not require the existence of the subject; rather, it may be true by its non-existence. Yes, the concept of the negative is only realized in the mind by its existence in it at the time of judgment only.

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions based on the container (zarf) of the subject's existence. The container is either the mind or the external world, and can be real or estimated. Thus we have five types: mental (dhihniyya) for real mental objects, true mental (haqiqiyya dhihniyya) for estimated mental objects, external (kharijiyya) for real external objects, true external (haqiqiyya kharijiyya) for estimated external objects, and absolute true (haqiqiyya 'ala al-itlaq) for universals like geometrical truths. The passage then clarifies that negative propositions do not require the subject to exist; they can be true simply because the subject does not exist. However, the concept of the negative itself must exist in the mind at the time of judgment.

Key Terms
السَّالِبَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is true according to the passage?
A) Negative propositions require the subject to exist externally.
B) Negative propositions can be true by the non-existence of the subject.
C) Negative propositions require the subject to exist in the mind forever.
D) Negative propositions are not true propositions.

Key Takeaway
Negative propositions are unique: they can be true without the subject's existence, though the concept must exist in the mind at the moment of judgment.

?

Impossibility as an Attribute of the Nature
Author argues impossibility is an attribute of the nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate why impossibility is an attribute of the nature (tabi'a) and critically evaluate three flawed alternative views that insist on judging individuals.

Core Idea
Impossibility inheres in concepts, not imaginary individuals.

This lesson addresses a classic puzzle: how can we truthfully say 'The partner of the Creator is impossible' when no such partner exists? The solution is to attribute impossibility to the nature of the concept itself. The author then refutes three misguided alternatives that try to force judgment onto individuals.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the Core Claim
Identify that the author states: 'Impossibility is fixed for the nature (tabi'a).' This means that impossibility is a property of the essence of a concept, not of any external individuals.
2
See How This Explains Truth
Propositions like 'The partner of the Creator is impossible' are true because impossibility is an essential attribute of the concept 'partner of the Creator', which has no external instances. The truth is independent of actual existence.
3
Recognize the Problem with Alternative Views
The author critiques three positions that all assume judgment is on individuals. Each leads to logical difficulties.
4
Analyze Each Refutation
First view: saying the judgments are negative – the author calls this arbitrary. Second view: saying they are affirmative requiring only conception – the author says it contradicts the obvious. Third view: saying they are about hypothetical individuals – the author points out this makes the attribute (impossibility) subsist more than the subject.
5
Accept the Author's Conclusion
Only the nature-based account is sound and resolves the paradox without falling into arbitrariness, absurdity, or self-defeating consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَالِامْتِنَاعُ ثَابِتُ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ، وَذَلِكَ صَادِقٌ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوَارِدِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ لَا إِشْكَالَ بِالْقَضَايَا الَّتِي مَحْمُولَاتُهَا مُنَافِيَةٌ لِلْوُجُودِ، نَحْو: «شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي مُمْتَنِع»، وَاجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ مُحَالٌ، وَالْمَجْهُولُ الْمُطْلَقُ يَمْتَنِعُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ ، وَالْمَعْدُومُ الْمُطْلَقُ يُقَابِلُ الْمَوْجُودَ الْمُطْلَقَ . وَأَمَّا الَّذينَ قَالُوا إِنَّ الْحُكْمَ عَلَى الْأَفْرَادِ حَقِيقَةٌ فَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ: إِنَّهَا سَوَالِبُ، وَلَا رَيْبَ أَنَّهُ تَحَكُمْ. وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ إِنَّهَا مُوجَبَاتٌ، لَا تَقْتَضِي إِلَّا تَصَوُّرَ الْمَحْكُومِ عَلَيْهِ حَالَ الْحُكْمِ ، كَمَا فِي السَّوَالِبِ مِنْ غَيْرِ فَرْقٍ، وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يُصَادِمُ الْبَدَاهَةَ . وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ: إِنَّ الْحُكْمَ عَلَى الْأَفْرَادِ الْفَرْضِيَّةِ الْمُقَدَّرَةِ الْوُجُودِ، كَأَنَّهُ قَالَ مَثَلاً: مَا يُتَصَوَّرُ بِعِنْوَانِ شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي ، وَيُفْرَضُ صِدْقُهُ عَلَيْهِ مُمْتَنع في نَفْس الأمر. وَلاَ يَذْهَبُ عَلَيْكَ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ يَكُونَ ثُبُوتُ الصِّفَةِ أَزْيَدَ مِنْ تُبُوتِ الْمَوْصُوفِ ؛ فَإِنَّ الاِمْتِنَاعَ مُتَحَقِّقُ وَثَابِتُ في نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، بِخِلَافِ الْأَفْرَادِ، فَتَدَبَّرُ.
Impossibility is an attribute of the nature [essence], and that is true by the absence of [external] instances. Then there is no problem with propositions whose predicates are incompatible with existence, such as 'The partner of the Creator is impossible', and 'The conjunction of contradictories is impossible', and 'The absolutely unknown cannot be judged', and 'The absolutely non-existent is opposite to the absolutely existent.' As for those who said that judgment on individuals is real: among them are those who said that they are negative [judgments]—and there is no doubt that this is arbitrary. Among them are those who said that they are affirmative [judgments] that require only the conception of the subject at the time of judgment, just as in the negative [judgments] without any difference—and it is not hidden that this contradicts the obvious. Among them are those who said that judgment is on hypothetical individuals assumed to exist, as if he said, for example: 'What is conceived under the description of partner of the Creator, and its truth is assumed, is impossible in itself.' And it should not escape you that it necessarily follows that the subsistence of the attribute is additional to the subsistence of the subject; for impossibility is real and fixed in itself, unlike the individuals. So reflect.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of impossibility in logical propositions. The author argues that impossibility is a fixed attribute of the concept (tabi'a) itself, not of any external individuals. Therefore, propositions like 'The partner of the Creator is impossible' are true because there are no individuals that satisfy the subject, not because we are judging non-existent individuals. The author then critiques three positions of those who insist that judgment in such cases is actually on individuals: (1) that they are negative judgments—arbitrary; (2) that they are affirmative judgments requiring only conception of the subject—contradicts common sense; (3) that they are on hypothetical individuals assumed to exist—this leads to the awkward conclusion that the attribute (impossibility) subsists more than the subject itself. The passage ends with a call to reflect. All examples come from kalām or logic: 'partner of the Creator', 'conjunction of contradictories', etc.

الطبيعة
الموارد

A) Because there are no individuals that are partners of the Creator.
B) Because impossibility is an attribute of the concept 'partner of the Creator' itself.
C) Because we are judging hypothetical individuals.
D) Because the judgment is negative.

Key Takeaway
Impossibility is a fixed attribute of the nature of a concept, not of its instances, and attempts to place judgment on individuals lead to arbitrary, paradoxical, or self-defeating positions.

Understanding the Negative of Predicate (Sāliyat al-Mahmūl)
Author introduces the innovation of negative of predicate and its distinction from simple negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the negative of predicate from a simple negative and explain the later logicians' reasoning about its existential import.

Core Idea
The negative of predicate shifts negation from the copula into the predicate.

Later logicians proposed a new type of proposition called 'negative of predicate' where the negation is attached to the predicate itself. This subtle shift has significant implications for whether the subject must exist. In this lesson, we will unpack the distinction and see what the later logicians aimed to achieve by this innovation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the innovation
The later logicians coined the term 'negative of predicate' (sāliyat al-mahmūl) as a new proposition type distinct from the simple negative (sāliba). They introduced this distinction to handle propositions about non-existent subjects.
2
Contrast with the simple negative
In a simple negative, the negation operates on the copula: 'Zayd is not seeing'. In the negative of predicate, the negation is made part of the predicate: 'Zayd is not-seeing' (i.e., the predicate itself is negative). The passage says: 'in the simple negative, both terms are conceived and a negation is judged; in the negative of predicate, one returns and predicates that negation on the subject.'
3
Capture the rule about existence
The later logicians claimed that the affirmative form of the negative of predicate does not require the subject to exist. In their view, 'Zayd is not-seeing' can be true even if Zayd does not exist, whereas the simple negative 'Zayd is not seeing' would require Zayd's existence for its truth.
The passage says: 'they ruled that the truth of affirmation in it does not require existence, just as the negation does not require it; rather, the negation requires it like the obtained affirmation.' This is nuanced: the positive form lacks existential import, but its contradictory (negation of the negative of predicate) does require existence.
4
Consider the implication
Think about why later logicians might want a proposition that can be true without subject existence. For example, propositions about impossible subjects (like 'the partner of God is non-existent') need to be meaningful and true without implying actual existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعَةُ: إِنَّ الْمُتَأَخْرِينَ اخْتَرَعُوا قَضِيَّةٌ سَمَّوْهَا سَالِيَةَ الْمَحْمُولِ، وَفَرَّقُوا بِأَنْ في السَّالِبَةِ يُتَصَوَّرُ الطَّرَفَانِ وَيُحْكَمُ بِالسَّلْبِ، وَفِي السَّالِبَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ يُرْجَعُ وَيُحْمَلُ ذَلِكَ السَّلْبُ عَلَى الْمَوْضُوعِ . وَحَكَمُوا بِأَنَّ صِدْقَ الْإِيجَابِ فِيهَا لَا يَسْتَدْعِي الْوُجُودَ ، كَالسَّلْب لَا يَسْتَدْعِيهِ، بَلْ السَّلْبُ يَسْتَدْعِيهِ كَالْإِيجَاب الْمُحَصَّلِ، وَقَرِيحَتُكَ حَاكِمَةٌ بِأَنَّ الرَّبْطَ الْإِيجَابِيَّ مُطْلَقًا يَقْتَضِي الْوُجُودَ وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالَ: الْحَقُّ إِنَّهَا قَضِيَّةٌ ذِهْنِيَّةٌ ، وَجَمِيعُ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ مَوْجُودَةٌ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ تَحْقِيقا أَوْ تَقْدِيرًا ، فَبَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ السَّالِبَةِ تَلَازُمٌ بِحَسَبِ الصِّدْقِ. وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ.
The fourth [point]: The later thinkers invented a proposition which they called the negative of predicate (sāliyat al-mahmūl). They distinguished by saying that in the [simple] negative, both terms are conceived and a negation is judged, while in the negative of predicate, one returns and predicates that negation on the subject. They ruled that the truth of affirmation in it does not require existence, just as the negation does not require it; rather, the negation requires it like the obtained affirmation. Your innate intelligence judges that the affirmative connector absolutely requires existence. Hence it was said: The truth is that it is a mental proposition, and all conceptual meanings are existent in themselves, either actually or hypothetically. So between it and the simple negative there is a necessary connection regarding truth. [But] there is something to be said about that.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage critiques an innovation by later logicians: the proposition called 'negative of predicate' (sāliyat al-mahmūl). In a simple negative (sāliba), we conceive two terms and deny the predicate of the subject. In the negative of predicate, the negation itself is made part of the predicate: we say 'Zayd is not-seeing' instead of 'Zayd is not seeing'. The later logicians claimed that this proposition, though affirmative in form, does not require the subject to exist. The author argues that common sense rejects this, because any affirmative connector implies existence. The correct view, he says, is that such a proposition is a mental proposition (qaḍiyya dhihniyya), and all conceptual meanings exist in themselves either actually or hypothetically, so that it and the simple negative are necessarily equivalent in truth. However, the author hints that this solution is not fully satisfactory ('fīhi mā fīhi'). The critique uses an example implicitly: 'Zayd is non-seeing' vs 'Zayd is not seeing'.

Key Terms
سَالِيَةَ الْمَحْمُولِ
السَّالِبَة
قَضِيَّةٌ ذِهْنِيَّةٌ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later logicians, the negative of predicate in its affirmative form (e.g., 'Zayd is not-seeing') does NOT require which of the following?
A) The truth of the proposition
B) The existence of the subject
C) The predicate being a real attribute
D) The negation being part of the predicate

Key Takeaway
The negative of predicate is an artificial logical type that shifts negation into the predicate, and the later logicians used it to avoid existential import for affirmative statements about non-existent subjects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the simple negative 'The Messenger is not a liar' and convert it into a negative of predicate proposition. Then explain whether the later logicians would say the truth of that proposition requires the Messenger's existence.

Critiquing the Negative of Predicate: Existence and Mental Propositions
Author critiques the later logicians' view on existential import and offers the mental proposition as a solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's critique of the negative of predicate and explain his alternative: the mental proposition.

Core Idea
The author argues that any affirmative connector implies existence, so the negative of predicate is best understood as a mental proposition.

The later logicians' innovation seems convenient for dealing with non-existent subjects, but the author finds it contrary to common sense. He insists that every affirmative judgement carries existential import and offers a different solution: the negative of predicate is merely a mental proposition. Let's examine the critique closely.

Guided Path
1
State the later logicians' claim
The later logicians claimed that the affirmative form of the negative of predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is not-seeing') does not require the subject to exist. This allows them to make true statements about non-existent or impossible subjects without implying actual existence.
2
Identify the author's objection
The author rejects this claim by appealing to innate intelligence (qarīḥa): 'Your innate intelligence judges that the affirmative connector absolutely requires existence.' In other words, any proposition with an affirmative copula, regardless of the predicate, entails the existence of the subject.
3
Understand the proposed solution
The author proposes that the negative of predicate is actually a mental proposition (qaḍiyya dhihniyya). Its terms are mentally conceived and exist in the mind either actually or hypothetically. This avoids the problem of existential import because mental existence is assumed.
4
Examine the equivalence claim
The author also states that between the negative of predicate and the simple negative there is a necessary connection regarding truth (talāzum bi-ḥasab al-ṣidq). This means that whenever the negative of predicate is true, the corresponding simple negative is also true, and vice versa. However, he hints that this claim is not fully unproblematic ('fīhi mā fīhi').
5
Consider the author's reservation
The final phrase 'fīhi mā fīhi' indicates the author is not completely satisfied with the mental proposition solution. Perhaps because it relies on a special notion of mental existence that not all logicians accept.

Ma'dula and Muhassala: Propositions with and without Negation in Terms
Author defines ma'dula and muhassala propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given proposition as ma'dula or muhassala and identify whether the negation is in the subject, predicate, or both.

Core Idea
Ma'dula propositions embed negation inside a term; muhassala keep it separate.

This passage introduces a crucial distinction in categorical propositions: whether the negation particle is part of the subject or predicate (ma'dula) or stands alone (muhassala). Understanding this distinction is essential for analyzing logical form in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where subtle differences in wording can change the meaning of a claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negation particle
Find the word 'lā' or 'ghayr' in the proposition. Determine whether it is attached to a term (e.g., 'lā-insān') or separate (e.g., 'laysa').
2
Classify as ma'dula or muhassala
If the negation is part of a term, it is ma'dula. Otherwise, it is muhassala. If ma'dula, note whether it is in the subject, predicate, or both.
3
Distinguish meaning vs. wording
Some terms like 'a'mā' (blind) imply negation in meaning but not in wording. The author notes that 'Zayd is blind' is ma'dula in meaning but muhassala in wording.
This affects how we classify implicit versus explicit negation.
4
Understand connector placement
In simple negatives, the connector (rābiṭ) comes after the negation. In affirmative negatives (e.g., 'Zayd is non-seeing'), there are two connectors with negation between them.
Connector placement helps identify the logical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ قَدْ يُجْعَلُ حَرْفُ السَّلْبِ جُزْءٌ في طَرَفٍ، فَسُمِّيَتْ مَعْدُولَةً، وَهِيَ مَعْدُولَةُ الْمَوْضُوعِ ، أَوْ مَعْدُولَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ ، أَوْ مَعْدُولَةُ الطَّرَفَيْنِ. وَإِلا فَمُحَصَّلَةٌ . وَازَيْدٌ أَعْمَى مَعْدُولَةٌ مَعْقُولَةٌ، وَمُحَصَّلَةٌ مَلْفُوظَةٌ. وَقَدْ يُخَصُ اسْمُ الْمُوجَبَةِ بِالْمُحَصَّلَةِ، وَالصَّالِبَةُ بِالْبَسِيطَةِ، وَهِيَ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْمَعْدُولَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ، وَيَتَأَخَّرُ فِيهَا الرَّابِطُ عَنْ لَفْظِ السَّلْبِ لَفْظًا، أَوْ تَقْدِيرًا. وَفِي الْمُوجَبَةِ الصَّالِبَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ رَابِطَانِ ، وَالسَّلْبُ مُتَوَسِّطٌ بَيْنَهُما .
Then the negation particle may be made a part of a term; it is then called ma'dūla (modalized). It is either modalized in the subject, or modalized in the predicate, or modalized in both terms. Otherwise it is muhassala (simple). 'Zayd is blind' is a modalized in meaning, and a simple in wording. The term 'affirmative' may be restricted to the simple, and 'negative' to the simple [negative]. And it (the simple negative) is more general than the affirmative modalized in the predicate. In it (the simple negative), the connector comes after the word of negation, either literally or by estimation. In the affirmative negative of predicate, there are two connectors, and the negation is intermediate between them.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions based on whether the negation particle is part of a term. If the negation is part of the subject or predicate, the proposition is called ma'dūla (modalized). Examples: 'al-Insānu lā-nāṭiq' (Man is non-speaking) — here 'lā' is part of the predicate. The opposite is muhassala (simple), where the negation is separate. The author notes that 'Zayd is blind' is ma'dūla in meaning (since 'blind' contains negation implicitly) but muhassala in wording (no explicit negation particle). The term 'affirmative' is sometimes reserved for simple affirmative, and 'negative' for simple negative. The simple negative is more general than the affirmative modalized in the predicate. In the simple negative, the connector (i.e., the copula) comes after the negation word. In the affirmative negative of predicate (i.e., a proposition like 'Zayd is non-seeing'), there are two connectors: the affirmative connector and the negation embedded in the predicate. The author uses the example 'Zayd is blind' (Zayd a'mā) — 'blind' means 'non-seeing', but the word itself is not overtly negative.

Key Terms
مَعْدُولَة (ma'dūla)
مُحَصَّلَة (muhassala)
الرَّابِط (al-rābiṭ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a ma'dūla proposition?
A) Zayd is not writing.
B) Zayd is non-seeing (i.e., blind).
C) Some animal is not man.
D) Every man is an animal.

Key Takeaway
A proposition is ma'dula when negation is part of a term; otherwise it is muhassala, and the distinction affects logical analysis especially in implicit negation cases.

The Three Modal Materials: Necessary, Impossible, Possible
Author classifies relations into three modal materials.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three modal materials (mawādd) and match each to the corresponding modality (jiha) in a proposition.

Core Idea
Every relation is necessarily, impossible, or possible — these are the materials that determine truth.

In this passage, the author introduces the foundational classification of modal propositions. Understanding these three modal materials is essential for evaluating the truth of statements that involve necessity, impossibility, or possibility. We will see how the 'material' of a relation is distinguished from the linguistic 'modality' that expresses it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three materials
The passage states: 'Every relation in itself is either necessary, or impossible, or possible.' List these three: necessary (wajiba), impossible (mumtani'a), possible (mumkina).
Note the OCR typo: the original has 'مُمْتَبِعَة' which should be 'مُمْتَنِعَة' (impossible).
2
Distinguish material from modality
The materials are the actual modal qualities of the relation. The modality is the linguistic marker that indicates the material. For example, the word 'necessarily' indicates necessary material.
3
Learn the Arabic terms
Memorize the three terms: واجبة (wajiba) for necessary, ممتنعة (mumtani'a) for impossible, ممكنة (mumkina) for possible.
These are the 'mawādd' (materials).
4
Apply with an Islamic example
Take the proposition 'God is necessarily existent.' The material is necessary, and the modality is the word 'necessarily'. Another: 'A partner with God is impossible' – material impossible, modality 'impossible'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كُلُّ نِسْبَةٍ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ إِمَّا وَاجِبَةٌ، أَوْ مُمْتَبِعَةٌ، أَوْ مُمْكِنَةٌ، وَتِلْكَ الْكَيْفِيَّاتُ الْمَوَادُّ، وَالدَّالُ عَلَيْهَا الْجِهَةُ ، وَمَا اشْتَمَلَتْ عَلَيْهَا يُسَمَّى مُوَجَّهَةٌ وَرُبَاعِيَّةٌ ، بَسِيطَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ حَقِيقَتُهَا إِيجَابًا فَقَطْ أَوْ سَلْبًا فَقَطْ ، وَمُرَكَّبَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ مُلْتَيْمَةٌ مِنْهُمَا ، وَالْعِبْرَةُ فى التّسْمِيَةِ لِلْجُزْءِ الْأَوَّلِ ، وَإِلَّا فَمُطْلَقَةٌ وَمُهْمَلَةٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ الْجِهَةُ ، وَهِيَ إِنْ وَافَقَتِ الْمَادَّةَ صَدَقَتِ الْقَضِيَّةُ ، وَإِلَّا كَذَبَتْ . وَالتَّحْقِيقُ أَنَّ الْمَوَادَّ الْحِكَمِيَّةَ هِيَ الْجِهَاتُ الْمَنْطِقِيَّةُ، وَقِيلَ: إِنَّهَا غَيْرُهَا ،
Every relation in itself is either necessary, or impossible, or possible. Those qualities are the materials (mawādd), and that which indicates them is the modality (jiha). What includes them is called 'directed' (muwajjaha) and quadrilateral. It is simple (basīṭa) if its reality is only affirmation or only negation; and composite (murakkaba) if it is composed of both. The consideration in naming is for the first part. Otherwise it is absolute (muṭlaqa) and neglected (muhmala) in terms of modality. And if it agrees with the material, the proposition is true; otherwise it is false. The verification is that the philosophical materials are the logical modalities, and it is said that they are other than them.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines modal propositions (al-qadaya al-muwajjaha). The author states that every relation (nisba) in itself (i.e., considered in the mind) is either necessary (wajiba), impossible (mumtani'a), or possible (mumkina). These three are called the 'materials' (mawadd). The marker that indicates which one is the 'modality' (jiha). A proposition that includes a modality is called 'directed' (muwajjaha). Some also call them 'quadrilateral' (rubā'iyya) because they have four parts: subject, predicate, copula, and modality. If the proposition's reality is only affirmation or only negation (e.g., 'Z is necessarily B' or 'Z is necessarily not B'), it is simple (basīṭa). If it is composed of both affirmation and negation (e.g., 'Z is possibly B' which implies the possibility of not-B), it is composite (murakkaba). The naming is based on the first part: if the first part is affirmative, it is called affirmative even if the second part is negative. Otherwise (if no modality), it is called 'absolute' (muṭlaqa) and 'neglected' (muhmala) regarding modality. The truth of a modal proposition depends on whether the modality matches the material: if it agrees, the proposition is true; otherwise false. For example, if the material is necessary, the proposition 'It is possible that A is B' is false. Finally, the author touches on a debate: whether the philosophical materials (the actual necessity, impossibility, possibility in things) are identical to the logical modalities (the terms used in propositions). The author inclines to the view that they are the same, but notes that some disagree.

Key Terms
الْمَوَاد
الْجِهَة
وَاجِبَة
مُمْتَنِعَة
مُمْكِنَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the three modal materials?
A) Necessary
B) Impossible
C) Possible
D) Contingent

Key Takeaway
Every relation has one of three modal materials: necessary, impossible, or possible, and the modality is the linguistic marker that indicates it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct one modal proposition for each of the three materials using Islamic examples. For necessary: 'Allah is necessarily existent.' For impossible: 'A partner with Allah is impossible.' For possible: 'Man is possibly disobedient.' Ensure the modality (word like 'necessarily', 'impossible', 'possibly') matches the material.

Simple and Composite Modal Propositions and Their Truth Conditions
Author classifies modal propositions into simple and composite and states truth condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a modal proposition as simple or composite and determine its truth based on agreement with the material.

Core Idea
A modal proposition is true only when its modality matches the actual material of the relation.

Having learned the three modal materials, the author now explains how modal propositions themselves are structured. Some are simple (involving only affirmation or only negation) and some are composite (involving both). The truth of any modal proposition depends on whether its modality agrees with the actual material. This lesson will help you assess the validity of modal claims in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define simple modal proposition
A modal proposition is simple (basīṭa) if its modality involves only affirmation (e.g., 'Z is necessarily B') or only negation (e.g., 'Z is necessarily not B'). It does not include both possibilities.
The term 'simple' here refers to the modal aspect, not the subject-predicate structure.
2
Define composite modal proposition
A modal proposition is composite (murakkaba) if its modality involves both affirmation and negation. For example, 'Z is possibly B' implies both that Z is B and that Z is not B are possible. The modality is composite because it includes both sides.
Composite modal propositions are essentially about contingency.
3
Understand the naming rule
When naming a modal proposition as affirmative or negative, the consideration is for the first part. So a composite proposition like 'possibly B' is considered affirmative because the first part is affirmative.
This rule is mentioned: 'The consideration in naming is for the first part.'
4
Apply the truth condition
A modal proposition is true if and only if its modality agrees with the actual material of the relation. For example, if the material is necessary, only the modality 'necessary' yields truth. If the material is possible, only 'possible' (or its equivalent) yields truth.
The passage says: 'And if it agrees with the material, the proposition is true; otherwise it is false.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كُلُّ نِسْبَةٍ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ إِمَّا وَاجِبَةٌ، أَوْ مُمْتَبِعَةٌ، أَوْ مُمْكِنَةٌ، وَتِلْكَ الْكَيْفِيَّاتُ الْمَوَادُّ، وَالدَّالُ عَلَيْهَا الْجِهَةُ ، وَمَا اشْتَمَلَتْ عَلَيْهَا يُسَمَّى مُوَجَّهَةٌ وَرُبَاعِيَّةٌ ، بَسِيطَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ حَقِيقَتُهَا إِيجَابًا فَقَطْ أَوْ سَلْبًا فَقَطْ ، وَمُرَكَّبَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ مُلْتَيْمَةٌ مِنْهُمَا ، وَالْعِبْرَةُ فى التّسْمِيَةِ لِلْجُزْءِ الْأَوَّلِ ، وَإِلَّا فَمُطْلَقَةٌ وَمُهْمَلَةٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ الْجِهَةُ ، وَهِيَ إِنْ وَافَقَتِ الْمَادَّةَ صَدَقَتِ الْقَضِيَّةُ ، وَإِلَّا كَذَبَتْ . وَالتَّحْقِيقُ أَنَّ الْمَوَادَّ الْحِكَمِيَّةَ هِيَ الْجِهَاتُ الْمَنْطِقِيَّةُ، وَقِيلَ: إِنَّهَا غَيْرُهَا ،

What includes them is called 'directed' (muwajjaha) and quadrilateral. It is simple (basīṭa) if its reality is only affirmation or only negation; and composite (murakkaba) if it is composed of both. The consideration in naming is for the first part. Otherwise it is absolute (muṭlaqa) and neglected (muhmala) in terms of modality. And if it agrees with the material, the proposition is true; otherwise it is false. The verification is that the philosophical materials are the logical modalities, and it is said that they are other than them.

Commentary
This passage defines modal propositions (al-qadaya al-muwajjaha). The author states that every relation (nisba) in itself (i.e., considered in the mind) is either necessary (wajiba), impossible (mumtani'a), or possible (mumkina). These three are called the 'materials' (mawadd). The marker that indicates which one is the 'modality' (jiha). A proposition that includes a modality is called 'directed' (muwajjaha). Understanding the Negative of the Predicate Proposition
Author defines and examines the innovation of the 'negative of the predicate' proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the negative of the predicate proposition and distinguish it from the simple negative in terms of truth conditions.

Core Idea
The negative of the predicate treats negation as a predicate, not a relation.

Later logicians introduced a new type of proposition called 'negative of the predicate' (sāliyat al-maḥmūl). This lesson unpacks its definition, contrasts it with the simple negative, and explains why the author considers it a purely mental proposition. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the nuances of propositional logic in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the innovation
Later scholars invented a proposition they called 'negative of the predicate'. Find where the passage mentions this innovation and note the Arabic term.
It is an innovation of the muta'akhkhirun.
2
Contrast with the simple negative
In a simple negative, the negation is a relation judged between two conceived terms. In the negative of the predicate, the negation is itself turned into a predicate and attributed to the subject. Compare the two definitions given.
Example: 'Zayd is not seeing' vs 'Zayd is non-seeing'.
3
Analyze truth condition difference
Later scholars ruled that an affirmative negative-of-predicate does not require external existence, unlike a simple affirmative. But the author argues that any affirmative copula absolutely requires existence.
Focus on the author's qariha (natural disposition) judgment.
4
Understand the author's resolution
The author concludes that the negative of the predicate is a mental proposition. All conceptual notions exist in extramental reality (nafs al-amr) either actually or hypothetically.
This resolves the existential import issue.
5
Establish the correlation
There is a necessary correlation in truth between the negative of the predicate and the simple negative. They are equivalent in truth value under the author's analysis.
The passage states 'between it and the negative there is a necessary correlation'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعَةُ: إِنَّ الْمُتَأَخْرِينَ اخْتَرَعُوا قَضِيَّةٌ سَمَّوْهَا سَالِيَةَ الْمَحْمُولِ، وَفَرَّقُوا بِأَنْ في السَّالِبَةِ يُتَصَوَّرُ الطَّرَفَانِ وَيُحْكَمُ بِالسَّلْبِ، وَفِي السَّالِبَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ يُرْجَعُ وَيُحْمَلُ ذَلِكَ السَّلْبُ عَلَى الْمَوْضُوعِ . وَحَكَمُوا بِأَنَّ صِدْقَ الْإِيجَابِ فِيهَا لَا يَسْتَدْعِي الْوُجُودَ ، كَالسَّلْب لَا يَسْتَدْعِيهِ، بَلْ السَّلْبُ يَسْتَدْعِيهِ كَالْإِيجَاب الْمُحَصَّلِ، وَقَرِيحَتُكَ حَاكِمَةٌ بِأَنَّ الرَّبْطَ الْإِيجَابِيَّ مُطْلَقًا يَقْتَضِي الْوُجُودَ وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالَ: الْحَقُّ إِنَّهَا قَضِيَّةٌ ذِهْنِيَّةٌ ، وَجَمِيعُ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ مَوْجُودَةٌ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ تَحْقِيقا أَوْ تَقْدِيرًا ، فَبَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ السَّالِبَةِ تَلَازُمٌ بِحَسَبِ الصِّدْقِ.
The fourth: Indeed the later scholars invented a proposition which they called 'negative of the predicate', and they distinguished that in the simple negative the two terms are conceived and the negation is judged, while in the negative of the predicate the negation is turned and made as a predicate on the subject. And they ruled that the truth of affirmation in it does not require existence, just as negation does not require it; rather negation requires it like the simple affirmative. And your natural disposition judges that the affirmative copula absolutely requires existence, and therefore he said: The truth is that it is a mental proposition, and all conceptual notions exist in the extramental reality in actuality or hypothetically, so between it and the negative there is a necessary correlation in terms of truth.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines the 'negative of the predicate' proposition (saliyat al-mahmul), an innovation of later logicians. It contrasts it with the simple negative (saliba). In a simple negative, both subject and predicate are conceived and a relation of negation is judged between them. In the negative of the predicate, the negation is itself made a predicate for the subject. The later scholars ruled that the affirmative truth of a negative of the predicate proposition does not require external existence—unlike a simple affirmative, which requires existence. However, the author's natural disposition (qariha) indicates that the affirmative copula always implies existence, so he concludes that such propositions are purely mental. All conceptual notions exist in the extramental reality (nafs al-amr) either actually or hypothetically, so there is a necessary correlation in truth value between the negative of the predicate and the simple negative.

Key Terms
سَالِيَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ (sāliyat al-maḥmūl): A proposition where the negation is made a predicate for the subject, e.g., 'Zayd is non-seeing'. [Note: This term is an innovation of later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) not found in earlier logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the negative of the predicate from the simple negative?
A) In the simple negative, the negation is judged between two terms; in the negative of the predicate, the negation is made a predicate.
B) The simple negative requires existence; the negative of the predicate does not.
C) They are identical in meaning.
D) The negative of the predicate has no subject.

Key Takeaway
The negative of the predicate proposition is an innovation where negation functions as a predicate, leading to different truth conditions that the author resolves by classifying it as a mental proposition.

?

The Three Matters and the Definition of Modality
Author defines the three matters and modality in modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three matters (mawadd) and the modality (jiha) in any modal proposition, and distinguish between simple and compound modal propositions.

Core Idea
The three matters (necessary, impossible, possible) are the real qualities; the modality is their indicator.

In Islamic logic, propositions are not merely true or false; they also carry a modal quality that reflects reality. This passage introduces the three fundamental modes of any relation in nafs al-amr: necessary, impossible, and possible. The verbal expression of these modes is the 'jiha' (modality). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the truth of modal statements in theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three matters
The passage states that every relation in extramental reality (nafs al-amr) is either necessary (wajiba), impossible (mumtani‘a), or possible (mumkina). These are the 'matters' (mawadd).
Necessary: cannot be otherwise; impossible: cannot be the case; possible: can be or not be.
2
Distinguish between matter and modality
Matter is the inherent quality; modality (jiha) is the linguistic indicator (e.g., 'necessarily', 'possibly'). For example, in 'Necessarily, the world is created', 'necessarily' is the modality, and the matter is necessity.
The same matter can be indicated by different modalities.
3
Classify modal propositions as simple or compound
A modal proposition is simple if it contains only affirmation or only negation; compound if it contains both (e.g., 'possibly, it is not necessary'). The passage says the naming considers the first part.
'Simple' here refers to the modal expression, not the categorical form.
4
Apply the truth condition
A modal proposition is true if and only if its modality agrees with the actual matter. For example, if the matter is necessity, the proposition must carry a necessity modality to be true.
Otherwise, the proposition is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كُلُّ نِسْبَةٍ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ إِمَّا وَاجِبَةٌ، أَوْ مُمْتَنِعَةٌ، أَوْ مُمْكِنَةٌ، وَتِلْكَ الْكَيْفِيَّاتُ الْمَوَادُّ، وَالدَّالُ عَلَيْهَا الْجِهَةُ ، وَمَا اشْتَمَلَتْ عَلَيْهَا يُسَمَّى مُوَجَّهَةٌ وَرُبَاعِيَّةٌ ، بَسِيطَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ حَقِيقَتُهَا إِيجَابًا فَقَطْ أَوْ سَلْبًا فَقَطْ ، وَمُرَكَّبَةً إِنْ كَانَتْ مُلْتَئِمَةً مِنْهُمَا ، وَالْعِبْرَةُ فى التّسْمِيَةِ لِلْجُزْءِ الْأَوَّلِ ، وَإِلَّا فَمُطْلَقَةٌ وَمُهْمَلَةٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ الْجِهَةُ ، وَهِيَ إِنْ وَافَقَتِ الْمَادَّةَ صَدَقَتِ الْقَضِيَّةُ ، وَإِلَّا كَذَبَتْ .
Every relation in the extramental reality is either necessary, or impossible, or possible. And those qualities are the 'matters' (al-mawadd), and the indicator of them is the 'modality' (al-jiha). And that which includes them is called 'modal' (muwajjaha) and 'quadripartite'? (rubā‘iyya), simple if its reality is only affirmation or only negation, and compound if it is composed of both. And the consideration in naming is for the first part, otherwise it is absolute (mutlaqa) and neglected (muhmala) from the aspect of modality. And if it agrees with the matter, the proposition is true; otherwise it is false.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines the fundamental classification of modal propositions. Every relation (nisba) that forms a proposition exists in extramental reality (nafs al-amr) in one of three modes: necessary (wajiba), impossible (mumtani‘a), or possible (mumkina). These are called the 'matters' (mawadd). The word that indicates which of these modes applies is the 'modality' (jiha), e.g., 'necessarily', 'possibly'. A proposition that includes a modality is called 'modal' (muwajjaha). The passage also distinguishes between simple (bassita) modal propositions that are purely affirmative or purely negative, and compound (murakkaba) that involve both. In naming, we consider the first part of the modal expression. If no modality is expressed, the proposition is 'absolute' (mutlaqa) or 'neglected' (muhmala) with respect to modality. For a modal proposition to be true, its modality must agree with the actual matter; otherwise it is false.

Key Terms
مَادَّة
جِهَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The subject term.

Key Takeaway
Every modal proposition's truth depends on the agreement of its modality with the actual matter (necessary, impossible, or possible).

The Identity of Philosophical Matters and Logical Modalities: A Dialectical Analysis
A dialectical dispute on philosophical matters and logical modalities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection and response in this dialectical exchange and identify the key distinction that resolves it.

Core Idea
The identity claim is defended by distinguishing two kinds of necessity: in itself and by another.

In this passage, the author defends the position that philosophical matters are identical to logical modalities. An objection is raised claiming this would lead to an impossibility. The response introduces a vital distinction that saves the claim. Understanding this dialectic sharpens your ability to analyze theological and philosophical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's assertion
Find in the Arabic the author's claim: that philosophical matters are the logical modalities. This is the position being defended (mudda'i).
The phrase 'وَالتَّحْقِيقُ أَنَّ الْمَوَادَّ الْحِكَمِيَّةَ هِيَ الْجِهَاتُ الْمَنْطِقِيَّةُ' states this.
2
Extract the objection
Look for the qīla (it is said) or the conditional 'wa-illā' introducing the objection: if they were identical, then lawāzim al-māhiyya would be necessary in themselves, which is impossible.
The objection uses a conditional to challenge the claim.
3
Analyze the response
The author responds by distinguishing two kinds of necessity: wujūb al-wujūd fī nafsihi (impossible for attributes) and wujūb al-thubūt li-ghayrihi (necessary for attributes). Show how this distinction neutralizes the objection.
The response does not deny the conditional premise but clarifies the consequent.
4
Determine the outcome
Based on the response, the objection is refuted. The author's verification stands: the matters are identical to modalities, with the proper understanding of necessity.
The passage ends with the response, confirming the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالتَّحْقِيقُ أَنَّ الْمَوَادَّ الْحِكَمِيَّةَ هِيَ الْجِهَاتُ الْمَنْطِقِيَّةُ، وَقِيلَ: إِنَّهَا غَيْرُهَا ، وَإِلا لَكَانَتْ لَوَازِمُ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَاجِبَةٌ لِذَاتِهَا . وَالْجَوَابُ: أَنَّهُ فُرِّقَ بَيْنَ وُجُوبِ الْوُجُودِ فِي نَفْسِهِ، وَبَيْنَ وُجُوبِ الثَّبوتِ لِغَيْرِهِ، وَالْأَوَّلُ مُحَالٌ غَيْرُ لَازِمٍ، وَالثَّانِي لَازِمٌ غَيْرُ مُحَالٍ.
And the verification is that the philosophical matters are the logical modalities, and it is said that they are other than them. Otherwise, the necessary concomitants of the essence would be necessary in themselves. And the response: that a distinction is made between the necessity of existence in itself and the necessity of subsistence for another. The first is impossible and not necessary, and the second is necessary and not impossible.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute about whether the 'philosophical matters' (al-mawadd al-hikmiyya) are identical to the 'logical modalities' (al-jihat al-mantiqiyya). The author asserts that the verification (tahqiq) is that they are identical. Another opinion claims they are different. The objection to the identity view is: if the matters are the same as modalities, then necessary concomitants of an essence (lawazim al-mahiyya) would be necessary in themselves, which is absurd. The response distinguishes two types of necessity: 'necessity of existence in itself' (wujub al-wujud fi nafsihi) and 'necessity of subsistence for another' (wujub al-thubut li-ghayrihi). The first is impossible for attributes, but the second is necessary and not impossible. Thus, the essence itself is not necessary in itself, but its attributes are necessary by another, which is acceptable. So the objection is refuted.

وُجُوبُ الْوُجُودِ فِي نَفْسِهِ
وُجُوبُ الثَّبوتِ لِغَيْرِهِ
الْمَوَادُّ الْحِكَمِيَّةُ
الْجِهَاتُ الْمَنْطِقِيَّةُ

The Moderns' Expansion of Matter in Modal Propositions
Author contrasts ancient and modern views on matter definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the ancient and modern definitions of matter (madda) and identify qualities of the relation in modal propositions.

Core Idea
Matter is not just three modalities—it is any quality of the relation, making modal types infinite.

In classical logic, matter was confined to the three modes: necessary, impossible, possible. But later logicians (al-muhdathun) saw this as too restrictive. They expanded 'matter' to include any attribute of the relation in a proposition, such as permanence or temporal limitation. This redefinition opened the door to an unlimited number of modal proposition types.

Guided Path
1
Recall the ancient definition
The ancients (qudama) defined matter as the three modes: necessary, impossible, possible.
These correspond to the three judgments in kalam: wujub, imtina', jawaz.
2
Identify the modern redefinition
The moderns (muhdathun) define matter as 'every quality that the relation has' (kull kayfiyya kanat lil-nisba).
The relation here is the nexus between subject and predicate in a proposition.
3
List examples of such qualities
The passage gives 'dawam' (permanence) and 'tawqit' (temporal limitation) as examples; think of others like 'mashruta' (conditional), 'munfasila' (disjunctive).
Each quality modifies the mode of the proposition beyond the basic three.
4
Understand the consequence
Because qualities are potentially infinite, the number of modal proposition types becomes infinite (ghayr mutanahiya).
This is why the moderns expanded the categories of modal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا عَلَى رَأي الْقُدَمَاءِ ، وَأَمَّا عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الْمُحْدَثِينَ فَالْمَادَّةُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ كُلَّ كَيْفِيَّةٍ كَانَتْ لِلنِّسْبَةِ ، كَدَوَامٍ وَتَوْقِيتٍ إِلى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ كَانَتِ الْمُوَجَّهَاتُ غَيْرَ مُتَنَاهِيَة .
This is according to the view of the ancients. As for the school of the moderns, matter is an expression for every quality that the relation has, such as permanence, temporal limitation, and so forth. Hence the modal propositions were infinite.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents the modern (muhdathin) definition of 'matter' (madda). According to the ancients (qudama'), matter was restricted to the three modalities: necessary, impossible, possible. The moderns expand it to include any quality of the relation (kayfiyya) in a proposition, such as 'permanence' (dawam) or 'temporal limitation' (tawqit) and others. Consequently, the number of modal proposition types becomes infinite (ghayr mutanahiya), because there are infinitely many possible qualifiers. This broader view allows for more nuanced classifications, such as 'conditional' (mashruta), 'temporal' (waqtiyya), etc., as detailed in the following passage.

Key Terms
الْمُحْدَثُونَ

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) They focus only on quantitative propositions.

Key Takeaway
The modern logicians redefined matter as any attribute of the proposition's relation, thereby expanding modal analysis beyond the classical three modes.

The Nine Basic Modal Propositions
Author enumerates modal proposition types according to the modern view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the nine basic modal proposition types from its condition and give an Islamic example.

Core Idea
Modal propositions qualify the mode of a judgment—necessity, actuality, possibility, or conditionality.

This passage from Bahr al-'Ulum lists nine fundamental modal propositions used by later logicians. These are not just abstract terms; they are the backbone of syllogisms involving modalities, which appear in kalam and usul al-fiqh arguments. Understanding them will sharpen your ability to evaluate the strength of claims about necessity and possibility in Islamic theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish necessary from non-necessary
Look for 'istihalat al-infikak' (impossibility of separation). If the relation cannot be separated at all, it is 'absolute necessary' (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa). This is the strongest modal type.
2
Identify temporal restrictions
If the judgment is bound to a time: a specific time (e.g., 'at noon') gives 'absolute temporal' (waqtiyya muṭlaqa); an unspecified time (e.g., 'at some time') gives 'absolute distributed' (muntashira muṭlaqa).
3
Spot conditional propositions
If the relation holds only as long as a certain attribute exists ('mā dāma al-waṣf'), it is 'general conditional' (mashrūṭa ‘āmma). If it holds as long as the subject's usual nature exists ('mā dāma al-waṣf al-‘urfī'), it is 'general conventional' (‘urfiyya ‘āmma).
4
Recognize perpetual and actual
If the relation never separates ('bilā infikāk'), it is 'absolute perpetual' (dā’ima muṭlaqa). If it simply occurs in actuality ('bi-fi‘liyyatihā'), it is 'general absolute' (muṭlaqa ‘āmma).
5
Differentiate between possible types
If the relation is not impossible ('bilā istihālatihā'), it is 'general possible' (mumkina ‘āmma). If both affirmation and negation are not impossible ('bilā istihālat al-ṭarafayn'), it is 'specific possible' (mumkina khāṣṣa).
The specific possible corresponds to two-sided possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَهِيَ إِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِاسْتِحَالَةِ انْفِكَاكِ النِّسْبَةِ مُطْلَقًا فَضَرُورِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ، أَوْ مَا دَامَ الْوَصْفُ فَمَشْرُوطَةٌ عَامَّةٌ ، أَوْ فِي وَقْتٍ مُعَيَّنٍ فَوَقْتِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ، أَوْ غَيْرِ مُعَيَّنٍ فَمُنْتَشِرَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ، أَوْ بِعَدَمِ انْفِكَاكِهَا مُطْلَقًا فَدَائِمَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ، أَوْ مَا دَامَ الْوَصْفُ فَعُرْفِيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ، أَوْ بِفِعْلِيَّتِهَا فَمُطْلَقَةٌ عَامَّةٌ، أَوْ بِعَدَمِ اسْتِحَالَتِهَا فَمُمْكِنَةٌ عَامَّةٌ، أَوْ بِعَدَمِ اسْتِحَالَةِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ فَمُمْكِنَةٌ خَاصَّةٌ ، وَلَا فَرْقَ بَيْنَ الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ فِيهَا إِلَّا في اللُّفْظِ . وَقَدِ اعْتُبِرَ تَقْيِيدُ الْعَامَّتَيْنِ وَالْوَقْتِيَتَيْنِ الْمُطْلَقَتَيْنِ بِاللَّادَوَامِ الذَّاتِيِّ، فَتُسَمَّى الْمَشْرُوطَةَ الْخَاصَّةَ وَالْعُرْفِيَّةَ الْخَاصَّةَ وَالْوَقْتِيَّةَ وَالْمُنْتَشِرَةَ، وَتَقْيِيدُ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الْعَامَّةِ بِاللَّا ضَرُورَةِ أَوِ اللَّادَوَامِ الذَّاتِيَّيْن ، فَتُسَمَّى الْوُجُودِيَّةَ اللَّاضَرُورِيَّةَ وَالوُجُودِيَّة اللَّادَائِمَةَ، وَهِيَ الْمُطْلَقَةُ الإِسْكَنْدَرِيَّةُ .
Thus, if it is ruled in it that the separation of the relation is absolutely impossible, then it is 'absolute necessary' (daruriyya mutlaqa); or as long as the attribute exists, then it is 'general conditional' (mashruta 'amma); or in a specific time, then it is 'absolute temporal' (waqtiyya mutlaqa); or an unspecified time, then it is 'absolute distributed' (muntashira mutlaqa); or by the non-separation of it absolutely, then it is 'absolute perpetual' (da'ima mutlaqa); or as long as the attribute exists, then it is 'general conventional' ('urfiyya 'amma); or by its actuality, then it is 'general absolute' (mutlaqa 'amma); or by its non-impossibility, then it is 'general possible' (mumkina 'amma); or by the non-impossibility of both sides, then it is 'specific possible' (mumkina khassa). And there is no difference between affirmation and negation in them except in wording. And the conditioning of the two general and the two absolute temporal (propositions) by intrinsic non-permanence is considered, so they are called the 'special conditional', 'special conventional', 'temporal', and 'distributed'; and the conditioning of the general absolute by the two (i.e., non-necessity or intrinsic non-permanence) is called 'non-necessary existential' (wujudiyya la-daruriyya) and 'non-permanent existential' (wujudiyya la-da'ima), and it is the 'Iskandariyya absolute'.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of modal propositions according to the modern school, based on the quality of the relation. It lists nine basic types: (1) Absolute necessary (daruriyya mutlaqa): the relation cannot be separated at all (e.g., 'God is necessarily existent'). (2) General conditional (mashruta 'amma): as long as the subject has a certain attribute (e.g., 'Every writer is a human as long as he writes'). (3) Absolute temporal (waqtiyya mutlaqa): at a specific time (e.g., 'Zayd is standing at noon'). (4) Absolute distributed (muntashira mutlaqa): at an unspecified time (e.g., 'The moon is eclipsed at some time'). (5) Absolute perpetual (da'ima mutlaqa): the relation never separates (e.g., 'The sky is always moving'). (6) General conventional ('urfiyya 'amma): as long as the subject's usual attribute holds (e.g., 'Every human is rational as long as he is a human'). (7) General absolute (mutlaqa 'amma): the relation is actual at some time (e.g., 'Some human is a writer'). (8) General possible (mumkina 'amma): the relation is not impossible (e.g., 'It is possible that Zayd is standing'). (9) Specific possible (mumkina khassa): both sides are possible (e.g., 'Zayd could be standing or sitting'). The passage notes that the qualification of the two general types and the two absolute temporal types with intrinsic non-permanence (la-dawam al-dhati) generates special conditioned types. Similarly, the general absolute conditioned with non-necessity or non-permanence yields the 'Iskandari absolute' (mutlaqa Iskandariyya). This classification is central to understanding modal logic in later Islamic tradition.

Key Terms
ضَرُورِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ
مَشْرُوطَةٌ عَامَّةٌ
مُمْكِنَةٌ خَاصَّةٌ
وَقْتِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ
مُنْتَشِرَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ
دَائِمَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ
عُرْفِيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ
مُطْلَقَةٌ عَامَّةٌ
مُمْكِنَةٌ عَامَّةٌ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of modal proposition holds that the relation is inseparable absolutely?
A) General conditional (mashruta 'amma).
B) Absolute necessary (daruriyya mutlaqa).
C) General absolute (mutlaqa 'amma).
D) Specific possible (mumkina khassa).

Key Takeaway
Modal propositions vary by the mode of the relation—necessity, perpetuity, conditionality, temporality, actuality, or possibility—and recognizing the precise condition is essential for sound logical analysis.
