Definition of the Absolute Necessary Proposition
Author gives the well-known definition of the absolute necessary proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining condition of an absolute necessary proposition and distinguish it from other types of necessary propositions.

Core Idea
Absolute necessity is tied to the subject's essence, not to time or condition.

This passage presents the standard definition of the 'absolute necessary proposition' (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa), a foundational concept in Islamic propositional logic. Understanding this definition is crucial for correctly classifying propositions in arguments, especially in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh. The definition emphasizes that necessity pertains to the entire duration of the subject's essence, a key distinction from other modal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the phrase 'الضَّرُورِيَّةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ' in the Arabic. This is the definiendum.
It is a type of proposition denoting necessity.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'proposition' (qadiyya) – it is a judgment about a subject and predicate.
3
Extract the differentia
The differentia is the condition: the judgment (affirmation or negation) is made with necessity 'as long as the essence of the subject exists' (mā dāma dhātu al-mawḍūʿ).
This means the necessity holds for the entire period the subject retains its essence, not just a specific time.
4
Distinguish from other necessities
Contrast this with temporal necessity (e.g., 'Zayd is standing as long as he is standing') or conditional necessity. Absolute necessity is unconditional regarding time but conditional upon the existence of the essence.
This distinction is used later in more complex classifications.
5
Apply the definition to an example
Consider the proposition 'Every human is rational.' This is absolute necessary because rationality is inseparable from humanness as long as the human essence exists.
The predicate 'rational' is necessarily affirmed of 'human' for the duration of the subject's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَكْمِلَةُ: فِيهَا مَبَاحِثُ الْأَوَّلُ: اشْتَهَرَ تَعْرِيفُ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ بِأَنَّهَا الَّتِي يُحْكَمُ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ ثُبُوتِ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ أَوْ سَلْبِهِ عَنْهُ، مَا دَامَ ذَاتُ الْمَوْضُوعِ

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a 'completion' (takmila) section that provides the standard definition of the 'absolute necessary proposition' (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa). According to the widely accepted definition, it is a proposition in which the judgment is made that the predicate is necessarily affirmed of the subject, or necessarily negated, as long as the essence (dhat) of the subject exists. For example, 'Every human is necessarily rational' qualifies as an absolute necessary proposition because rationality necessarily inheres in humans as long as the human essence exists. The definition is cut off here but would likely continue to clarify the condition. Note that 'as long as the essence exists' distinguishes it from conditional necessities tied to temporary attributes.

Refining the Definition of Absolute Perpetual Proposition
Author raises a doubt about the definition of absolute perpetual proposition concerning the predicate being existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in the definition of the absolute perpetual proposition when the predicate is existence, and explain the proposed solution.

Core Idea
A definition that fails to exclude 'existence as predicate' creates a contradiction with the general absolute.

Logicians define the absolute perpetual proposition as one where the relation is judged perpetual as long as the subject's essence exists. But a subtle doubt emerges: if the predicate is 'existence' itself, this definition would make the perpetual differ from the absolute, destroying their contradictory relationship. We explore this doubt and the refinement needed to preserve logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Restate the standard definition
The absolute perpetual (da'ima mutlaqa) is defined as: a judgment that the relation holds perpetually as long as the subject's essence exists. Write this definition in your own words.
This is the starting point for the critique.
2
Identify the predicate condition
Consider a proposition where the predicate is 'existence' (wujud). For example, 'The world exists as long as it exists'. In such a case, the relation is always true, but note that 'perpetuity' and 'absoluteness' coincide: the proposition is both perpetual and absolute.
The doubt arises specifically for existence-predicated propositions.
3
Understand the doubt (shakk)
The author argues: if the predicate is existence, then the essential perpetuity (dawam dhati) differs from the general absolute (itlaq 'amm). Why? Because in a proposition with existence as predicate, the perpetual is not in contradiction with the absolute; they are the same. But logically, perpetual and absolute are supposed to be contradictory modifiers. This reveals a flaw in the definition.
Contradiction between modalities is essential for logical relations like contrariety.
4
Analyze the proposed solution
The solution offered is that the definition implicitly assumes the predicate is something other than existence. The immediate understanding (al-mutabadir) from the definition is that the predicate must be different from existence. Thus, existence-predicated propositions are excluded from the scope of the definition.
The text cuts off, but the solution is to restrict the definition to predicates other than existence.
5
Evaluate the refinement
Consider whether this implicit condition adequately resolves the doubt. Does it preserve the intended logical relations? This shows how logicians carefully refine definitions to avoid unintended consequences.
Critical refinement is a key skill in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : الْمَشْهُورُ في تَعْرِيفِ الدَّائِمَةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِدَوَامِ النِّسْبَةِ مَا دَامَ ذَاتُ الْمَوْضُوعِ مَوْجُودَةٌ ، وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ ، وَهُوَ: أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ يُفَارِقَ الدَّوَامُ الذَّاتِيُّ الْإِطْلَاقَ الْعَامَّ فِي قَضِيَّةٍ مَحْمُولُهَا الْوُجُودُ، فَلَا يَكُونُ بَيْنَهُمَا تَنَاقُضِ. قِيلَ في حَلَّهِ : الْمُتَبَادَرُ مِنَ التَّعْرِيفِ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَحْمُولُ مُغَايِرًا لِلْوُجُودِ، فَلَيْسَ
The second: The famous definition of the absolute perpetual [proposition] is that in which it is judged with the perpetuity of the relation as long as the essence of the subject exists. And here there is a doubt, which is: that it necessitates that the essential perpetuity differs from the general absolute in a proposition whose predicate is existence, so there would be no contradiction between them. It was said in its solution: the immediate understanding from the definition is that the predicate must be different from existence, so it is not...
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the definition of the absolute perpetual proposition (da'ima mutlaqa). The standard definition is: a proposition where the relation is judged to be perpetual as long as the subject's essence exists. The doubt raised is that, if the predicate is 'existence' itself, then the essential perpetuity (al-dawam al-dhati) would differ from the general absolute (al-itlaq al-'amm) because in a proposition with existence as predicate, the perpetual and the absolute would not be contradictory. The proposed solution is that the definition implicitly requires the predicate to be different from existence. The text cuts off at 'فَلَيْسَ' (so it is not...), likely continuing with an explanation of what the predicate must be. This shows how logicians refined definitions to avoid logical contradictions.

Two Doubts on Modality with Existence as Predicate
Presents and resolves two logical doubts about necessity and possibility when the predicate is existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'necessity at the time of existence' and 'necessity conditional upon existence' in modal propositions with existence as predicate.

Core Idea
Modal propositions with existence as predicate require careful distinction between types of necessity.

This passage resolves two complex doubts about modal propositions where the predicate is existence itself. The first doubt concerns how necessity can coexist with possibility when the predicate is existence; the answer distinguishes between two types of necessity. The second doubt deals with universal negative propositions that seem false when the subject is hypothetical. Mastering these distinctions is crucial for handling modal claims in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first doubt
Locate the first doubt: when the predicate is existence, a necessary proposition (e.g., 'Zayd is necessarily existing') might conflict with a special possible proposition. The text says 'لزم عدم منافاة الضرورة للإمكان الخاص' – necessity not conflicting with special possibility. This seems problematic because necessity and possibility are usually opposites.
2
Understand the answer to the first doubt
The answer distinguishes between 'necessity at the time of existence' (الضرورة في زمان الوجود) and 'necessity conditional upon existence' (الضرورة بشرط الوجود). The first means the proposition is necessary only during the time the subject exists; the second means it is necessary only if the subject exists at all. This resolves the apparent conflict.
3
Examine the objection and counter-objection
An objection is raised that this distinction restricts necessity to eternal propositions, making it not general. The counter-objection uses essential attributes (الذاتيات) like 'animality of man' which are necessary for the essence always, not conditional on existence. This shows that some necessities are independent of existence.
4
Analyze the second doubt on universal negatives
The second doubt concerns 'negation as long as existence' (السلب ما دام الوجود). For example, 'No phoenix is a man necessarily' – since phoenixes don't exist, the negation should be true, but the doubt claims it isn't true without existence. The answer explains that 'as long as' (ما دام) qualifies the affirmation implicit in the negation, allowing truth by absence of subject.
5
Reflect on the broader implications
The passage concludes that existence is broader than actual and hypothetical existence, and many corruptions follow if these distinctions are ignored. The trained student should see how modal logic applies to Islamic theological claims about divine attributes and created entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَوْجُودًا. وَفِيهِ شَكٍّ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ ، الْأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ إِذَا كَانَ الْمَحْمُولُ هُوَ الْوُجُودُ لَزِمَ عَدَمُ مُنَافَاةِ الضَّرُورَةِ لِلْإِمْكَانِ الْخَاصٌ . وَأُجِيبَ بِالْفَرْقِ بَيْنَ الضَّرُورَةِ في زَمَانِ الْوُجُودِ وَبَيْنَهَا بِشَرْطِ الْوُجُودِ، وَأُورِدَ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ حَصْرُهَا فِي الْأَزَلِيَّةِ الَّتِي يُحْكَمُ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ أَزَلًا وَأَبَدًا، فَلَا تَكُونُ أَعَمَّ ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَمَّا لَمْ يَجِبْ وُجُودُ الْمَوْضُوعِ لَمْ يَجِبْ لَهُ شَيْءٌ فِي وَقْتِ وُجُودِهِ، وَنُوقِضَ بِثْبُوتِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ ؛ فَإِنَّهُ ضَرُورِيُّ لِلذَّاتِ دَائِمًا لَا بِشَرْطِ الْوُجُودِ ، وَإِلَّا لَكَانَتْ حَيَوَانِيَّةُ الْإِنْسَانِ - مَثَلاً ـ مَجْعُولَةٌ ، فَافْهَمْ. الثَّانِي: السَّلْبُ مَا دَامَ الْوُجُودُ لَا يَصْدُقُ بِدُونِهِ، فَلَا تَكُونُ الصَّالِبَةُ أَعَمَّ، وَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْعَنْقَاءِ بِإِنْسَانٍ بِالضَّرُورَةِ». وَأُجِيبَ بِأَنَّ مَا دَامَ ظَرْفٌ لِلنُّبُوتِ الَّذِي يَتَضَمَّنُهُ السَّلْبُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَجُوزُ صِدْقُهَا بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوْضُوعِ وَبِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَحْمُولِ ، إِمَّا فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ أَوْ بَعْضِهَا، نَحْوُ لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْقَمَرِ بِمُنْخَسِفَ بِالضَّرُورَةِ». وَفِيهِ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يُنَافِيَ الْإِمْكَانَ ؛ فَإِنَّ كُلَّ فَمَرٍ مُنْخَسِفٌ بِالْفِعْل صَادِقٌ ، فَيَصْدُقُ بِالْإِمْكَانِ ، وَيَبْطُلُ مَا قَالُوا: إِنَّ السَّالِبَةَ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ الْأَزَلِيَّةَ وَالْمُطْلَقَةَ مُتَسَاوِيَتَانِ ؛ فَإِنَّ سَلْبَ الْأَعَمِّ أَخَصُّ مِنْ سَلْبِ الْأَخَصُ . وَبِالْجُمْلَةِ يَلْزَمُ مَفَاسِدُ غَيْرُ عَدِيدَةٍ لَا تَخْفَى عَلَى الْمُتَدَرِّبِ. وَغَايَةُ مَا يُجَابُ بِهِ: أَنَّ الْوُجُودَ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْمُحَقِّقِ وَالْمُقَدَّرِ ، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ.
...exists. There is a doubt from two aspects. The first: that if the predicate is existence, then necessity not contradicting special possibility follows. And it was answered by distinguishing between necessity at the time of existence and necessity conditional upon existence. And it was objected that it entails restricting it to eternal [propositions] wherein the necessity of the relation is judged eternally and perpetually, so it is not more general; because when the subject's existence is not necessary, nothing is necessary for it at the time of its existence. And it was refuted by the establishment of essential attributes; for they are necessary to the essence perpetually, not conditional upon existence; otherwise, the animality of man, for example, would be accidental, so understand. The second: the negation as long as existence is not true without it, so the universal negative is not more general, and it entails that 'No phoenix is a man necessarily' is not true. And it was answered that 'as long as' is a container for the affirmation that the negation includes, and then it may be true by the absence of the subject and by the absence of the predicate, either in all times or some of them, like 'No moon is eclipsed necessarily'. And therein [lies] that it entails not contradicting possibility; for every moon eclipsed actually is true, so it is true with possibility, and what they said becomes false: that the necessary eternal negative and the absolute are equivalent; for the negation of the more general is more specific than the negation of the more specific. In summary, many corruptions follow that are not hidden from the trained student. And the utmost that can be answered is: that existence is more general than the actual and the hypothetical, and therein lies what lies therein.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage discusses two logical doubts concerning modal propositions where the predicate is existence itself. The first doubt: if the predicate is existence, then a necessary proposition should not conflict with a special possible one; the answer distinguishes between necessity at the time of existence and necessity conditional upon existence. An objection is raised that this would restrict necessity to eternal truths, making it not general; a counterargument uses essential attributes like 'animality of man' which are necessary for the essence regardless of existence. The second doubt concerns universal negative propositions like 'No phoenix is a man necessarily'; the answer explains that 'as long as' modifies the affirmation contained in the negation, allowing truth by absence of subject or predicate. A further point shows that this interpretation would allow possibility to coexist with necessity, refuting the claim that necessary eternal negatives and absolute negatives are equivalent. The passage concludes that existence is broader than actual and hypothetical existence.

Key Terms
الضَّرُورَةِ
الْإِمْكَانِ الْخَاصٌ
السَّلْبُ مَا دَامَ الْوُجُودُ

The Perpetual Absolute: Definition and a Subtle Doubt
States the common definition and solves a doubt about predicate existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions of a perpetual absolute proposition and resolve the doubt about its relation to the general absolute.

Core Idea
The perpetual absolute requires the predicate to be other than existence.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified based on the modality of their judgment. One important type is the 'perpetual absolute' (al-dā'imah al-muṭlaqah), where the relation holds as long as the subject's essence exists. A subtle doubt arises: if the predicate is existence itself, does the perpetual absolute still differ from the general absolute? The logicians solved this by clarifying that the definition implicitly assumes the predicate is other than existence.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition
Read the definition: a perpetual absolute is one in which permanence of the relation is judged as long as the subject's essence exists. Note the key condition: the duration is tied to the essence of the subject, not to time.
This is the famous definition (al-mashhūr).
2
Identify the doubt
If the predicate is existence (e.g., 'The active intellect exists'), then the truth of the proposition does not depend on the subject's essence after it ceases to exist—leading to confusion with the general absolute (which does not require permanence).
The doubt: the perpetual absolute would then be no different from the general absolute.
3
Analyze the solution
The solution is that 'what is understood from the definition' is that the predicate must be ontologically other than existence (mughāyir li-l-wujūd). Thus, the self-perpetual (al-dawām al-dhātī) is not applicable when the predicate is existence.
The definition implicitly restricts the predicate's nature.
4
Apply the example
Consider: 'The active intellect does not exist actually' is false. Its contradictory is 'The active intellect exists,' which is a perpetual absolute with existence as predicate. This illustrates that the definition's condition avoids contradiction.
The example confirms the rule: existence cannot be the predicate in a proper perpetual absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : الْمَشْهُورُ في تَعْرِيفِ الدَّائِمَةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِدَوَامِ النِّسْبَةِ مَا دَامَ ذَاتُ الْمَوْضُوعِ مَوْجُودَةٌ ، وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ ، وَهُوَ: أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ يُفَارِقَ الدَّوَامُ الذَّاتِيُّ الْإِطْلَاقَ الْعَامَّ فِي قَضِيَّةٍ مَحْمُولُهَا الْوُجُودُ، فَلَا يَكُونُ بَيْنَهُمَا تَنَاقُضِ. قِيلَ في حَلَّهِ : الْمُتَبَادَرُ مِنَ التَّعْرِيفِ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَحْمُولُ مُغَايِرًا لِلْوُجُودِ، فَلَيْسَ هُنَاكَ دَوَامٌ ذَاتِي . أَقُولُ: «الْعَقْلُ الْفَمَّالُ لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودِ بِالْفِعْل كَاذِبٌ ، فَيَلْزَمُ صِدْقُ نَقِيضِهِ، وَهُوَ دَائِمَةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ مَحْمُولُهَا الْوُجُودُ.
The second: The famous definition of the perpetual absolute is that wherein the permanence of the relation is judged as long as the essence of the subject exists. And here there is a doubt, which is: that it entails that the self-perpetual departs from the general absolute in a proposition whose predicate is existence, so there is no contradiction between them. It was said in its solution: what is understood from the definition is that the predicate is other than existence, so there is no self-perpetual there. I say: 'The active intellect does not exist actually' is false, so its contradictory is true, and it is a perpetual absolute whose predicate is existence.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage defines the 'perpetual absolute' proposition (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa) as one in which the relation (e.g., affirmation) is judged to hold permanently as long as the subject's essence exists. A doubt arises: if the predicate is existence itself, then the perpetual absolute would not contradict the general absolute (which does not require permanence). The solution is that the definition implicitly assumes the predicate is other than existence; otherwise, the notion of 'self-perpetual' does not apply. The commentator then provides an example: 'The active intellect does not exist actually' is false, so its contradictory (the active intellect exists) is true, and that contradictory is a perpetual absolute with existence as predicate, illustrating the point.

Key Terms
الدَّائِمَةُ الْمُطْلَقَةُ
الدَّوَامُ الذَّاتِيُّ
الْإِطْلَاقَ الْعَامَّ
الْعَقْلُ الْفَعَّالُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition is necessary for the definition of the perpetual absolute to avoid contradiction with the general absolute?
A) The subject must be eternal.
B) The predicate must be other than existence.
C) The proposition must be affirmative.
D) The duration must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The perpetual absolute requires the predicate to be distinct from existence, otherwise it collapses into the general absolute.

Two Interpretations of the Common Conditional
Explains two interpretations of the common conditional and their relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two interpretations of the common conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma) and identify each in a given proposition.

Core Idea
The common conditional has two meanings: necessity tied to the attribute vs. necessity throughout its times.

The common conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma) is a type of conditional proposition used in logic. This passage clarifies that it can be understood in two distinct ways: one where the necessity of the relation is conditional upon the descriptive attribute itself, and another where the necessity holds in all times that the attribute exists. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly evaluating logical arguments involving conditional statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Recognize that this is about the common conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma), a conditional where the condition is a descriptive attribute (wasf 'unwani).
2
Understand the first meaning
The necessity of the relation is conditional upon the attribute itself (e.g., 'Every writer moves his hand while writing'). The attribute must have an input (madkhal) in the necessity.
3
Understand the second meaning
The necessity holds in all times of the attribute (e.g., 'Every writer moves his hand at all times of his writing'). The attribute may not have a direct input in the necessity.
4
Compare the relationship
The two meanings have a partial overlap (umum min wajh), meaning some cases satisfy both, some only one.
5
Apply to an example
Test a proposition like 'Every acting person is an agent while acting' to see which interpretation applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّالِثُ : الْمَشْرُوطَةُ الْعَامَّةُ تَارَةٌ تُؤْخَذُ بِمَعْنَى ضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ بِشَرْطِ الْوَصْفِ الْعِنْوَانِيِّ، وَأُخْرَى بِمَعْنَى ضَرُورَتِهَا فِي جَمِيعِ أَوْقَاتِ الْوَصْفِ، وَفِي الْأَوَّلِ يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ لِلْوَصْفِ مَدْخَلٌ فِي الضَّرُورَةِ، بِخِلَافِ الثَّانِي، وَبَيْنَهُمَا عُمُومٌ مِنْ وَجْهِ.
The third: The common conditional is taken sometimes to mean the necessity of the relation conditional upon the descriptive attribute, and another time to mean its necessity in all times of the attribute. In the first, the attribute must have an input in the necessity, unlike the second. And between them is a generality from one aspect.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage clarifies two meanings of the 'common conditional' proposition (al-mashruta al-'amma). The first meaning: the relation (e.g., predication) is necessary conditional upon having the descriptive attribute (like 'every writer moves his hand while he is writing'). The second meaning: the relation is necessary in all times that the attribute exists (e.g., 'every writer is a hand-mover at all times of his writing'). The first requires that the attribute plays a role in the necessity, whereas the second does not necessarily tie the necessity to the attribute itself, just to the times. The two meanings have a partial overlap (generality from one aspect), meaning each includes cases the other does not.

Key Terms
الْمَشْرُوطَةُ الْعَامَّةُ
الْوَصْفِ الْعِنْوَانِيِّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the two interpretations of the common conditional?
A) They are identical
B) They are contradictory
C) They have a partial overlap (عموم من وجه)
D) The second is a subset of the first

Key Takeaway
The common conditional can mean either necessity conditional on the attribute or necessity in all times of the attribute, and these two meanings have a partial overlap.

?

?

The Universal Possible is a Genuine Modal Proposition
Argues that the universal possible is a genuine modal proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify why the universal possible (al-mumkina al-ʿāmmah) qualifies as a genuine modal proposition using the criteria of the quality of the relation.

Core Idea
Possibility is a modality, so possible propositions are modal propositions.

Some scholars argued that the universal possible is not a real proposition because it lacks a firm judgment. This passage refutes that by showing that possibility itself is a modality—a quality of the relation—and therefore any proposition expressing possibility is indeed a genuine modal proposition. We will unpack this defense step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The passage begins by stating that some people denied the universal possible is an actual proposition because it does not contain a judgment (ḥukm). Locate this claim in the Arabic: 'ذهب قوم إلى أن الممكنة العامة ليست قضية بالفعل؛ لعدم اشتمالها على الحكم'.
2
Understand the refutation's core
The author responds by asserting that possibility (imkān) is a quality of the relation (kayfiyyat al-nisba), and the foundation of the relation is affirmation (thubūt). Therefore a possible proposition does contain a judgment—it affirms the relation in a weak mode.
3
Compare modalities
The passage ranks modalities: necessity and impossibility indicate strength of the connection (وثاقة الرابط), while possibility indicates weakness. Recognize that all three are modalities that qualify the affirmation.
4
Address the immediate understanding
The author concedes that when we say 'possible', the immediate understanding is occurrence in actuality, but this does not harm its generality as a modal proposition. Just as 'existence' is general, so is possibility.
5
Conclude by analogy
The final sentence argues: if the possible is modal, then the absolute (non-modal) proposition is even more clearly a proposition. This reinforces the idea that modality does not disqualify a proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ : ذَهَبَ قَوْمٌ إِلَى أَنَّ الْمُمْكِنَةَ الْعَامَّةَ لَيْسَتْ قَضِيَّةٌ بِالْفِعْلِ ؛ لِعَدَم اشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى الحُكْم ، فَلَيْسَتْ مُوَجَهَةٌ ، وَذَلِكَ خَطَاً ، أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ الْإِمْكَانَ كَيْفِيَّةُ النِّسْبَةِ، وَأَصْلُ النِّسْبَةِ النُّبُوتُ. نَعَمْ، ذَلِكَ أَضْعَفُ الْمَدَارِحِ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالُوا: الْوُجُوبُ وَالاِمْتِنَاعُ دَالَةٌ عَلَى وَثَاقَةِ الرَّابِطِ، وَالْإِمْكَانُ عَلَى ضُعْفِهَا، فَالنُّبُوتُ بِطَرِيقِ الْإِمْكَانِ نَحْو مِنَ الثَّبوتِ مُطلَقا . غَايَةُ الْأَمْرِ: الْمُتَبَادِرُ مِنْهُ عِنْدَ الْإِطْلَاقِ هُوَ الْوُقُوعُ عَلَى نَسْجِ الْفِعْلِيَّةِ، وَذَلِكَ لَا يَضُرُّ فِي عُمُومِهِ، كَمَا قَالُوا فِي الْوُجُودِ، وَإِذَا كَانَتِ الْمُمْكِنَةُ مُوَجَّهَةٌ فَالْمُطْلَقَةُ بِالطَّرِيقِ الْأَوْلى.
The fourth: Some people held that the universal possible is not an actual proposition; because it does not contain a judgment, so it is not modal. And that is a mistake. Do you not see that possibility is a quality of the relation, and the foundation of the relation is affirmation. Yes, that is the weakest of degrees. And therefore they said: necessity and impossibility indicate the strength of the connection, and possibility indicates its weakness, so affirmation through the mode of possibility is a kind of absolute affirmation. The utmost: what is understood from it when absolute is occurrence in the manner of actuality, and that does not harm its generality, as they said about existence. And if the possible is modal, then the absolute is all the more so.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage refutes a claim that the universal possible (al-mumkina al-'amma) is not a genuine proposition because it lacks a judgment. The author argues that possibility is a quality (kayfiyya) of the relation (nisba), and the relation itself is based on affirmation (thubut). So a possible proposition does contain a judgment. It is acknowledged that possibility indicates the weakest degree of affirmation compared to necessity and impossibility. The passage further explains that when we say 'possible', the immediate understanding is occurrence in actuality, but this does not affect its generality; similarly for existence. Therefore, if the possible is considered a modal proposition, the absolute (non-modal) proposition is even more clearly a proposition.

Key Terms
الْمُمْكِنَةُ الْعَامَّةُ
كَيْفِيَّةُ النِّسْبَةِ
النُّبُوتُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the universal possible a genuine modal proposition?
A) Because it contains a strong connection between subject and predicate
B) Because possibility is a quality of the relation, and the relation includes affirmation
C) Because it is about actual occurrences
D) Because it is a universal statement

Key Takeaway
Possibility is a genuine modality, so propositions expressing possibility are full modal propositions with a judgment, albeit a weak one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'The resurrection is possible' (البعث ممكن) from Islamic eschatology. Show why it qualifies as a genuine modal proposition according to the criteria in this passage.
?
Find a proposition in Usul al-Fiqh that expresses a possible ruling (e.g., 'It is permissible to use analogical reasoning in some cases'). Explain how its possibility modality makes it a proposition with a judgment.

Distinguishing Non-Perpetual and Non-Necessary Propositions
Explains that 'non-perpetual' indicates a general absolute, and 'non-necessary' indicates a universal possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposition is a general absolute or a universal possible based on the qualifiers 'non-perpetual' and 'non-necessary'.

Core Idea
Non-perpetual = general absolute; non-necessary = universal possible.

This passage clarifies two important modal terms in propositional logic. When you see 'lā dawām' (non-perpetual), it points to a general absolute proposition—one that is not perpetual but not necessarily possible. 'Lā ḍarūrah' (non-necessary) points to a universal possible proposition—one whose necessity is negated. Understanding this mapping is key to correctly classifying propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qualifier
Look for the terms 'لادوام' (lā dawām) or 'لا ضرورة' (lā ḍarūrah) in the proposition.
These terms are often used as modal qualifiers.
2
Map to proposition type
If 'لادوام', it indicates a general absolute (مطلقة عامة). If 'لا ضرورة', it indicates a universal possible (ممكنة عامة).
The passage explicitly states this correspondence.
3
Check quality and quantity
Both agree in quantity (universal) but differ in quality: non-perpetual negates perpetuity, non-necessary negates necessity.
This explains their different modal forces.
4
Apply to compound propositions
If the proposition is compound, its unity or multiplicity depends on whether the judgment is one or multiple. Multiplicity arises from difference in quality, subject, or predicate.
This is an additional point from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْخَامِسُ : اللَّادَوَامُ إِشَارَةٌ إِلى مُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ ، وَاللَّا ضَرُورَةُ إِلَى مُمْكِنَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُخَالِفَتَى الْكَيْفِيَّةِ وَمُوَافِقَتَيْ الْكَمِّيَّةِ لِمَا قُيِّدِ بِهِمَا ، لأَنَّهُمَا رَافِعَانِ لِلنِّسْبَةِ، مِنْ غَيْرِ تَفَاوُتٍ ، فَالْمُرَكَّبَةُ قَضِيَّةٌ مُتَعَدِّدَةٌ ; لِأَنَّ الْعِبْرَةَ في وَحْدَتِهَا وَتَعَدُّدُهَا بِوَحْدَةِ الْحُكْمِ وَتَعَدُّدِهِ ، وَتَعَدُّدُهُ إِمَّا بِاخْتِلَافِهِ كَيْفًا أَوْ مَوْضُوعاً أَوْ مَحْمُولًا، لَا رَابِعَ لَهَا .
The fifth: Non-perpetual is an indication of a general absolute, and non-necessary is an indication of a universal possible, differing in quality and agreeing in quantity with what is qualified by them, because they both negate the relation, without differentiation. So the compound is a multiple proposition; because the criterion for its unity and multiplicity is the unity and multiplicity of the judgment, and its multiplicity is either by difference in quality, or subject, or predicate, no fourth.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage explains that the term 'non-perpetual' (lā dawām) refers to a general absolute proposition (i.e., a proposition that is not perpetual, but not necessarily possible), while 'non-necessary' (lā ḍarūrah) refers to a universal possible proposition. Both negate the relation (affirmation or negation) but differ in quality: non-perpetual negates perpetuity, non-necessary negates necessity. They agree in quantity (universal or particular). The passage then discusses the composition of propositions: a compound proposition is multiple if it involves more than one judgment; the multiplicity arises from difference in quality, subject, or predicate – no other source.

Key Terms
اللَّادَوَامُ
اللَّا ضَرُورَةُ
الْمُرَكَّبَةُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'non-perpetual' (lā dawām) indicate?
A) A universal possible
B) A general absolute
C) A necessary negative
D) A disjunctive proposition

Key Takeaway
Non-perpetual always maps to a general absolute proposition, and non-necessary always maps to a universal possible proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'The world is non-perpetual' (لادوام), classify it as general absolute or universal possible. Explain your reasoning using the passage.
?
Consider the statement 'Humans are not necessarily rational' (لا ضرورة). Is it a general absolute or universal possible? What does that imply for its modal force in a syllogism?
